The Simile.
IN' the first place, a simile is
not a metaphor. A simile
is merely a reference : you
take two things, .set them
side by side, and compare them
“ her eyes are like stars. ” But
a metaphor is a complete trans-
ference : you merge one thing
wholly in the other —“ her eyes
arc stars.” So that the metaphor
extends the simile’s method, and
instead of shivering on the brink
plunges boldly in. It would
be hard to say which is . the
more valuable literary figure ;
but while both are among the
earliest acquisitions of language,
the simile, is a little the earlier,
a little the simpler, a little less
fanciful and recondite.
Not the tear of God, but the comparison of ob
jects and ideas, is the beginning of wisdom. From
the moment birth opens our eyes we are classi
tying and dividing perceptions into likes and op
posites—such as light, darkness; sound, silence
land, water; and so on., Our own understanding
comes by comparing one thing to or front another
and to convey our ideas into another’s under-
standing there is no better way than the way of
the simile. . Often statement puts in position
the nail which simile hammers home. “They
sank as lead in the mighty waters ” is far more
vivid than “ They sank in the mighty waters/’
That simile relies upon the common knowledge
that lead sinks quickly; and, to be effective, a
simile, should recall an idea familiar to the
simile’s audience. Platinum sinks more quickly
than lead ; and to a person thoroughly acquainted
with the properties of lead and platinum “They
sank like platinum ” would convey a stronger im-
pression. But not to the Israelites.
Children, recapitulating the human record, are
constantly employing similes to express their
meanings: everything is. “like” this or that.
Ihe literatures of primitive races, the children of
humanity, blossom in similes as naturally as
water . rl Pple.-s ipto waves when a wind comes.
And in primitive similes you may read the
primitive life. The early poets teem with
r u- gold and silver, sword and
shield, the Sun and moon and stars, all the
objects most known and thought about in daily
life. Prom each was abstracted the idea of its
leading quality, for use in comparison. The
sword was sharp ; the sun hot, fierce, ruddy;
the IhUs high ; the valleys green ; and so on.
. other times come other manners and
similes ; and urban church-goers find little force in
the idea of “mountains skipping like rams”
lation WaS S ° effective an image to a pastoral popu-
With the search for the simile for its own sake,
or for the sake of decoration, came sin into the
literary world. The, epic is a long, slow-moving
composition ; and probably in Homer’s day minds
also moved more slowly. If so, two things should
have resulted : that an idea took longer to pene-
trate, and that it was held more tenaciously when
it had penetrated. The long and ornamental
simile of Homer might then have been none
too long for his climax, since if it had been
shorter the climax would have found his
audience unprepared—just as you wasted words
on bwift s Laputan who had not been called to
attention with a flapper. If Homer, in the eighth
book of the Iliad, had merely said that the
Greek watch-fires encompassed Troy as the stars
compass the moon on a clear night, and proceeded
to talk about other thing's, very likely his full
meaning would not have soaked down to the
Grecian brain. When he took half-a-dozen lines
to say that—
As in the heights of heaven the moon gleams clear, and
around her
Shine in their beauty the stars, nor is one cloud moving
in ether;
Shines forth every cliff, and the jutting peaks of the
headlands,
Forest and glen: then, as opens the rifting firmament
heavenwards,
Star is revealed upon star ; and gay is the heart of the
herdsman :
then, presumably, he had gained sufficient momen-
tum to add that—
Not in le-ss number than they, from the Xanthus’ stream
to the sea sands,
Glimmered the red watchfires that encompassed Uion
alway.
But for Virgil’s audience it is doubtful if the
Homeric simile was not a mistake. Many of
Milton’s long similes are certainly mistakes; and
when Matthew Arnold, in “Balder Dead,” writes
of Hermod returning from Hell
and shall X tell thee how he seems?
liike as a farmer, who hath lost his dog,
Some morn, at market, in a crowded town—•
Through many streets the poor beast runs in vain,
And follows this man after that, for hours ;
And, late at evening, spent and panting, falls
Before a stranger’s threshold, not his home,
With flanks a-tremble, and his slender tongue
Hangs quivering out between his dust-smear’d jaws,
And piteously he eyes the passers by ;
But home his master comes to his own farm,
Far in the country, wondering where he is—
So Hermod comes to-day unfollow’d home—
he certainly sacrifices his poem to classical tradi-
tmn. Very likely that is the way primitive man
would have talked, and the way in which primi-
tive man would have understood his god talking •
but the verisimilitude will not justify Arnold’
He is writing for the modern mind; and lie has
succeeded, when telling about Hermod, in in-
teresting the modern mind less in Hermod than in
a strange lost dog. While we are admiring his
dog his poem is waiting.
. A simile is a detail, and must always be subor-
dinated to the main theme. As soon as it hinders
the theme more than it helps, it becomes obnoxi-
ous. There is, indeed, a kind of writing which is
avowedly nothing but a collection of beautiful
images—ryou may linger over one and pass to the
next while the meaning flows in an under-current •
but in the best writing the thought distinctly
leads. I his fragment of John Fletcher’s is thickly
strown with similes; but each has its office and
bears its burden ; and the reader is delayed at
each step only as long as is necessary to receive
the idea.
Care-charming Sleep, thou easer of all woes,
Brother to Death, sweetly thyself dispose
On this afflicted prince ; fall like a cloud,
In gentle showers ; give nothing that is loud,
Or painful to his slumbers ; easy, light,
And as a purling stream, thou son of Night
Pass by his troubled senses ; sing his pain,
Like hollow murmuring wind or silver rain ;
Into this prince gently, oh, gently slide,
And kiss him into slumbers like a bride.
A refinement on the idea that the simile should
help the theme is the practice of making the simile
suit the subject. A simile dragged in neck-and-
heels from an alien field, no matter how cleverly,
is a bad simile. When Charles Kingsley, in
“Yeast,” writes quite seriously of his heroine
Argeinone that her “eyes shone out like twain
lakes of still azure, beneath a broad marble cliff of
polished forehead,” he would hardly have written
worse if he had written “marble mantelpiece.”
Hut J. R. Gieen is rhetorically effective when he
says that “ the diplomatic contest between Philip
and Elizabeth was like the fight which England
was soon to see between the ponderous Spanish
galleon and the light pinnace of the buccaneers.”
When the plan is carried to extremes, however,
it is carried too far. The Belgian writer, Camille
Lemonnier, has a story of a fisher-maiden whose
cheeks were red as salmon ; teeth as bright as a
wet oar s blade in the sun ; and the coils of her
hair as heavy as towing-ropes. These might be
natural similes for the maiden’s lovers ; but they
are accentuated a little too much for Lemonnier’s
readers.
Properly used, the simile is one of the best
weapons in the writer’s armoury. The new theory
that as far as possible it should be absorbed in the
word—the antipodes of Homer’s method—will
doubtfully ever find general acceptance even with
the cultured. Just how far ornament should go is
a question of taste ; but to deprive style entirely
of the ornament of the simile would surely be un-
wise.
A considerable number of similes (mixed with
metaphors) have been forwarded in response to the
request for (1) the most poetical, and (2) the most
curious and piquant simile. Some famous ex-
amples went unquoted. Nobody thought of Sheri-
dan s Jew for six weeks turned Christian—
He stands like a dead wall between church and syna-
gogue, or like the blank leaves between the Old and New
Testament.
Priest-ridden Henri Trois might have been in-
stanced, who said that the presence of a friar ex-
erted as agreeable an effect upon his mind as the
most delicate and gentle tickling upon his body.
Justin M ‘Carthy was bright when, in speaking of
public executions, he said that the soul took its
flight as if it were a trapeze-performer in a circus.
But there is no end to quotation.
ihe following is judged the most poetical simile
contributed ; and to the sender, Mr. Hubert
Church, Wellington, M.L., 10s. 6d. has been
posted :
Her face is like the milky way i' the sky,
A meeting of gentle lights without a name.
Sir John Suckling.
The following is judged the most curious and
piquant simile contributed. Will the sender, Mr.
Edward Tregear, M.L., please forward address ?
A bushman once said to me of a dull mate : “ Dull? I
should think so. He was like a dead policeman in a wet
mackintosh.”
Some others—mixed. From S.F.—
The rich lights fade out one by one;
And like a peony
Drowning in wine, the crimson sun
Sinks down in that strange sea.
F. J. Daley.
H.J.A. quotes from Shakespeare’s “Rape of
Lucrece ”
Her eyes, like marigolds, had sheathed their light,
And canopied in darkness sweetly lay
Till they might open to adorn the day.
“Ben D.” prefers this from the same poem—
Without the bed her other fair hand was,
On the green coverlet, whose perfect white
Showed like an April daisy on the grass,
With pearly sweat resembling dew of night.
(It has been noted that the Stratford meadows of
his youth give Shakespeare some of his most
charming images.) A.R.B. is deluded by Long-
fellow’s Arabs’ tents—a shining example of what
to avoid. H.J.A. quotes Victor Daley’s girl’s
funeral—
And then the dark procession wound along,
Like a black serpent with a snow-white bird
Held in its fangs.
Which is a little too intellectually ingenious for
poetry ; though undoubtedly a striking simile.
H.C. offers Corporal Trim’s quaint simile from
“ Tristram Shandy
“Nothing,” continued the corporal, “can be so sad as
confinement for life, or so sweet, an’ please your Honour,
as liberty.”
“ Nothing, true,” said my uncle Toby, musing.
“ When a man is free,” cried the Corporal, giving a
flourish with his stick thus :
A th x Ban 1 of my father’s most subtle syllogisms could
not have said more for celibacy,
11. M. writes :
D. G. Rossetti and Browning are writers who have a
special faculty for simile - expression. Unlike most
authors, they use the simile often, not as the result of
the exigencies of description, but as the poetic embodi-
ment of a thought necessitating a descriptive situation
for its environment. The simile which suggests a situa-
tion is generally more daring than that which is sug-
gested by the situation. Thus Rossetti in his "Match
with the Moon ’’
Weary already, weary miles to-night
I walked for bed : and so, to get some ease,
I dogged the flying moon with similes.
And like a wisp she doubled on my sight
In pends; and caught in tree-tops like a kite;
And in a globe of film all liquorish
Swam full-faced like a silly silver fish
Last like a bubble shot the welkin’s height
Where the road turned, and got behind me, and sent
My wizened shadow craning round at me,
And jeered, “So stop theineasiue—one, two,-three.’’ —
And if I faced on her, looked innocent.
But just at parting, half-way down a dell,
She kissed me for good-night. So you’ll not tell.
Browning’s simile is rather polished poetic conception
than inspiration. From Pauline —
. . . the blackthorn boughs
In the sunshine were white with coming buds,
Like the bright side of a sorrow.
From “ Pippa Passes” —
6th Student. Look at the bridegroom. Half his hair
in storm and half in calm,-patted down over the left
temple,—like a frothy cup one blows on to cool it.

16. —“ (Enone.”
My soul is an enchanted boat

He had a face like a benediction.
Lon Quixote.
Trailing like a wounded duck, working out her soul.

Watched the compass chase its tail like a cat at play.
Kipling--" Ballad of the Bolivar."
You could no more keep him to his word than you
could hold an eel in a bucket of slush.
A married laborer, kept awake half the night by a
crydng child, to his wife : “Jenny, I’d rather sleep with
a bag full of devils than that kid.”
An artillery-sergeant to a dirty recruit: «Do you think
you can come into this orderly-room spitting like a dam
Maxim ?”
A miserable-looking woman speaking of her husband :
“Sir, he was meaner than three-haporth of ‘God-help-
us’ chucked out of a hospital window.”
Drunken stockman rebuked by a bush-preacher in a
baggy suit of black : “Do you think I’ll be jawed by a
chap with trousers on him like the belly of a concertina?”
The proverbs of savage people are full of most admirable
similes. For instance, the Fijians, who, like the Maoris,
call their parrot kaka because of its cry ( kah-kah ), say of
a boaster—“ He is like a parrot, always calling out his
own name.”

. Italian novelist D’Annunzio recently holidayed
in Egypt with fourteen trunks and a valet. This
was his travelling wardrobe :
Shirts

embellishments) 48

Ditto of quiet tinted silk 2 dozen
Hats, evening suits, smoking coats,
shooting jackets innumerable.
Gloves for walking 48 pairs
Ditto for evening 24 pairs
Mufflers of beautiful silk 3
Walking sticks ... 12
Umbrellas of violet hue ... 8
Parasols, green .... 10
Handkerchiefs 20 dozen
Cravats, resplendent and varied ...150
Waistcoats 10
Shoes for walking 14 pairs
Shoes for crocodile hunting 2 pairs
Slippers, “soft, silent and tremulous" 2 pairs
The Australian backblocks storyteller, writing in
his shirt while his tenderly-patched moleskins are
drying, is invited to hold this dose to his slusn
lamp and consider how many “mufflers of beauti-
ful silk” he will wear when he cometh into his
kingdom.
Stead in St. Petersburg ! —lately stigmatised
the Russian censorship as imbecile, idiotic, and
mischievous—-for Russia, for the Emperor, and
for peace. By all accounts, nobody in Russia ever
before told the Censor so plainly what he thought
of him—“such uncompromising public denuncia-
tion of official action is absolutely unprecedented,
and is regarded as a grave event.” Stead has the
courage of his fanaticism, anyway.
Conan Doyle has been saying unkind things
about that ardent British log-roller, Rev. Robert-
son Nieoll, whose defence is that his strange
enthusiasms were in no case exchanged for value
received. No one supposes that the editor of the
Bookman and the British Weekly received real
sovereigns for his remarkable labours. But, if he
is as honest as he swears, and gives to him that
asketh hoping nothing in return, his incapacity is
past all whooping.
Good England’s wrath against the Sunday Daily
Mail had some justifying logic. Another of the
Harmsworth publications, the Sunday Companion,
declares it exists as eternal foe of “ the Sunday
newspaper and the hosts of impurity.”
The 1 Memoirs of Sergeant Bourgogne ” —a
private soldier’s record of the retreat from Moscow
‘ Are not apt for quotation. Pew passages are
prominent. It is from the steady dribble of the
small details of defeat and suffering that one gets
full vision of that evil flight. The tragedy is piled
line upon line, little upon little. And through all
the horror of death from starvation, death in
battle, death by cold, shines his soldiers’invincible
adoration of Napoleon—the man who for millions
of subjects put off the raiment of humanity
and became as a god, above all good and evil. No-
body now knows how Napoleon looked in his
THE MAN OF MOSCOW.
Drawn by F. Vallotten.
prime ; but of the innumerable portraits I doubt
if one better realises the Idea of the hero than the
sketch upon this page by Vallotton, who never
saw him.

A correspondent wants the Govt, subsidy to
Sydney School of Arts stopped, and the money
devoted to _ the Public Library. It is not a new
idea: but it is as good as ever. With one pro-
perly-maintained free lending library in the Market
building, there is no necessity to subsidise another
a few hundred yards away—which daunts the im-
pecunious with 6s. per quarter subscription. If
the Govt, maintains the Eree Library, people who
want to pay can patronise the booksellers. Sydney
Mechanics’ School of Arts has done good work in
its day; but between the Technical College and
the Public Library, its day as a subsidised institu-
tion has passed.

What his publishers call the “ cheap edition ” of
Buskin is still dear-enough; and a New Yorker
not long ago sent to an English friend a pirated
American set at £3 or so. The Customs seized jt;
and the buyer wrote to Ruskin asking for relief.
The reply—
Sir : I do not see that your friend’s desire to give you a
present at my expense is any apology for your intrusion
upon me. —Yours, etc., John Ruskin.
But—consolation —the autograph is valued at
£3 3s.

Q. Govt, has accumulated 14,000 books and
6000 pamphlets for the new Public Library ; but
no building is yet available.
THE BOOKFELLOW.
I read in Sydney Sunday Times of 18th June
some verses signed “ Uncle Billy,” commencing—
and so on, with sentiment similar to that of the
Times bard. I presume that if Byron had seen
this ghostly imitation on the battlements of
futurity he would have remarked, “0 my pro-
phetic soul, my Uncle Billy ! ”
DON J.
It was not to gaze on the waterfall
That Marion left her father’s hall :
It was riot to pluck the opening flower
She made her way to her favorite bower :
Nor to feel the breath of the Summer breeze-
As it swept- so gently through the trees.
Why was that blush on her maiden cheek?
What—ah, what—had she come to seek?
As long ago as 1816 Byron wrote—
It. was not to gaze on the waterfall
That Parisina left the hall . . .
And if she sits in Este’s bower
’Tis not for the sake of its full-blown flower —

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was a pleasant little diversion at a
recent meeting of South Dunedin (M.L.) Borough
Council, when the Mayor arose and blandly in-
formed the gaping councillors that it was his
intention to strike two of their number off the
burgess-roll for non-payment of rates. One of the
gentlemen referred to reared up and said that he
had been hardly dealt with ; had he been notified
that such extreme action was contemplated he
would have paid the beastly rates. And the rest
of this awful council mostly applauded and snorted
wildly about the Mayor’s tyranny. But the
Mayor was equal to the opportunity, and stood
up and told them many truths about the class of
fraud that had the chilled-steel cheek to come
there with their own rates in arrear and instruct
the clerk to prosecute ratepayers who were behind
in payment. At present it seems as though it
would be a good plan on the part of South
Dunedin to poll-axe its council, and declare the
Mayor civic dictator.
On a Mountain Top.
A season here on the hills together,
A walk and a draught of native wine—
A quiet pipe in the amber weather,
While far, down far in the gulf divine
White smoke, adrift, like a wind-blown feather,
Up-spires and curls from the green benign.
Strong suns that wheel through purer azure,
White burnished moons of silver fire,
All stars alight with larger measure
Shine in this keener air and higher,
Wherein, like gods (of clay), our pleasure
Is thus to walk and talk and tire.
The still.night, long, in deep abysses,
Moon-white, star-pierced, the mists arise
To cover deeply all long green tresses,
As when sleep rolls over weary eyes,
And, careless of blows or coin or kisses,
Gold head or grey in quiet lies.
Like dear love-dreams in the dark thereunder,
Where the silent drooping trees abide ;
By stern old cliffs that stand and ponder
And list, forlorn, for a vanished tide,
The blue-white fire-flies wink and wander—
The lost streams murmur a sweet aside.
Awake ! The Slayer of silver trances
Is storming up the eastern steep !
Arise ! for the Morning darts his lances
Of light on the huddled mists of Sleep ;
The strange rich forest awakes and dances,
The fountains sing aloud in the deep.
The river on to the ocean races
To carry the secret news of Night ;
Farewell to all mystical, far, green places,
All lonely valleys of dear delight.
The City is full of passionate faces,
Is loud with the cry of a world-ol I fight.
Sydney, June, 1899.
V. E. QUINN.
Bars of Gold.
Gone are the days when Freedom flung
Her battle-cry to earth and sea;
Lost are the lays, the songs unsung
Which breathed of brotherhood to be ;
Upon the nations night is falling,
Darkness that veils, until the Dawn,
The serried ranks, in mass appalling,
Of Mammon’s gilded spawn.
Spurning the nineteen-hundredth year,
That beams from our resplendent life,
Now does their hateful front appear
And mock the Judgment’s harvest-knife;
To foster evil in its springing
Glibly the tales of peace are told,
Whilst men their bloodless hands are wringing
Beside the Bars of Gold.
Shattered are hopes of measured right
That filled the fond enthusiast’s scheme.
Folly asserts her subtle might,
And open truth is held a dream,
As Bower, a cerecloth darkly weaving,
Guards, closely hid, the maxims cold
Which frame her bondage, pa.st retrieving,
Within the Bars of Gold.
Banish in time the living Pride ;
Destroy the servile ties of rank ;
Facing the crystal throne, decide
To drink the faith our fathers drank;
Serve none for hope of soft advancement,
Fearlessly live by love controlled ;
So shall we break that deep entrancement
Behind the Bars of Gold.
Swift must depart the honeyed lie,
The studied fraud, the broken trust;
Fortune will not in scorn pass by,
Nor Victory’s sword dishonored rust;
The parent’s breast no more be riven ;
Shame may not claim a daughter sold ;
Nor woman’s heart to lust be driven,
Eucaged by Bars of Gold.
Then, at the ruddy harvest-tide,
Not wealth alone shall garner grain ;
Nor may her hireling crew decide
That guilty hands clasp holy gain.
Her robber hordes, in sloth engendered,
Mighty thre i wrong, revered of old,
In Hell beneath shall sink surrendered
Within the streams of Gold.
W. GRIFFITH.

IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Payette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and fife
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his. son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring ’rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fioht. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew, no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Doodle, was heard as never
was heard before or since.

SATURDAY, JULY 15, 1899.
The Transvaal.
Obviously the Transvaal is doomed. It is
doomed if it “reforms ” its electoral system
by giving full suffrage to the swarms of
British and other alien exploiters who have
poured into the country uninvited, and
despite systematic discouragement ; for if it
does so, the Boers abdicate their authority,
and hand over the whole control of their
native land to the new majority. The men
who were born in the Vaal country, who
snatched it from the wilderness, who stole
it against stupendous odds, who grew up
in it, and tilled it, and humped the White
Man’s Burden through it for the best part
of a century, have been suddenly out-
numbered by the eager, cosmopolitan, hook-
nosed hustlers and boodlers and first robbers
and wild-catters and financial hawks of
Johannesburg, and if the latter are granted
complete citizen rights they will own the.
Legislature. Then they will either run it
themselves, or hand it over to the British
Government, or farm it out to the Char-
tered Co. This is what will obviously
happen if the Boers grant full suffrage to the
influx of the new locusts. And if they don’t
grant it they will almost certainly be wiped
out another way, by the arms of Britain,
which will some day annex the Transvaal by
way of avenging the “wrongs” of the un-
welcome British intruders. So Kruger and
his burghers, if all reports are true,
half incline to take the wiser course, and
concede nothing and go down fighting. If
they give the suffrage to the new ma-
jority—the strangers who have rushed their
country —the latter will legally rule the
Legislature and the Government, and will
legally barter away the country, and if the
Boers resist they will be perfectly unlawful
rebels, liable to execution when captured.
And if they yield nothing the Boers will
make their last fight as at least semi-lawful
combatants, patriotically defending their
native land.
* * * *
By mutual consent the rights of the
African natives have been dropped out of
the question. They always are when the
white man takes to humping the White
Man’s Burden in the black man’s land. It
is a question between the 70,000 Boers and
the 100,000 or 200,000 Uitlanders (the esti-
mates of the latter vary to a remarkable
degree); the 600,000 niggers are not con-
sidered on either side. The history of the
Boers is short and eventful. In the time of
the Napoleonic wars, when Holland was
trodden down by the emperor’s armies,
Britain occupied the Dutch colony at the
Cape of Good Hope on behalf of Holland,
its friend and ally, to keep it from falling
into French hands. Then it kept the Cape
for itself. There was some little trouble, and
the principal prisoners taken were hanged
up till they were dead at Slaughter Nek.
Then the bolder spirits among them
trekked ” far out into the wilderness, and
founded the South African Republic and
the Orange Free State. They fought desper-
ate battles ere they made good their footing ;
the memorable Sunday when Pretorius and
460 farmers shattered a Zulu force 12,000
strong is only one of the many great days in
Boer history. And the British persistently
followed them up. They snatched Natal
from the Boers in the early days of the re-
public. The Chartered Co. grabbed the land
to the north of the Transvaal, and shut the
burghers in on that side. The Imperial
Government closed down on their attempts
to purchase the Portuguese seaport of
Lorenzo Marquez, and insisted that the Boer
territory must be for ever an inland state,
cut off from commerce except with the adja-
cent British territory. Britain made two
serious attempts to annex the country ; the
Chartered Co. made a third. In each case
the Boers treated their prisoners at least a
great deal more humanely than Britain
treated the Dutch at Slaughter Nek, and the
Boers got only insult in return for their
humanity. And, though Britain by solemn
treaty recognised the independence of both
Boer republics, yet when the Diamond Fields
were discovered in the Orange Free State
they were promptly seized by British forces,
on the ground that they were on territory
rightly belonging to an obscure Kaffir chief.
Then Britain admitted that it had made a
mistake and its Kaffir had no real claim after
all; whereupon Britain kept the rich Dia-
mond Fields even to this day.
* * * *
With all these experiences the Boers of
the Transvaal know well that to give the
full suffrage to the swarm of unwelcome
Outlanders —in other words, to hand over
their country and their authority to the
strangers of Jolmnnesburg—is only another
name for annexation. The Boer policy is
strictly legal according to all recognised ideas
of international law. Every country, it is
admitted, has a right to make its own laws
regarding the admission of foreigners to
citizenship, or to refuse the admission of
foreigners altogether. The fact that the
Outlanders pay a very large share of taxation
is only the natural corollary of the other
fact that they own by far the largest part
of the wealth—which wealth they have
made wholly out of the Boer country. In
the other matter, that they are taxed with-
out having a voice in the expenditure of the
money, and that there should be “no taxa-
tion without representation,” the Boers
simply adhere to the policy of Britain,
France, Germany, the U.S., Australia, and
every other civilised country. In every
country the alien pays the same taxes as the
born or naturalised citizen ; he has no repre-
sentation ; and he has no right to claim
naturalisation except what right the law may
give him, if it gives him any at all. The
decision of some Australian provinces to
issue no more naturalisation papers to
Chinese, yet to make them pay the same
taxation as ordinary citizens, and to further
aggravate the case of “taxation without
representation ” by levying extra duties on
them—duties from which the man with
representation is exempt—is distinctly a case
in point.
* * * *
The Boer law of naturalisation is not so
very remarkable after all. Any alien may
be naturalised and get a vote for the Lower
House after two years’ residence and pay-
ment of £2. He may get a vote for the
Upper House 12 years later—or again he
may not. In Great Britain the alien gets a
vote for the Lower House after Jive years’
residence and payment of £5, and he never
by any chance gets a vote for the Upper
House—even 400 years’ residence wouldn’t
bring that any nearer. If English law ap-
plied to the Transvaal there would probably
be notmany more voters in Johannesburg than
there are now, for the hawks and hustlers of
the Rand are a transitory crowd, and only a
small proportion of them could qualify for
naturalisation according to the hard British
law. Johannesburg and its immediate sur-
roundings have only four members, which
looks ridiculously few according to popula-
tion, yet according to the number of voters
it isn’t seriously unjust. The alleged wrong
which the Boer does to the foreigner doesn’t
even begin to compare with the wrong done
by the Westralian to his own countryman on
the W.A. goldfields, where Kalgooriie has
one member for 5674 voters, while Ashburton
has one for 42; or with cases in Great
Britain, where Newry has a member
for 1850 voters and Walthamstow one
for 19,845. And if British law was applied
all round to the Transvaal, Johannesburg
while having probably few more voters
than it has now, and no more representation,
would still pay the bulk of the taxation.
British law covers such imposts as municipal
rates that run as high as 65., 75., and in at
least one case over 95., in the £, also
customs duties, duties on the admission of
barristers, annual license on attorneys, soli-
citors, proctors, and law-clerks, on alkali
works, on agreements, affidavits, valuations,
articles of clerkships, indentures, armorial
bearings, arbitration awards, bank-notes,
bankers’ licences, cheques, receipts, beer,
bonds, cards, carriages, motor-cars, com-
panies’ capital, contracts, share transfers,
conveyance of property, debentures, declara-
tions, churches, drunkards’ retreats (even
the drunkards’ retreat is taxed in good old
Hingland), mortgages, probates, inventories,
gun licences, house-agents’ licences, house-
tax, income-tax, insurance-policy tax, land-
tax, lease-tax, tax on allotment of shares,
on man - servants, patents, dealers in
plate, gold-refiners, bonds, property-tax,
income-tax, railway-passenger tax, tax
on restaurants, share - warrants, voting
warrants, and God only knows what
besides. And if that were applied to
Johannesburg, the chances are that Johan-
nesburg would pay quite as heavily as
it does now. And the simple-living Boer,
who deals in very few of these taxable
luxuries, would be just about where he is
now.
♦ * * *
Britain has about 40,000,000 people. And
if Britain were rushed by 60,000,000 Germans
whom it hadn’t been strong enough to keep
out in the first place, and these Germans
demanded that the British law should be
abrogated so as to give them the franchise—
so as to enable them to take over the whole
government of the country ; and Britain
was firmly convinced that they would use
their power to enact that Britain be at once
annexed and governed by Kaiser Bill and
garrisoned by his spike-helmeted myrmi-
dons—well, Britain would also die in the
last ditch before it gave way. And it
would also be nasty and aggravated
and quarrelsome. And it would also
tax these aliens without representation, as it
always has done to the alien within its
gates. The Boer probably isn’t a nice
neighbor. He is ignorant—the British rule
drove him out into the wilderness so long
ago that he has become a being of the wilder-
ness, and quite as dense as the British agri-
cultural laborer was 40 years ago. He has
been cut off from communication with other
races so long—partly through the British
policy in driving him back from the coast—
that he is absurdly arrogant. He is proud
—the handful of men who licked the British
empire in three battles must either be proud
or have a very low opinion of the British
empire. And the Briton hates him and
maligns him just as the Briton does every-
body who has licked him—all nations are
built that way. The Briton is a moderately
generous, supercilious, condescending enemy
to the man that he has licked—but that is a
different story. And the Briton doesn’t see
why the sort of law that he serves out to
aliens should be served out to him, or why
his policy of taxing wealth should be adopted
by anybody save himself. The Boer law re-
garding the naturalisation and representation
of aliens is more liberal than that of Eng-
land, and the Boer system of taxation upon
wealth is not really more aggressive
than the British variety—yet the Briton
who talks about Boer oppression sees nothing
ridiculous in his loud declaration that Britain
is the freest country in the world. The
strangers in the Transvaal came there—a
very large proportion of them—as a band
of impecunious wanderers, swagmen, toe-
raggers and dead-beats, and the Trans-
vaal made them rich, and they wear boiled
linen and fare sumptuously every day, and
are not at all grateful. So the Boer republic
has to go down sooner or later, whether it
gives concessions or whether it doesn’t. And
the Boer—like the heroic, unlettered, proud
and not too clean bigot that he is—may yet
decide to concede nothing and go down
fighting. It is the more dignified end of the
two and after all it isn’t quite such certain
death as concession would be.
The Brisbane “Telegraph’s” Nine Fish-
Yarns.
The Brisbane Telegraph, which is running
the anti-Federal cause in Queensland with very
little brain, but with a vast amount of dull
malignity, is funny reading these times. Some
of its recent appeals to the people are as
follows :
The Convention Bill provides as under :
1. Six State-paid Parliaments and a Federal-paid Par-
liament—altogether 680 paid politicians (more members
than in the British Parliament, which is unpaid). Six
State-paid Governors and a paid Governor-General. All
for 4,000,000 people.
2. A paid Interstate Commission to manage railways
built and paid for to develop our territory and trade, yet
Victoria and South Australia are to have the prepon-
derating influence in appointing the commission. No
appeal from this commission. We cannot control or
dismiss it.
3. A Federal Senate of six members for each State-
elected by their respective States votingas one electorate.
None but rich men can afford to canvass a whole State.
4. Additional taxation of £1 upon evei*3 r man, woman
and child, this is £6 per family of six, three weeks’ wages
to a working man. The provisions of the Bill necessitate
it.
Who gets the money? For five years the State
Treasurer, after five years the weaker colonies. You
pay it.
the billites say they cannot tell the cost. Financial
experts say they can, and they are all against the bill,
because of this cruel burden of taxation and its unwork-
able financial provisions.
(5) Rivers are to be under the control of the Interstate
Commission, the same as railways, and that commission is
empowered to prevent irrigation.
And what are you t.o get in return for your sacrifice of
money and freedom ? A broad national life—whatever
that may be—under the Southern Gross. Will that feed
and clothe you and your family and pay the rent?
(6) Mr. Reid says he expects to get the Federal Premier-
ship. What does Mr. Barton expect ? The point is, what
do you get?
Think over it carefully, and you will join your local
anti-bill league.
If you do not believe these statements, STUDY THE
BILL.
Kill this bill by voting “No” on September 2.

* * * *
Such a weird, wild, crude collection of lies
should be bottled in spirits and put away care-
fully in the snake department at the museum.
Some of the real facts of the case are these :
Regfarding* Lie No. 1: The Bill does NOT provide for
six State-paid Parliaments and a Federal-paid Parlia-
ment 680 paid politicians.” At present, there are six
State Parliaments, numbering 645 members, of whom 424
are paid. The Convention Bill provides for running the
business of Australia with a Federal Parliament of
about 110 members—just the number that Queensland
employs under Provincialism to run the business of one-
eighth of the Australian people. That doesn't make 680
paid politicians. And the Bill leaves the States full
authority to reduce their State Parliaments to as few
members as they please ; and to reduce the payment of
State legislators, or abolish it altogether. In fact, it only
provides for about 110 paid Federal politicians, and for
nothing else. The rest of the story is a lie.
Further regarding Snake-Yarn No. 1: The Bill doesn’t
provide for “six State-paid Governors and a Governor-
General.” The States may stop paying their State
Governors and go in for the gratis article whenever they
like. And, by the way, 680 politicians are NOT “ more
members than in the British Parliament.” The British
Commons number 670; Lords, about 646; total, 1316.
The Brisbane paper does its little “fake” by reckoning
both Houses in each Australian Parliament, and only one
House in the British Parliament.
J^ e Fish-Story No. 2 : The Interstate Commission will
NOT manage Queensland’s railways, or those of any other
State. Under Clause 102 it is provided that “ The Par-
liament may, by any law with respect to trade or com-
merce, forbid, as to railways, any preference or discrimi-
nation by any State . . . if such preference or discrimi-
nation is undue and unreasonable or unjust to any State.”
And that is absolutely all. The Interstate Commission
has power to prevent N.S.W. charging higher rates on
Queensland goods than on its own in order to damage
Queensland’s trade, or vice versa, and that is the full ex-
tent of its powers. It can’t interfere in the management
or control of any State’s railways unless that State is
trying to injure another State by charging special rates
on its goods.
And, with further relation to Fish-Story No. 2 : Victoria
and S.A. WON’T have a preponderating influence in ap-
pointing the Commission (the miserably ignorant Bris-
bane rag seems to think S.A. is a place with 2,000,000
people), because they haven’t a preponderating popula-
tion or a preponderating vote in Parliament. In a
Federation of six provinces they will have 41 votes
out of 110. Queensland and N.S.W. will have a larger
influence than Victoria and S.A. Also Queensland, Vic-
toria, and Tasmania will have a larger influence than
N.S.W. and S.A. Also N.S.W. and Victoria will have a
larger influence than Tasmania and Kangaroo Island, and
Brisbane will have more influence than Geelong. The
matter mast go by the voice of the majority. What else
is the majority for, does anybody know 1 And “we”
(Queensland) will have as much power to control or dis-
miss the Commission as Queensland’s population and
Parliamentary representation entitle it to—more than
S.A. or Tasmania or W.A., and less than Victoria or
N.S.W.
Re Ananias No. 3 : The Bill does not provide that everv
State shall be one electorate. It says (clause 7) : “ Until
the Parliament of the Commonwealth otherwise pro-
vides, the Parliament of the State of Queensland, if that
State be an Original State, inay make laws dividing the
State into divisions and determining- the number of
Senators for each division.”
Also: In the Federal Convention election each State
was a single electorate, and that Convention was full of
poor men—bootmaker Trenwith, Graham Berry who
lives on a pension, the notoriously far-from-affluent Reid,
Lyne, and Barton ; Dr. Cockburn—in fact, there were
only three really rich men in the whole show. That is
how the one-electorate system works by actual experi-
ence. The tale about how only rich men can represent
large electorates is based on the quite erroneous idea that
a member has to bribe the whole electorate. It is note-
worthy that some of the biggest electorates in N.S W.
are represented by very poor men, mostly Labor members'.
Re Yarn No. 4 : Financial experts are'NOT all against
the Bill. And no sane individual dreams that Federation
will cost £1 per head (13,(500,000) extra per annum.
Even that very ferocious anti-F. deralist and alleged
financial expert, Dr. MacLaurin, of N.S.W., put the sum
down at £750,000 a year—about one-fifth of the above
figure.

Concerning Whale-Anecdote No. 5 : The Interstate
Commission does NOT control rivers. At least, clauses
101 to 104, which deal with it, never once refer to rivers.
The one allusion to rivers is in clause 100, which savs •
“ The Commonwealth shall NOT, by any law or regula-
tion of trade or commerce abridge the right of a State or
of the residents therein to the reasonable use of the
waters of rivers for conservation or irrigation.” And
why should the Federal Parliament want to stop irriga-
tion ? _ Or why should the people want to elect anti-
lrrigationists? It is a much more horrible fact that the
present provincial Parliaments have all full authority
pr £ verlt irrigation.” Yet they don’t stop irrigation.
Mr. Reid didn’t say he expected to get the Federal
Premiership, though he cheerfully admitted that he
would like it. The Bulletin doesn’t know what Mr
Barton expects. What does the Brisbane Telegraph
expect ?

Also :
There is no reason why Queensland shouldn’t hold its
own against-the “products of Victoria,” unless the
Queenslander is a low, degraded, inferior being with-
out the grit or brain or courage of the Melbo’urnite
Queensland is better supplied with coal than Vie-
has 427,000,000 acres against Victoria’s
56,000,000. It has not much over one-third the popu-
lation. Queensland is to Victoria what the US is
to Britain, and with its stupendous area and fewer mouths
to feed, it should be able to pour grain into the small
heavily-populated southern State, and grow rich on the
trade. If little crowded Victoria can dare to open its
markets to Queensland, then Queensland, with so much
greater resources, can open its to Victoria, unless all the
grit has gone out of the Queenslander. The alarm about
Vic. competition in N.S.W. and Queensland appears to
suggest that the Victorian is a god, and the Cornstalk or
Bananalander a cockroach—and a very poor cockroach at
that.
Also, the country with the Biggest Debt on Earth in
proportion to population (which is Queensland) shouldn’t
holler so loudly about the “diseased finances” of its
neighbors. And if its neighbors’ industries are “debili-
tated,” then why should Queensland fear to encounter
them on even terms ? Is Queensland so poor and wretched
that these competitors must be dead as well as dehili-
tated before it can meet them ?
Also the item about Queensland only importing £271 982
worth of goods from its neighbors and £5,735 000 worth
from the rest of the world is pure sham and invention
Queensland imports about £2,250,000 from its neighbors
—the Brisbane Telegraph fakes the figures by giving onlv
the overland imports from the other provinces And the
other provinces take over £5,000,000 worth of goods from
Queensland. And the other provinces offer, if Queens


land stops taxing their 2J millions worth of goods, not to
tax her 5i millions worth or thereabouts. Queens-
land, under Federation, can only suffer from scientific
Protection on a little more than half its imports. And
it now suffers (if that is the right word) scientific protec-
tion on all of them. And the Brisbane Teleoraph al-
leges, in Prevarication No. 7, that Protection is beneficial,
and Queensland has it now, and shouldn’t federate for
fear of losing it. While, in Prevarication No. 9, it urges
Queensland not to federate because the horrois of Pro-
tection will then be inflicted on it.

There are a number of other points worth

ruined into the bargain, will still be so strong

PLAIN ENGLISH.
“The Lash of Unlimited Taxation.”
Brisbane Telegraph urges the Queensland
parochialist to rise up and organise :
It will cost a little money, but it is better to pay for
freedom at the price of a few meetings and posters than
to pay the slave-master hereafter. When once he has
taken up that fearful lash, “unlimited power of taxa-
tion,” so strenuously advocated even by some of our own
people, Mr. Thynne to wit, it will be too late. We say,
“ Organise everywhere, and victory is absolutely sure.”
The “slave-master,” of course, is the Common-
wealth—the Australian nation—which makes
the above slab of logic look very beautiful
indeed. At present the people have “ un-
limited powers of taxation ” through the State
governments. When they federate they will
also have unlimited powers of taxation
through the Federal Government. The powers
won’t be any more unlimited than they are
now—when a thing is already absolutely un-
limited nothing on this earth can make it any
more unlimited than it is. It is expressly
stipulated (clause 51, section 2) that in taxation
matters there shall be no discrimination between
States—taxation must be absolutely uniform.
Therefore no State or combination of States
can impose taxation on other States without
also imposing it on themselves. And no State
wants to tax itself more than it can help. So
the Commonwealth Bill authorises the slave-
master (the people of Australia) to wallop the
slave (also the people of Australia) with “ that
fearful lash, unlimited power of taxation.” In
other words, it gives them the same power that
they have now, and which they always had,
and which they would have whether it was
specified in the Bill or not, to spank themselves
—to pound themselves with the lash aforesaid —
to grind themselves down with heavy taxation,
and jump on themselves, and chew their own
ear generally. Tenders are hereby invited for
a grand historical painting showing the Aus-
tralian citizen walloping himself with the lash
of “ unlimited power of taxation,” and, as his
own slave-master, driving himself along in
chains in front of himself and vainly asking
himself for mercy.

Secret Trials and Secret J’sF.
“ F.C.” : Speaking on the Address in Reply,
Vic. M.L.A. Hume Cook froze on to Chief
Sec. Peacock, as head of the Penal Dept., and
characterised the prison administration as
abominable. Which is so true that a Royal
Commission to enquire into the whole subject is
urgently called for. Instances could be fur-
nished of men sentenced to two or three years
whose sentences have been extended from time
to time by secret unreported trial, at which the
prisoner wasn’t formally arraigned before a
jury, or defended (just sentenced privately by
J.P. brought round for the purpose), to four,
five, and even nine years for comparatively
trifling irregularities. Some interesting infor-
mation also would be procurable about the
hideous effects of the “ solitary ” and “ separ-
ate ” systems ; such, for instance, as the fact of
three Pentridge prisoners being charged in one
day with attempted suicide. Said Commission
might further inquire as to how many persons
are now in Pentridge not “ doing time,” but
simply because when due for discharge they
were too ill or old and feeble to leave the gaol.
If we cannot yet hope for a rational scientific
treatment of society’s irregulars, gaols should,
at least, be kept exclusively for offenders—not
for the poor and the old and the sick. Also,
those disgraceful visiting justices’ courts—-
where the prisoner has no legal assistance, and
where his witnesses, if he have any, are gagged
—should be promptly abolished, and persistent
offenders against prison discipline should be
retried before a jury, in open court. Some of
the lunatic asylum brain-specialists, too, might
profitably take to studying incipient dementia
as induced in our prisons. And, anyhow, a
people that can find no better remedial agents
for criminality than the dark-cell, the lash and
the gallows has an unholy front to call itself
civilised.
The Pitiable Old Cove.
Melbourne Argus goes down on its two large
Conservative knees and implores Victoria not
to re-enact or extend the minimum-wage law,
because it will be disastrous to that good old
stalking-horse, “ the aged worker,” and deprive
him of employment. Not, let it be observed,
because it will be disastrous to the sweater.
The Tory never considers the sweater. With-
out the minimum wage the “ aged worker ” has
to struggle with the young worker for a living,
and with the minimum wage he has to do the
same thing. In bad cases he used to make per-
haps 15s. a week at the trade where the young
worker made 355. or 40s.—and his boss occa-
sionally employed the relic because he was 20s.
or 255. cheaper. The minimutn-wage law put
him up to, say, 255. a week, while the capable
young worker remains where he was ; and the
old remnant gets a job now and then because he
is 10s. or 15s. cheaper. In every trade the
young and thoroughly competent worker can
make over the minimum wage, and the aged
ditto only gets the minimum wage ; and when
the thoroughly competent man costs Bs. a day,
and the ancient only costs 55., and in cases
where there is only ss. worth of work to be
done, and the boss doesn’t want to pay
Bs. for ss. worth of work, then he employs the
venerable tradesman at ss. He curses a little
because he can’t get him at the old 3s. 6d. or
3s. or 2s. 6d., but he doesn’t bite off his own
ear to get level with his face, all the same. The
three great Tory griefs about the minimum
wage were : that by raising the screw of the
aged it would throw the aged out of work ;
that by enacting that beginners should be paid,
instead of working gratis as heretofore, it would
throw the very young out of work ; and that,
by preventing the incompetent middle-aged
from working below a certain figure, it would
throw the incompetent out of work. And The
Bulletin has five reasons for asserting that
this logic was all the poorest, silliest, saddest
kind of bosh, and the boshiest kind of hypo-
crisy :
(1) There is a certain amount of work to be done in
Victoria, and there are enough people to do it, Willi a few
over. That percentage over, which forms the social
problem everywhere, constitutes the unemployed.
(2) If the employers, by reason of a minimum-wage
law, sack all the old workers, then there won’t be enough
hands to do the work. The employers, by reason of the
surplus workers, can spare some of the old men, but not
all of them. They are partly dependent on the old men.
(3) There is quite as much work to be done under the
minimum wage as there was before —indeed it appears
from State statistics that there is more. Therefore if
there are more old men out of work because of the mini-
mum wage there must be fewer young men out of work.
It is quite as tragic, and as expensive for the State, and
as bad for the State, for a young man to starve as an old
one. In fact, it is worse, for a young man often has
young children dependent on him ; the old man seldom
has. Also the old man often has grown-up descendants
who can be made to contribute to his support; the young
one hasn’t. If the State has to support one of them it is
better for it to support the old man —the burden doesn’t
last so long. And when, as frequently happens, the
young man has young children to share his privations, it
means the stunting and weakening of future generations
—an argument which doesn’t apply in the old man’s case.
(4) Moreover, the old man who keeps his billet deserves
some consideration as well as the old man who loses his
billet. With no minimum wage, you might easily find 1000
very ancient parties working for wages that hardly kept
body and soul together, for the wages didn’t allow them
to have enough body to be worth mentioning. If a
minimum-wage law gives even 500 of these enough wages
to live as decent old men should, then it does well. And
if the other 500 have been sacked, and replaced by younger
men previously unemployed—well, 500 patriarchs out of
work are less tragic and less of a public burden and a
public evil than 500 young men out of work and 1000
patriarchs working for not enough to support them.
(5) Moreover, if the minimum-wage law throws the old
man out of work to make room for the younger one, the
State can provide for the venerable person to some
extent by old-age pensions. It can’t possibly pension
off the young men if they are left unemployed, in order
that the cheap and venerable ones may have the billets
under the old policy of unlimited competition.
For these five reasons, and for a number of
others which The Bulletin may endeavor to
specify when the next test-match is over, it
believes in the minimum wage for all trades
and for all Australia. And for these same
reasons it regards the lament about the alleged
old men who are alleged to have lost their
billets through the minimum wage as a very
poor, thin, shabby hypocrisy of the Tory
sweater. If the Tory crowd could produce
even one definite and visible old man who had
lost a definite and visible job through the
minimum wage law, and support him by reliable
affidavits, the hypocrisy might look a little less
mean than it does. But that actual and specific
old man hasn’t been forthcoming up to date.
A Solicitor and Four Zulus.
L. F. Heydon, an eminently peaceful solicitor,
who has never, so far as The Bulletin knows,
sought the bubble 6s. Bd. at the cannon’s
mouth, writes to Sydney Telegraph about the
Boers :
The Boers, who had 6000 men under arms against our
800 men, won the engagements at Laing’s Nek and
Majuba Hill. General Roberts was close at hand with
2000 British reinforcements, but unfortunately Mr. Glad-
stone interfered, and, as he said, “saved the English
nation from bloodguiltiness,” by arresting General
Roberts’s advance. As to the Boer courage and prowess.
At Majuba Hill and Laing’s Nek they certainly fought
well; but with the odds of numbers in their favor, and
our men were no doubt very badly led. That the Boers
have established no very wonderful record as dangerous
fighters is proved by their numerous previous defeats
having earned for them the nickname of “ the cowardly
Boers.” The Zulus, under Oetewayo, would have anni-
hilated them. But I have no wish to underrate their
courage. The Dutch have been always a race of brave
men. Indeed, they are our nearest blood relations in
Europe. I should suppose, therefore, that Dutch
colonists would fight about as well as British colonists.
The resistance that the Transvaal Boers can make to the
power of England may, therefore, be reckoned as being a
little larger than, say, Tasmania could make.
And here is a bit of Boer history from the same
paper :
The crowning disaster to the British arms was yet to
come. On 26th February, 1881, General Collet, with
600 soldiers climbed to the top of Majuba Hill. From
this point he hoped to command the Boer camp in the
Nek below. General Joubert recognised that the English
commander had gained a strategic advantage. At once
150 of his men volunteered to take the hill, and actually
made their way up, in face of the superior force above,
the bullets passing over them as if they were charmed.
At noon, Nicolaas Smit, with 70 or 80 farmers, scrambled
over the crest of the hill. The soldiers, seized with panic,
fled down the other side, leaving 92 killed, 134 wounded,
and 59 prisoners, as against one man killed and five
wounded on the Boer side. General Colley was among
the slain. Sir Evelyn Wood soon afterwards had com-
mand of a strong force, and was preparing to avenge
Majuba, when peace was proclaimed.
Query : If the Boers are the badly - licked
cowards that Mr. Heydon alleges, how did that
mere handful of farmers hold their own unaided
for over 50 years against the swarms of hard-
fighting savages who surrounded them ? And
if it is certain that the Zulus under Cetewayo
would have annihilated them, how did it come
to pass that Pretorius, wffth his 450 farmers,
broke a Zulu army 12,000 strong in that famous
battle of 16th December, 1838? And, seeing
that each Boer that day had to face over 24
Zulus as his own little share (and these were
times when the white man’s gun wasn’t nearly
so superior to the black man’s spear as it is
now, and when 24 niggers to one white was far
longer odds than it is in 1899), how would L. F.
Heydon, who scorns the Boers with a great
scorn, look facing, say, four Zulus ?
The Doing's of Policeman X.
The smellful case of the man lately sentenced
to three years’ gaol by Judge Williams at
Bendigo (Vic.), for forging and uttering a
cheque, again crops up. Accused produced
four witnesses and some documentary evidence
to show that he was in Adelaide at the date of
the alleged forgery, and asserted that he could
have produced many more only he hadn’t the
means to subpeena them, and the Yic. police,
who were prosecuting him, refused to assist.
Judge Williams made no inquiry into this
charge of attempting to suppress evidence for
the defence, nor did he adjourn the case to
allow of inquiry. He didn’t even suspend sen-
tence, but shunted accused off to do three
years. But he mentioned that if the Govern-
ment didn’t inquire into the matter it would be
" a blot ” on the administration of justice. The
Yic. Government has been inquiring, and all the
information obtained so far has been “favorable
to the prisoner.” The whole case is another odor-
iferous sermon on the need for a State Defender
and a State Defence Department. The penniless
prisoner can’t hire anyone to look up evidence
that may prove his innocence. He can’t, except
in rare cases, get anyone to do it gratis. He
can’t do it himself for he is safely locked up.
And the police will see him hanged first —in
fact, their business is to get him convicted and
hanged as much as possible, and to prevent
any evidence coming to light that may spoil
the conviction and the hanging, and thereby
prevent them figuring as able officers who
promptly sheeted the crime home. If an
innocent prisoner gets off it means that Police-
man X has made an ass of himself again by
capturing the wrong man, while the right one
gets away, and that crime is stalking with
impunity through the land through Police-
man X’s incompetence. So it is nobody’s
business except the public’s to save the
innocent accused ; the public doesn’t want
to have harmless men locked up even for Police-
man X.’s glorification while criminals are left
at large to do more damage, but then the public
is a very large, vague animal that counts for
little. On the other hand, it is the business of
Policeman X., who is a definite and concrete
person, to get somebody convicted—somebody
or anybody—for the sake of his own reputation.
And, except when a Judge rouses it by unusual
remarks about a blot, the Government seldom
inquires much into the doings of Policeman X.
A Forgotten Boomer.
G. N. Taylor, the first of Melbourne’s boom-
bank managers to be sent to gaol, was lately
under consideration as a debtor .to the Crown.
After serving an 8-years’ sentence at Pencridge,
the released Taylor was seized again on account
of a £5OOO fine, which constituted the second
clause in his sentence. Not having paid the
cash penalty, he had to retire into seclusion of
a comparatively free-and-easy order at Mel-
bourne gaol, whence he wrote, protesting that
he and his wife are penniless. The Crown was
somewhat sceptical on this point, but as the
result of inquiries the debtor got his,release
last week. Attorney-General Isaacs stated
that, although not absolutely convinced that
Taylor is unable to pay the fine, he thought
there w’as so much doubt surrounding the
matter that the Government would be war-
ranted in giving him his liberty. In one sense,
G. N. Taylor has cause for complaint against
the Crown, apart from the question of ways
and means to pay the £5OOO fine. It was not
all his fault that the Land Credit Bank fell to
pieces at such an early chapter in the Book of
Revelations. Later on thesamesmash would have
ranked as a mere “ unforeseen result of unwise
speculation,” and, even as it was, Taylor
would have escaped with an original punish-
ment of two years’ or so imprisonment but for
the honest indignation of Duffy, M.L.A., who
publicly commented on the “softness” of the
sentence passed by Judge Hodges, and caused
the prisoner to be further tried and convicted
on another charge. Melbourne, in those days,
was not educated to the subsequent stinkful
hush-it-up policy. The conspiracy to let finan-
cial bygones be bygones, and say no more about
’em, had not fairly started when Taylor’s
casual frauds came to light. If he could have
waited a while longer before getting found out
he might have enjoyed a black frock-coat and
the moral support of his Collins-street acquaint-
ances all these weary years. The Bulletin is
almost prepared to say that Taylor has com-
parative cause for suing the Yic. Crown for
defamation of boom-time character, in which
case the damages would probably cover the
amount of his unpaid fine and put things
square.

Deakin’s Newest Craze.
Alfred Deakin, ex-Spiritualist, ex-Buddhist,
ex-Theosophist, and ex-half-a-dozen other
faiths, is a Christian at time of going to press,
and is busily engaged working up Melbourne
into a demonstration against the secular educa-
tion which has prevailed in the State schools
since 1881. Alfred D., the “man with the
woman’s brain ” as he has been aptly called,
has been yelling himself hoarse in suburban
town-halls. “ We do not want our children to
grow up in ignorance of a God ; we do not
want the country flooded with criminals,”
shrieks dancing-Dervish Deakin to his monkey-
bearded followers. But, unfortunately, it is not
only the wild-eyed followers at Deakin’s
meetings who are affected. The great
mass of the people, who never dare to
believe anything until the daily paper or
some garrulous Deakin tells them, have be-
come doubters. A year or two ago, a man had
only to cry “ Free, secular, and compulsory”
to raise cheers; but to-day, in the absence
of anybody to yell that phrase loud enough, the
Deakin gag about criminals is being so gener-
ally believed that politicians and ministers are
stooping to temporise. The exact value of
secular education as a crime-factor in Victoria
may be easily determined. Public instruction
was begun by the State in 1872. For nine
years the religion - tinctured Irish National
school-books were used ; but in ’Bl Ramsay,
the Minister of the day, abolished these,
and free, secular, and compulsory has been
the rule since. Assuming that the average
pupil leaves school at 12, and that the
criminal commences his career at about 18,
the church-educated lads should have made the
crime-rate abnormally small until 1878, when
the State scholars came in with their natural
iniquity only modified by the Irish books)
Crime should then have continued at a moder-
ate rate until 1887, when the godless lads of 18
should have come along from the secular
schools seeking whom they could devour. And
from 1887 onwards the crime of Victoria should
have become steadily worse until at the pre-
sent date—when all of the criminal age are
products of the Free, Secular, and Compulsory
System—Victoria ought to be a seething den of
horrors. What is the actual condition of
things ? During 1898 there was less crime
legally recorded in Victoria than during any
year since 1879 —not less proportionate crime,
but less actual crime, and that in the face of
the fact that the population has quadrupled.
Of those who have swelled the statistics of
crime a big proportion exhibit the most fervent
devotion to religion. “I never searched the
effects of a thief yet,” says a prominent
Melbourne detective, “without finding some
evidence of strong piety.” It was the same
detective who arrested a jeweller’s shop sneak-
thief with a Bible in his pocket and a written
prayer folded in its leaves imploring Heaven
to gaze kindly on his enterprise. Of those who
have not gone to gaol but should have, the best
judgment may he given by procuring any daily
paper of ’BB, ’B9 <>r ’99 and listing the names on
the directorates of banks and companies which
have since “ gone broke ” under disgraceful cir-
cumstances. Nine out of ten are godly persons
who have been prominently connected with
some religions body. Nine out of ten, or there-
abouts, should he doing time to-day for their
cowardly and callous swindles. According to
all available Victorian statistics religious edu-
cation and crime go together. Victoria shuffled
off a great dead-weight when it got rid of the old
religious school system, and if it drinks too
much overproof Deakin and goes back to the
old ideas, it will probably find that the old ideas
are very much the same as in 1872.
The Forrest Govt, begins to fool with double-
edged weapons in W.A. Its programme for
the new session includes :
Measures dealing’with the dividend tax, rural land im-
provement, encouragement of local industries, concilia-
tion boards for industrial disputes, trades unions, electoral
reform, public service, truck system, bankruptcy, patents,
trustees’ investments, weights and measures, foreign com-
panies and looal registers, hospitals, Sunday observance,
a bill to provide for free education, and several other
measures.
Plainly Bigjohn realises that the democratic
element from the East has become too strong
for him and needs to be placated. So he will
crawl out of the old rut that he knows, and
try to get into the new rut that he doesn’t
know. And the old ’Gropers in the old rut will
disapprove of him leaving it, and the new
people will probably be disgusted at the half-
hearted way he gets into the new path. It is a
case of the leopard having to change his spots
in his old age—to get rid of the old comfortable
spots and get measured for a fresh lot. If
Forrest stays on as a hopeless Tory the
great democratic majority will wipe him
out sooner or later. And if he tries to
turn some kind of a democrat he has a very big
turning to do, and John Forrest turns just
about as actively as a town hall.

N.S. W. Judge Docker, who oscillates, as he
thinks, between the wise and the witty, said
the other day from the Bench that if drink
could be prohibited we would have a Garden of
Eden or an Arcadia. It may be taken that
there was no drink in the Garden of Eden, but
Judge Docker, as an amateur Sunday preacher,
regularly asserts that the crime that has
blasted the human race for 6000 years was per-
petrated there. And the race was only two
members strong —Adam and Eve. And when
Cain killed Abel, thus putting up the appalling
record of murdering 25 per cent, of the whole
human race, there was no devout brewer
Toohey building a St. Mary’s, or a holy Tooth
a Si. Andrew’s, out of the profits of “ boozing
up” poor old Adam and Eve or their scape-
grace Cain. It was the time of fresh, clear
water drinking, according to J udge Docker.
Apropos boiler-explosions and the need of
legislation on the subject, the N.S. W. Mining
Regulations make a particularly fine ass of
themselves in the same matter. They provide
that mining boilers shall be tested once a year
—and there they stop. Not a word is said as
to the nature of the test. Certainly it does not
matter much, because nearly two years ago
Judge Murray, then a Royal Commissioner,
pronounced this and most of the other Mining
Regulations ultra vires. These regulations are
supposed to protect the working miners ; yet,
though the Govt.’s own Commissioner pointedly
declared that they are insufficiently protected,
the department has shown no anxiety to im-
prove matters. The rush with which the
Commission was appointed is in striking con-
trast with the treatment which the Commis-
sioner’s damning report has received.
♦
Melbourne has lately been regaled on quite a
number of judicial farces. The last was in con-
nection with the prosecution of Detective Wil-
son for alleged perjury, arising out of the
notorious tote cases. Wilson swore that he
did not give a certain witness a copy of the
evidence he (Wilson) wanted the witness to
give. That was before the luncheon adjourn-
ment. After the court resumed, Wilson inti-
mated that he wanted to correct that section of
his evidence, and he then admitted that, on the
day before the trial, he had given the witness a
copy of the evidence he was to swear to. He
was committed for perjury on that clear issue,
and the said witness was prepared to swear
that, during the adjournment of the court,
Wilson made inquiries, and found that not
only was the evidence which he had given to
him not destroyed, but that in all proba-
bility it was in the possession of the other
side. When he came before the higher court,
however, it was found that the Law Dept,
had added so many other charges of perjury,
which it was impossible to sustain, to the
presentment, that the very clear issue on which
Wilson was committed was entirely clouded.
The jury returned a verdict of not guilty, but
the presiding judge showed what he thoilght of
the case by telling Wilson before he left the
dock that he had been guilty, as a detective, of
a very doubtful though common practice) inas-
much as he himself admitted that he did wait
upon the witness in question, to see whether he
was able to repeat correctly the evidence which
he (Wilson) wanted him to give.

„ Chinatown,” which made its
hrst Australian appearance in June, 1896, is back
for a two-weeks season at Her Majesty’s (Sydney).
It makes The Bulletin feel very old and very
much as if it had been up all night to think of the
years that have accumulated on top of this trifle
Also of the people who have got into gaol since
its first appearance ; and of the people who were
m gaol then but have since got out earlier than
they should have done; and of people whose hair
was then black but is now bright yellow ; also of
the folk who then had no teeth worth speaking of
and who now have 32 of them in their mouths and
32 more in a drawer to change with. The old
Chinatown ” came to storm Sydney as a sure
success, and the new one comes to cover the
disastrous retreat of the “ Stranger in New York ”
which couldn’t possibly hang out any longer.
Ihe company is the same in one or two spots
i Sadie M‘Donald sleeps now in
Waverley Cemetery. Of the two fat men—
the one that used to sing to the other fat man
and the fat man that used to listen—only one fat
man is with the present co., so a rather leaner
man has to be sung to instead. Lawton the
amazing whistler, is whistling in other parts, and
Sam Marion takes his place. Harry Conor’s Wel-
land Strong is as good as ever, and he was exceed-
ingly good at all times; and Hugh Ward makes
as satisfactory a Ben Gay as his predecessor
Miss Carrie Moore, too, is a very bright little
maid as Flirt, and the writer has no fault to find
with the new widow. Public opinion seems to
rise up in many sixpenny bars to say that the new
widow. Miss Allene Crater, can’t compare with
the old one, but The Bulletin is half inclined to
give the present short-skirted mourner the pre-
ference.
* * * *
As for “ Chinatown ” itself, this paper
feels less critical than it did in ’96. It has gone
through many harrowing experiences since then.
It has seen the “Stranger in New York,” for one
thing, and after that anybody would feel less
critical and more thankful for middle-sized
mercies. It has discovered that American musical
comedy generally contains a giddy old man who
ambles on two unreliable legs after girls, and a
divorced female, a,nd a> ball, and some girls who
stay out all night in short frocks, and a fat man
who swims in cocktail. And it has learned to
take these things as they come, like washing-day
and the coal-bill and other advantages and calami-
*les - By comparison, the “ Trip to Chinatown ”
is all that this paper said it wasn’t three years ago
—a bright, clever production full of smart dia-
logue, and without a dull moment or a dull saying
or a dull character in it from first to last. The
person who doesn’t see it will thereby make a mis-
take that he will regret for ever afterwards. This
isn t exactly what The Bulletin remarked in its
issue of 4th July, 1896, but it is how it feels now
after seeing “The Stranger in New York.”
Chinatown ” is staged as effectively as ever, and
it will last till the end of next week, when Harry
Conor’s company retires from the scene rather sud-
denly.
** * *
There will be another change of bill at
Sydney Royal on Saturday (July 15), when the
“Physician,” by Henry Arthur Jones, will be
offered to a discriminating public. It is a pity
that so pretty a thing as the “ Summer’s Day ”
should be good for only a fortnight’s run, but it is
not a play that appeals to the masses, though to
the poetic soul it is calculated to be a thing of
beauty and a joy for at least three consecutive
weeks. But the poetic soul is so rare that it is
necessary for most of it to be there on the same
night to fill the big Royal, and when the cold
rams of heaven rained upon it and caused a good
part of it to stay away, there was no visible cause
for keeping the “Summer’s Day ” on view after
the second week. Any person who desires to get
a glimpse of a really beautiful little play is hereby
warned that this (Thursday) and to-morrow nights
are the last opportunities.
** * *
Holloway’s tale of Blood and Sin com-
mences every evening at 7.45 punctually, and as
Mr. Stanford isn’t beheaded till a little after 11,
the audience gets great value for its money. The
present Lyceum drama has one great merit that
cannot be overlooked. It is a painful necessity at
the end of every drama that the hero must be
reprieved, and his character cleared, and he and
his best girl must be rescued out of the furnace of
affliction before their clothes are quite burnt off
them. In most cases they are rescued too soon,
and have time to make a long, weary speech about
how their future lives will be peaceful, and un-
interesting, and as flat as the Old Man Plain, and
how they will doze by the fireside in the evening,
and go to bed regularly at 9.30, and lead a per-
fectly unemotional and cow-like existence for ever
after as the reward of virtue. In the present
instance there isn’t time to go fully into the usual
particulars about the utter dreariness that is to
come, and that is at least something to be thank-
ful for.
** * #
Cinquevalli, and the Valdares with their
remarkable biking feats, and Richard Douglas,
who is a very pleasant solo humorist, together
with Harry Shine and sundry new and old artists,
are keeping the Tivoli boom alive, and that small
but honest theatre is still doing well.
** * *
When this papor went to press it was
understood that the season of the Georgia Min-
strels and Cake Walkers, at Sydney Palace, Was
to close on Wednesday— which was yesterday.
Miss Amy Castles has also left, and the Castles
boom in Sydney is already dead—so dead that no-
body has even mentioned the young lady in the
writer s hearing for the last five days. The con-
gested amusement market of Sydney should now
have time to recover a little.

There’s a growing demand in Melbourne
for pictures on a sheet. “Uitlander” Samson,
the genial little journalist who contemplates man-
kind through a pane of glass, is disclosing his
views of South Africa in sundry city and suburban
places. Also, the Saturday-night appearances of
John Fuller’s show at the Town Hall have an up-
ward business tendency. The Myrioramic back-
ground is very good of its kind, and the junior
Fuller who lectures what time he isn’t playing a
piano with one hand and an organ with
the other, gets through a lot of work in
a. cheerful manner. Moreover the incidental
singing represents full value for the public’s
humble “bob.” Last Saturday night the pretty
tenor warble of Fuller perc was supported by a
capable co. including Miss Lenore Orelli, a vastly
improved artist since she “came out’’(and soon
went in again) some three years ago. Miss Orelli’s
enunciation is rather odd, as of yore, but her notes
are beautiful. The Myriorama bill for this week’s
Saturday will be “From London to Niagara
