Paul Pelham, a seeming disciple of Charles
Godfrey, with a large reputation as a music-hall
songwright, is the newest feature at Melb. Bijou.
Likewise the gentleman who left his little back-
room at Bloomsburee is once more upon the
Rickards job, and several other people of com-
paratively recent interest are taking their turns
as before.
** * *
Bland Holt’s guillotine drama holds out
manfully at the Royal, where the difference
between the. elegant fit of Walter Baker’s prison
suit and the felonious bagginess of Brown’s ditto
is freely commented on. The fact that Baker goes
forth to execution in a poetic black tie and
superior boots is likewise adding to the popu-
larity of Bland Holt, who arrives at the last
moment on an educated horse, armed with a
free pardon for the hero aforesaid. Apart
-^ le release of Baker, the finish of
“Straight from the Heart” is open to doubt.
After the Nihilists have burst-up the guillotine
with their timely bomb Brown is left to roam at
large, and The Bulletin would like to ask in that
much mentioned name of the French Republic,
whether Brown is afterwards doomed to sneeze
into the basket, or is released on his own un-
supported allegation of Albert Norman’s guilt.
The curtain falls in a hurry just when this question
is beginning to arise.
** * *
The kernel of Pinero’s new play, which
Brough is about to produce at Sydney Royal :
The gay Lord Quex wishes to marry Muriel Eden.
Muriel’s friend, Sophy Fullgarney, knows Quex for a
rake, and desires to spoil the match. She tries to entice
the sinner herself, but he won’t consent. Luckily she
overhears a talk he has with the Duchess of Strood.
This lady Quex had met, so to say, in Naxos, where she
had been used to receive him in her own room with a
peculiar brand of champagne and cigarette. Being senti-
mental, Ariadne desired a last farewell, and named her
chamber at midnight and another bottle of the old
brand as its components. Sophy heard Theseus
promise to come. Fortune favored her; she
P' ace of the Duchess’s maid, dressed
the Duchess—and went forthwith to spy at the
keyhole. The Duchess tied a ribbon on the outer
handle of the other door, and then Quex, deeply bored,
came in. Presently they remarked the keyhole,
opened the door suddenly, and there was Sophy.
Quex packed the Duchess off to Mrs. Jack, and
rang for Sophy. She comes in with a wonderful
strut and toss of the head, and Quex locks the door.
What has she heard ? Why, everything 1 What will she
do ? Why, break off the engagement ! What will she
take? £2OOO, £4OOO, £8000? She is inexorable. ‘Very
well, then,’ says in effect the man; * how are you
going to get out? If you are found here, as you will be,
you are ruined. The Duchess is safely off. They will think
it a vulgar intrigue of mine with you.’ They take point
after point until the poor girl, reaching the climax of the
case against her, remembers that her fiance, a profes-
sional palmist, is sleeping in the house. Even then she
gives up her all for Miss Eden. J After all,* she says, ‘ I
may be ruined, but she won’t marry you,’ and she rings
the bell to alarm the household. Quex is overwhelmed
by so great a pluck. Before the awakened servants have
reached the door he has found words to thank her as she
deserves, and to offer her the key. She dismisses the
people with a true Cockney flourish, and reels off to bed
vmdly assuring Quex, ‘ You’re a gentleman, and I’ll do
what I can for you.’ ”
** * *
Marshall Hall’s anxiety for the musical
welfare of Melbourne has moved him to snort at
Scherek’s orchestral concerts. The want of har-
mony in the selection of the items is what hurts
Marshall Hall’s soul. “No true artist can take a
pleasure in listening to such programmes,” he
says, for even the “ works of the great masters ”
are no good individually if they don’t form a
beautiful sequence of numbers on the programme.
Marshall Hall is trying hard to educate the people
to a proper understanding of true worthlessness
in music. He has cursed comic songs, laughed at
light opera, blackguarded Newbury’s ballad con-
certs, and bemoaned the bad taste of Ben no
Scherek. His faithful disciples had better make
up their minds, once and for all, that there is no
pleasure in listening to any sort of performance
where Marshall Hall doesn’t boss the proceedings.

The Bulletin is informed by a son of Richard Capper
whose demise was here noted (17/6/99), that the latter’s
father was a personal friend of Lord Byron, and that
instead of being merely Coppin’s stage-carpenter, he was
George’s chief mechanist and a participant in the manage-
ment of the theatre.
“ H.” : The Maoriland demise of Towle, better known
as Harrison, so long conductor of W. and M.’s opera co ,
has been rather passed over. Many years ago he was
musical critic for the Aoe, and, while in clerkly business,
wrought up to high musical accomplishments. His wife
used to be a popular singer and actress, as Miss A'urip
Kelton.
Dear Bulletin.— The statement that Carrie Moore was
educated at Geelong Convent looks like a theatrical
romance or a paragraphist’s fiction. And, anywav, the
statement that she “ went direct from cloister to stage ”
is absolute tominy-rot. For that matter, Cissy Loftus
didn’t go “ direct from cloister to stage.” She made her
first appearance at the London ’alls as a young mimic of
popular actresses or variety artistes. It must have taken
her some time to complete her studies of the originals,
whom she certainly hadn’t seen in a convent.—E.
“ Hermes" : One thing that sticks out prominently at
Sydney Palace Theatre is the ease with which the dark
people fire off their melody. The average white singer
makes fearfully hard work of it, and it is often most har-
rowing to see him growing purple in the face, expanding
his chest, glaring over the orchestra, and staggering
about as he lifts his heavy voice up to the vaulted roof.
But Gerard Miller simply looks at you in a friendly way,
as though he was going to ask you to have a drink, and
then—the very seats thrill. One wonders where it comes
from. Also Flora Batson, a frail-looking little woman,
never moves a muscle, and the melody bubbles up
through the limelight and flows all through' the building.
It is restful after a hard day’s work.
Some of the “ business” in the “King’s Musketeer,”
at Melb. Princess wants “ editing ” badly. For instance
when Miladi Watson gets from Cardinal Richelieu a
lettre de cachet for putting her enemies in the Bastille,
she calmly takes the written document and rubs it on
the blotting-paper 1 Richelieu might just as well write
the order with a fountain-pen. “ Pounce,” generally
made of powdered resin, or gum sandarac—was used to
dry ink in those days, and was powdered on to the paper
from a castor. Indeed, the writer knows of an old-
fashioned Tory London attorney who uses pounce in his
private office to this day, and looks Upon blotting-paper
as new-fangled.
Ere Henrietta Watson was engaged for “The King’s
Musketeer” Musgrove, Who Was looking for a good star
in London, had intended to secure Lewis Waller and his
wife, Florence VVest. Waller is one of the verv best of
the younger actors in Loridoh, fine-looking, very intelli-
gent, and a genuirte artist. lie has played Ibsen, arid
pleases even the fastidious Wm. Archer. Florence West
is a red-haired mummeresS, fond of strorig. parts. The
Wallers Were willing to come, but when the chance of
engaging MiSs Watsori turned up, the Firth thought she
would be more of a draw. Probably they were right—
though Waller will keep.
The conscientiousness devoted to the work of dramatic
criticism by Melbourne papers was made evident to the
densest last week when the three dailies carefully re-
corded a change in the cast at the Princess, Miss Einelie
Hughes taking Miss Harrie Ireland’s place as the Queen,
and Miss Rose Musgrove the part of Gabrielle. The
change, each paper insisted, led to a decided improve-
ment in “The King's Musketeer.” The fact that the
change of cast did not happen till the following evening
was a mere inadvertence.
The two Englishes, who give a clever musical “turn”
at Melb. Bijou, experienced a “ University Students’
Night” the other week, and made a pardonable mistake
in considering themselves insulted by the rowdv young-
sters. The trouble started with some students’‘chaff, of
the usual inane character, which provoked a few feeble
retorts from the male English. One or more of the
idiotic “ humorists ” in the gallery replied by throwing
things—bits of orange-peel, &c. —and something that
looked like a lump of crusty bread hit the stage very near
to where the lady English was standing. Under the cir-
cumstances her husband's subsequent attempt at a
satirical speech was not so foolish as the students’ apolo-
gists professed to consider it. The Englishes are new-
chums, their entertainment doesn’t invite badinage or
practical jokes, and they couldn’t be expected to regard
the hurling of the aforesaid missiles as amiable expres-
sions of innocent merriment.
It has been pointed out to The Bulletin that certain
absurdly bigoted remarks concerning this paper's critical
attitude towards Miss Amy Castles, which were attributed
to Sydney Freeman’s Journal, were in reality to be
(dis)credited to the Catholic Press. The Bulletin is
glad to find that the F. J. does not countenance such in-
sane sectarianism, but has at the same time to say that the
two Sydney Catholic organs were in such general unison
about The B.’s iniquity in not humbly swallowing Miss
Castles whole, that a little confusion of ideas in the
matter was not surprising.
“ Delcomyn” : As Amy Castles has done so well out of
benefit concerts, the other provinces are naturally also
seeking out budding prodigies for the public to applaud
and send to Europe for musical education. Which is hard
upon said public, as it opens out limitless possibilities as
regards the rabbit-artist and the fish-warbler and the
bottle-seraph. Moreover, there are plenty of street-
singers who would greatly benefit by an academy educa-
tion, and Sydney could rest in peace until the Boer War
breaks out and we have something important to occupy
our minds.
Sydney Morning Herald speaks of the fact having been
overlooked in “certain quarters”— i.e., The Bulletin
quarter—that Amy Castles may develop into a very suc-
cessful vocal artist without attaining the position of a
Melba. But the young lady was boomed in Melbourne as
a future Melba or a future Tietjens, and already she has
earned in three months more money than the average
accomplished artist can earn in three years. The boom
is ridiculous, and the S.M.H. chooses to overlook that
palpable fact.
“Cockaigne”: Gordon’s masterpiece was his scenery
for the “ Merchant of Venice,” with the Bancrofts, in the
little Prince of Wales Theatre, London. Poor G.G. used
to speak of how cramped this stage was. When “ Peril”
was brought out, the principal “ setting” had to remain
unmoved all through the 100 nights of the piece, other
scenery being built in front.
The nice old lady in “One Summer’s Day’’explains
that she conveyed uncle's message to his erring nephew :
“I told him exactly what you said, except a damn and
two devils.”
Cinquevalli has learned one trick that all in his line
should strive to catch on to—the art of making a failure
look like a deliberate intention, by a smart recovery. If
he drops a ball, he picks it up with so clever a trick that
everyone is glad of the slip.

of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, has just secured a
contract for the manufacture and supply of all the bolts,
nuts and washers which may be required by the Victorian
railways during the next three years. He has also con-
tracted to supply all the carbonic acid gas necessary for
working the railway refrigerating and ice-making plant—
this is onffof I. and E. Hall’s patent carbonic anhydride-
machines, the same system as has been adopted by the
Govts, of South Australia, Queensland, and Western Aus-
tralia. Mr. Ferguson has a large stock of these machines
(suitable for meat-preserving co.’s dairies, butchers, and
others) on hand at present, which he is selling at greatly-
reduced prices. *

COLUMN
The tardy arrival of Westralia’s revenue returns
for the year ended 30th June completes the list. Results
(Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria, S.A., and Westralia being
for the year to 30th June ; Maoriland for the year to 31st
March ; Tasmania for the six months to 30th June) are
Increase as
compared with
Revenue. corresponding
period of
last year.
Victoria (12 months) .. £7,378,842 £491,379
Queensland ( „ ).. 4,174,086 405,934
N.S.W. ( „ ).. 9,754,184 272,092
. Maoriland ( ~ ). . 6,186,428 198,796
S.A. ( „ ).. 2,665,501 98,890
Tasmania ( 6 months) .. 461,948 17,873
Decrease as
oompared with
corresponding
period of
last year.
Westralia (12 months) .. 2,478,811 £275,935
One moral attached to these figures is that the
Westralian Federal difficulty is rapidly settling itself.
The one great reason why Westralia “didn’t see its way ”
was that its revenue was so extraordinary during the
boom-time that it didn’t want to go into partnership with
any common, ordinary place in the east. The partner-
ship would have involved no very great loss of money at
any time. The Federal tariff would probably be as high
as that of Westralia, for that province has a very moder-
ate tariff—it wasn’t the size of its duties, but the incredi-
ble amount of dutiable goods it purchased in the roaring
days that swelled the receipts. Under Federation it will
still be credited with the duties on all its foreign goods,
whether imported direct or via some other province, so
there will be no loss there. And it was agreed that W.A.
should retain its interprovincial duties for a time, and at
the end of that time, the chances are that W.A. would
have got down to about the level of its neighbors, in
revenue and imports, and there would be no special ad-
vantages to lose. The following figures show how W.A.
is getting down :
Revenue per inhabitant.
1896 1897 ( 1898 1899
Westralia £l4 4 3 £2O 12 2 £l7 0 3 £l4 14 10
Queensland 718 2 713 11 715 6 8 7 6
New South Wales 74 10 73 6 707 74 11
South Australia 748 79 10 751 74 11
Victoria 5 9 4 512 10, 517 1 6 5 8
Tasmania 415 9 417 8 501 543
In 1897, the gap between Westralia and Tasmania (the
largest revenue and the smallest) was £l5 14s. 6d. per
head ; in ’99 (just two years’ later) the discrepancy had
got down to £9 10s. 7d. In ’97 W.A. had nearly three
times the revenue of Queensland ; now it has less than
twice the revenue. Also, the two provinces at the tail of
the list are catching up very fast with the two in the
middle. The arguments against Federation based on dis-
crepancies of revenue mostly show a tendency to vanish as
time goes on.

* ♦ * *
Queensland had a gorgeous outbreak of loan ex-
penditure for the financial year to 30th June, getting
through no less than £1,090,539. This was equal to £2 3s.
per inhabitant —the worst record in Australasia, barring
that of Westralia. Some recent years’ loan expenditure in
Queensland: —
1892 .. £662,281 1896 .. £592,158
1893 .. 1,035,523 1897 .. 1,148,341
1894 .. 378,077 1898 .. 937,066
1895 .. 230,120 1899 .. 1,090,539
Queensland tried a year or two of decent economy, and it
was a bad time, for the province had grown so accus-
tomed to living on borrowed money that it couldn’t brace
up and be decently independent all at once. So it
promptly gave up trying and started to lay the founda-
tions of another boom and another great smash. Now
population is increasing reasonably fast because the
country supplies the new people with work and wages
out of borrowed money ; and the revenue is better be-
cause the inrush of borrowed money is whooping trade
along again, and the surplus is larger because a lot of the
expenditure is charged to loans. The loan expenditure
per inhabitant of the various provinces during the last
few years is an interesting study ;
1895-6 1896-7 1897-8 1898-9
N.S.W. .. £0 19 11 £1 310 £1 4 10 £1 11 3
Vic. .. 038 045 07 10
Q’sland .. 159 288 118 8 280
S.A. .. 1 910 019 11 17 3
Tas. .. 014 310 10 0 095
W.A. .. 6 8 7 I 18 18 4 11 14 2
All through this period Victoria was the most self
dependent and solvent of the Australian provinces—in
fac,t it was the only one of the lot that at any time came
anywhere near paying its way out of revenue. Westralia
was the very worst of the crowd all along. Queensland
has gone down from second best to second worst. Tas-
mania has grown more solvent while N.S.W. has degener-
ated. But the trail of the grand old Australian borrow is
over them all.
* * •» »
Now that Old Age Pensions have been made a
definite feature of the Victorian Government’s programme,
preachers and philanthropists and other useful people
have begun to rain torrents of good advice upon Plain
George Turner as to how he should raise the money.
Most of these valuable characters agree that further taxa-
tion is inadvisable, but that the land-revenue, or part of
it, should be set apart as a pension-fund. The plain
brown fact that if you take (say) £200,000 a year of land
receipts out of the general revenue for a pension-fund,
it will be necessary to raise another £200,000 a year
by taxation to fill the gap in the general revenue, is
quite beyond the mental range of these well-meaning per-
sons. A special genius, however, proposes issuing a
“pension stamp,” which would have to be affixed to all
receipts for wages at the rate of sd. for every ss. The
principal trouble hereabouts is that very few people give
receipts for wages, and if they all did, and all put on the
Jd. stamp, there aren’t enough wages in Victoria to supply
the required revenue. This is quite apart from the fact
that the Jd. stamp would be a terrible waste of paper and
gum.
* * * *
The Royal Bank of Queensland, which always
gets its half-yearly balance-sheet in first—it is to be hoped
this result isn’t arrived at by slurring the audit, but any-
how, the Queensland Royal’s document comes out with
amazing celerity—shows the best half-year’s profit
(£7830) for the last seven years. The profits since ’94
have been :
June, 1894 .. £6,360 June, 1897 .. £7,463
December, 1894 .. 6,477 December, 1897 .. 7,440
June, 1895 .. 6,946 June, 1898 .. 7,460
December, 1895 .. 6,033 December, 1898 .. 7,579
June, 1896 .. 6,445 June, 1899 .. 7,830
December, 1896 .. 6,515
The half-year’s profit is at the rate of £3 13s. 3d. percent,
per annum on shareholders’ funds, as compared with £3
11s. 3d. last half. The Royal pays its usual 2* per cent,
dividend, which, with dividend duty thereon, absorbs
£5075; adds £2OOO to reserve fund, making it
£43,000; and the other £755, with previous bal-
ance, leaves £2329 at credit of profit and loss. The
Royal’s wise policy, pursued ever since the 1893 troubles,
of carrying a fixed proportion of its profits to reserve, and
investing the reserve-fund separately in State securities,
is producing good results. The business in general looks
about the same as last half-year, but the reserve of cash
and liquid securities has increased from £299,514 to
£319,056, and now equals 36 per cent, of all liabilities to
the public, as compared with 33 per cen t. at the end of
’9B. The Royal of Queensland, like many other small
banks, has a large capital in proportion to the amount of
business done, and the average small bank of its class is
safer from a depositor’s point of view than many more
imposing institutions. The present balance-sheet looks
satisfactory all round. In a country where there is no
independent State-audit and State-valuation of securi-
ties, nothing better than that can be said of any bank.
The respectable increase in profits is no doubt
owing, in great part, to a perfectly new cause which ought
to make the profits of all Australian banks go up with a
sudden rush this half-year. Six months ago it was
decided to charge every customer ss. per half-year for the
trouble and ink and mental strain of keeping his account,
and the first charge was made on 30th June. If a bank
has 2000 accounts that means £5OO extra profit in one act.
The charge is not on live accounts only ; wherever a
vanished customer has left a small balance, the ss. will be
levied regularl.v till it is used up. So the innovation cuts
three ways : it furnishes a large source of revenue out of
current business which should make the banking industry
look up considerably; it enables all unclaimed balances
to be appropriated by degrees, which is more revenue ;
and by wiping out these old balances it saves much
trouble and woe in carrying the weary desolate fragment
forward from year to year.
* * * *
Victoria has a surplus (estimated) of about
£250,000 this year, which will scrape a small bit off the
enormous accumulated deficit. But already there are
demands for reduced taxation on one side, and more ex-
penditure on the other, and Turner is told that there is
less need for economy because the finances are now
squared, which they aren’t —not by a good 2J millions.
In N.S.W., it is fixed by law that £150,000 a year must be
allotted to reduce the deficit —it is part of the regular
expenditure, and if the Treasurer doesn’t succeed in pro-
viding the £150,000, he has the disgrace of showing a
deficit himself. In Victoria, there is no law requiring any
annual payment in reduction of the deficit. Turner may
feel confident that, while he is in office, he will keep
reducing it steadily, but that isn’t the question. The
country wants a law passed making it obligatory on the
possible spendthrift who comes after Turner to do like-
wise.

Mudgee, N.S.W.: “ A large number of gold-

Gallvmont, N.S.W., now worked by about 20
tributors, crushed 500 tons for 2920 z. gold, which pays
well. Tributors are prepared to sink to a deeper level if

* * * *
Cobar Gold-mines, N.S.W. (old Fort Bourke),
expects to make a start next month with its 100-head
mill. Another £30,000 has been expended in cyanide and
other machinery and mine-development, bringing the
total up to over £60,000. The O.G.M.’s is probably the
biggest and most up-to-date cyanide plant in Australasia,
but, as far as writer can hear, though provision is made
for the classification of slimes, no arrangement has yet
been made for their treatment—which means keeping
back about a third of the gold. Once more—why has no
attempt been made to penetrate the copper zone in this
property ? Some months ago the co. was asked to sell an
area between the main shaft and the Cobar-Chesney
boundary, on which ground it has done no work and
apparently doesn’t intend doing any; yet the co. modestly
asked £50,000 for it. If it got copper-ore below 300 ft.
like that of the Great Cobar, and the chances are in its
favor, the problem of ore-treatment would be solved for
all time : simply because the gossans and other oxidised
ores would make a smelting blend with the copper sul-
phides such as is made at the Great Cobar copper-mine.
* * * *
At the Cobar-Chesney =£20,000 has already been
spent, but it will cost another £15,000 to complete the
winding, air-drilling, concentrating and smelting plant.
Main shaft cut down to 400 ft., leaving 160 ft. to complete.
Some four or five months must elapse before smelting
starts. Upwards of 250,000 tons copper ore are said to be
in sight, and nearly 10,000 tons at grass. Recently one
expert of repute estimated visible ore at 30,000 tons only ;
the mine authorities regard this gentleman as very short-
sighted. Owing to trouble with old co.’s directors, who
foolishly wanted £12,000 in debentures called up within a
month, the new directors decided to make two Is. calls
before the issue, so as to make themselves independent of
the cussedness and obstinacy of the old co.’s board.
• • • •
Great Western, Cobar : Another 3d. call. Mar-
ket wide—buyers, Is. ; sellers, 4s. Might be made a big
mine : never with baby management. The proceeds of
the call are supposed to be devoted to paying for a
cyanide plant, by which the co.’s little heaps of rather
coppery tailings will be tackled.
The rain which fell the other day deprived
Cobar Gold Mines of £lO a day from the Copper Syndi-
cate as water-money. The Young Australian is crush-
ing, and all look forward to a few cleans-up to justify the
market-price of shares. Though there has been water in
the Great Western tank for three weeks, no attempt has
been made to start crushing, and the co. has gone in
search of more copper ore. The Occidental mill and cya-
nide plant are in full swing, and it is believed by those
concerned that the bank overdraft will soon be paid off.
The ore at present under treatment is from the lower
levels of the mine, and prospects never looked brighter.
The first forthcoming mill and cyanide clean-up will, it is
thought, produce £l5OO worth of gold.
Mr. 0. G. Warnford Lock, in departing from
Wentworth Mines (N.S.W.), lets off a tale of woe. Upper
levels exhausted and all working faces impoverished ; a
barren zone encountered and outlook generally mournful.
Nothing left for the co. but to treat 50,000 tons of tailings
and to work out the few remaining stopes. For, as the
departing super, puts it, it is not as if they had lost their
way and had failed to get on to the track of ore-bodie3 ;
they are right on the veins themselves, which have lost
their gold with depth. This seems to be accounted for
by the increased dimensions of the diorite belt and the
corresponding pinching of the serpentine—a contingency
of which writer was always afraid ; for he knows no deep
mine in Australia working in serpentine. Tons of gold
have come out of this mine, the shutting down of which
looms near. Truly a doleful prospect for the poor little
syndicates around it looking for the Lucknow “ joint.”
Lloyd's Copper Company Ltd., London, took
over Burraga (N.S.W.) copper-mine last Saturday, when
Lloyd junior and lawyer received £95,000 on behalf of Lloyd
senior, who had already pocketed £5OOO deposit. The pur-
chase includes Lloyd’s old refining-works at Lithgow, with
all copper matte there, on the road, and at the mine.
Everything, in fact, but some 20 tons of refined copper
awaiting shipment. The deal includes the town of Bur-
raga, such as it is. The concern will run on as usual till
the arrival of the consulting-engineer from London, when
a new system of mining on a bigger scale, and working
and smelting the waste-dumps, will be determined. G.
A. Stockfeld takes the general management after years of
travelling throughout Australasia examining and report-
ing on mines without finding many to please him. The
co.’s capital is £250,000 ; working capital, £50,000. Not a
case of over-oapitalisation considering the purchase price
and working capital ; the thing is a sure-enough invest-
ment if copper keeps anything like its present price. It
must have been a shock to Lloyd, who is somewhere in
England, to receive the purchase-money, for he has, dur-
ing the last 15 years, pocketed so many deposits on op-
tions for smaller amounts that he must have made abso-
lutely certain the deposit on the bigger sum would also be
forfeited. At last he has got his biggest price, and others
have got the mine. For over 20 years work has been
almost exclusively confined to one run of ore._ Less than
£lOOO would pay for all the prospecting done in that time.
It is now essential to pay fair mining interest on a quarter
of a million, and the London management, which is usually
extravagant, has not only to work over the enormous
waste ore and slag dumps, but to develop the property
outside of the shoot, which has been persistently followed
down to an incline depth of 900 ft. If the co. is as fortu-
nate in the selection of its mine as it is in its manager,
it is to be congratulated.
* » * *
The weighing-clauses of N.S.W. Coal Mines
Regulation Act have at last become effective, and penal-
ties of £3 and costs have been inflicted upon the managers
of three Newcastle mines. In these cases Govt, prose-
cuted. The Act is now almost three years old. The
clauses which enforced fresh burdens on miners were put
in action at once, but those which increased the responsi-
bility and assured the just dealing of mine-owners were
Ministerially suspender! for almost three years. Probably
the near opening of Parliament has spurred Govt, to
action.
* * • *
Broken Hill Municipal Council consists of 12
men, just half of whom are pledged to a Labor platform
and to a solid vote. Mostly the matter under considera-
tion is decided against the Labor men by the Mayor’s
casting vote ; and it was on the ex-Mayor’s casting-vote
that the present Mayor was appointed. But now, one of
the other six being absent —he is chasing copper slugs at
Chillagoe—the Labor half-dozen have an innings, and
start off with a notice to B.H. Prop. Co. that if it
doesn’t pay up its rates (it is assessed at £2300) in 14 days
the bailiffs will go in. A few weeks ago the co. offered to
pay £2OOO, but this was declined ; and now it will not
pay a cent. Apparently the co.’s contention is that the
mine is on a proclaimed goldfield and not liable. There is
much law in prospect.
Mt. Lyell is now taking over 2000 tons of cus-
tom-ores monthly, and expects to double that quantity
within the next few months. The co. will make much
money in this business, and the mines around will pro-
bably find that it will pay to allow it to do so.
* * * *
A Bulletin correspondent exalts the Briseis
tin-mine :—The Briseis lately paid a Is. div.—amount
£3ooo—and it is announced that flotation on London
market is assured. Strange that most of our most im-
portant mines are handed over to British investors. The
Briseis shareholders have, it is true, spent an enormous
sum in development of, unfortunately, a more experi-
mental than practical nature, but the present manage-
ment has undoubtedly placed the mine upon the div. list
at last. Last month’s yield was 30 tons, result of 30 men’s
labor, thus showing that each man raised one ton for the
month. The man receives £'9, the co. £Bo—a nice profit
of £7l on each man employed. Oan you point to another
mine in Australia that will return the same profit upon
the same expenditure ? Yet the shareholders are sacrific-
ing the property for the benefit of British money-bags.
As room is made more labor will be employed, and as
there can be no doubt about the enormous bulk of tin-
drift to be operated upon, 200 men may soon be at work
and an average of 200 tons per month be the result.
P.S.—There seems to still be a good deal of difference of
opinion about the Briseis. The above is absolute fact.
I was there yesterday and the face is simply magnificent;
some seams of pure black ore are 3in. thick, and they are
numerous throughout the face; the bottom is not yet
touched.

That powerful and persistent boomer, “ Moses
Moss,” in London Truth, says that Peakhill Goldfields
(W.A.) at £7 10s. are very tempting, and takes up half-a-
column to show why. Says that the mine is producing
over 5000 oz. a month. This is false, and, what’s more,
“ M.M.” must know it’s false. The last 3-ield of over
5000 oz. was the result of three months’ crushing, and
£7los. a share represents over two millions of money, on
which the Peak is not paying 3 per cent. Moses declares
that with the additional 20 head ordered the production
should be close on 10,000 oz. per month. This is another
boomer, for even if 30 heads can produce 50000 z., which
they don’t, how are 50 going to double it? He admits
having no knowledge of Schlapp’s report, but is quite sure
it is favorable ; because Gov. “ Jerry ” Smith spoke in the
highest terms of the mine it is assumed he got his cue
from Schlapp. The more reasonable assumption is that
if Schlapp’s report is distinctly favorable something would
have been heard about it, as it must ha,ye been written
over a month ago. Probably when shareholders (at £7
10s.) do read Schlapp’s report their own feelings may re-
call his name.

Brookman’s Boulder, W.A., cables that the
directors in London have collared all the unissued shares.
These were declined in Australia at £1 some months ago,
but that was when the original shares were less. Not re-
markable that they were snapped up in London at par.
when the ordinary shares were about 30s. Unless a row
is made at once the transaction will soon be forgotten,
especially if they go back to £l, which is likely enough.

Brookman’s Exploration, W.A., has gone into
liquidation. No cash div. to shareholders; consolation
instead getting one W.A. smelting share for every 12
in the B.E. Time was when these shares were over £3
each, representing about half-a-million of money. ’Twas
when they imagined Pearce had found several greater
Boulders, Lake Views, Associated, &e., in the Lake Lefroy
district. They all proved awful duffers, hence liquida-
tion.
* * * *
The Bulletin office often has a lot of ore in
sight, and though the practice of sending it in has never
been encouraged, this paper receives too many requests
for gratuitous assays and opinions as to value and treat-
ment of samples submitted. Arrangements have now
been made for testing, by assay and otherwise, samples of
ore, provided the sum of £1 is sent with each to cover
cost. Assays alone do not serve the miner’s purpose,
hence certificates supplied to Bulletin correspondents
will not only give a statement of metallic contents —
owners will be advised by an experienced, practical metal-
lurgist as to the best methods of treating the ore in bulk,
whether by wet or dry crushing and amalgamation, smelt-
ing, cyaniding, chlorinating, etc. Parcels should not be
less than lib. nor more than 101 b.

Q. mining notes: —Most transactions coppery.
Mt. Garnets and Linsdales soaring, due principally to
“ whispered ” information. The mines meanwhile are dis-
creetly silent. Various nebulous syndicates await Q.
coming session for just one small railway. Gympie paid

Mutooroo, which sprang to the front during the
French syndicate’s copper-boom and very soon paid
£12,000 in divs. on a trifling expenditure, has been “ in-
spected for a powerful syndicate ” ; beyond that nothing
has been done there lately, in which respect it is almost
unique among those S.A. copper-claims that have ever
paid divs. But the Australian Smelting (Dry Creek) Co.
is the owner ; and that canny crowd knows something
safer just now than working a copper claim —selling it to
somebody who can show the purchase-money. The mine
is just over the border from Broken Hill, and the country
round about has been “ wolfed” lately.
•*• * •
Most places that show green stains on their
rocks are being invaded now by men with napping-
hammers in their hands and the copper-boom in their
mouths. The north of S, Australia (that province owes
more to copper than do any of the others) is being simply
over-run. All the abandoned shows have been re-taken ;
and everywhere such remarkably fine reports are coming
that the men who threw up the mines some years ago
stand condemned as the most amazing idiots still at
liberty. Quite as remarkable is the number of “ power-
ful syndicates ” that exist. Every other “ show ”is either
under offer to “ a powerful syndicate,” or is just going to
be.
* * * *
S.A. is right in the middle of a copper-boom,
and news also comes along that telluride ore has been
discovered at Leigh Creek, hitherto notorious as the scene
of S.A.’s only coal-mine. Adelaide is the native home of
the wild-cat promoter, and the market is being flooded
with copper co’s., and gold claims are being pegged-out
for miles around Leigh Creek.
• • * * v
Melb. Age vigorously warns the Vic. publio
against the numerous dredging schemes now being mar-
keted, and says it would be madness to plunge into them
without obtaining evidence of value, which, in most cases,
might be got with small outlay by prospecting with
bores. Were such tests made, the Age remarks, more
than half the schemes would die a righteous death.
Heaven knows, it is easy enough to prospect alluvial flats
before dredging them.

* • » *
The South Tharsis Copper Co., Mt. Lvell,
have lately entered into a contract with the Austral Otis
Engineering Co., South Melbourne, to supply a complete
crushing and concentrating plant for treatment of their
ores, which are mostly low grade. The plant is to be a
very complete one and comprises compound steam-engine
and boiler of 100 horse-power, 3 rock-breakers, 2 sets Roger
rolls, 8 4-compartment eccentric jiggs, 4 improved buddies
and Otis circular vanner, with the necessary trommels,
&c., for sizing and classifying the ore. The plant
has a capacity of 100 tons per day, with provision
for 200 tons. The Otis Co. are also supplying a self-acting
tramway from the mine to the mill; also, a pumping plant
to supply the mill with water from the dam.

To get a better monthly average than Bendigo
has had this last six months we have to go back as far as
1883. The total for the six months was 109,8640 z. And,
curiously, divs. were £lo9,B6o—as near £1 an ounce as
you can get it. Calls amounted to only £65,342, which is
a deal below the average ; and, in a place where there is
still much prospecting to be done, that isn’t necessarily a
good sign. Far and away the boss producer was the
veteran Garden Gully, whose 10,6870 z. gave £28,390 pro-
fit. M‘Duff’s spurt was even better from a shareholder’s
point of view—£lB,lso from 52830 z. The miners’ end of
the purse was thus pretty small.

Mildura, the Vic. irrigation settlement on the
Murray, once almost given up as a hopeless case, this
season sends out 850 tons of raisins, 25 tons of currants,
50 tons of sultanas and other dried grapes, 120 tons of
apricots, 225 tons of canned fruits, 30,000 gallons of wine,
1000 gallons of oil, besides other fruits—figs, almonds, &c.
And about 40,000 cases of oranges and 20,000 cases of
lemons are now being shipped. The curse of Protection
once more.

[?]
Premier Reid last week made a voyage of
discovery through the N.S.W. Govt, charitable
institutions at Parramatta, all of which are ad-
ministered by the Chief Secretary. The terrible
condition of the overcrowded George-street asylum
has been pointed out to Sunday Brunker every
week for the last three years. About 18 months
ago the old man inspected the place himself, but
nothing has been done. The Dept, lives in
daily dread of what might happen to the con-
gested three decker building with its 1100 helpless
inmates in case of fire. The Premier must
have been shocked and startled by his visit, for
the next day the Colonial Architect was at Rook-
wood selecting a site for new pavilions, and in the
afternoon the timber was on the spot. The new
accommodation will provide for 500 men, but the
relief will be only temporary—the steady monthly
increase in the asylum numbers will soon account
for all the new space. The Govt, will some day
have to face the absolute necessity of concen-
trating all the benevolent institutions on some
modern system. This scheme of reorganisation
has been hung up for years, and the Ministry Has
turned over the responsibility to the Public
Service Board, which seems to be quite unable to
appoint a Director of Charities.
* * * V
N.S.W. Charities Dept, has some curious
little ways. There are hundreds of persons who
receive small allowances paid monthly by P.O.
orders. The Director of Charities sends a list to
the accountant of the Colonial Secretary’s office
who sends it on to the Post-Office which, at its
leisure, makes out the orders and returns them to
the Col. Sec.’s office, which at last forwards them
to the intended recipients. Thus the money,
instead of coming to hand on the Ist of the month,

* * ♦ *
The worst feature of the altogether dis-
graceful O Callaghan baby scandal was seen in
Saturday week’s Age. Among the prominent items
of “Town Talk” appeared one stating that one
Lynch, a retired farmer, had acknowledged him-
self as the much-sought father, and would appear
at next adjourned meeting of the inquest. He
never did so, although two meetings took place
before the inquest was closed. This is a clear ease
of drawing a red-herring over the scent. The
Abgus, in a leading article, had declared that the
real father’s name “ was known to a thousand and
one people, and, that being so, it was necessary
to give some needy and willing recipient a good
lumping douceur to take the onus on himself and
so to ease the public mind—or bamboozle it. The
little game was played successfully so far as those
are concerned who read with innocent faith what-
ever may be printed. To others “not in the
know ” the question yet remains in doubt, but en«
quiry will perhaps clear it up.

for the hire of cabs to convey barristers to Gov.

absolutely refused cab-fare.
» » • •
Australian newspapers are never tired of
jibing at the American press, and charging all
sorts of corruption at the American newspaper-
man. Of late political motives have run certain
Australian dailies into very peculiar positions,
and in Melbourne recently personal likes and dis-
likes for persons in forty-third-rate offices have
led the dailies into most unworthy situations.
Out of regard for persons in high places, barrister
Gregory’s case in the Law Courts was never men-
tioned, and the other day an individual who hap-
pens to belong to the Customs detective depart-
ment was able to keep all mention of a case, which
his wife had against him, out of the papers, not-
withstanding the fact that it was good “copy.”
It is not suggested that these points are worked by
means of bribes, but however they are worked
they don’t reflect much credit on the great Aus-
tralian dailies that throw so much mud at the
press of other countries.

A monster charity-fete was organised in
Perth, and, after six months’ labour, the mountain
brought forth the sum of .£lOO odd for distribu-
tion amongst the charities. The balance-sheet
gives the total receipts at ,£768, of which ,£695
accrued from the sale of tickets. On the other
side of the ledger, management and salaries
absorbed <£l26 ; art-union, sports, and concerts,
£270 ; and advertising, ,£264 ; the credit-balance
being .£6O odd. The organiser, to show his
bnna-fides, donated his own salary, at the rate of
£2 per week, to which he was entitled during his
period of management. At the wind-up meeting,
the promoter bewailed the lack of assistance he
had received from the public. In almost every
instance, he said, the one question he had met
with on the part of those whose assistance he
sought was : “ Where do I come in ? ” —an ex-
pression he had never heard in any other part of
Australia.

In Wellington (M.L.) both the O.E. and
R.C. clergy are canvassing for subscriptions for
cathedrals. Staples’ Brewery Co. diplomatically
gave £5OO to each. Meanwhile, the country clergy
complain that they want less cathedral and more
salary. The M.L. bush-parson gets a very small
stipend —which is generally two years overdue.

Brisbane Courier the other day had a
column report about the case of a man whose
crime seemed to be lack of work. The proceed-
ings lasted two hours ; there was an adjournment,
and the man must eventually be discharged for
want of evidence. Yet in salaries and expenses
the investigation will cost £3O, whereas a railway-
pass and a ill-note would have sent the man out
into the Biljim country, where he would have had
a chance to earn what he ate. Meanwhile the
Gatton, Oxley, and Woolongabba horrors are on
the shelf. And yet the Cockier, over such a
miserable business as the above-mentioned, has
the front—or the fawnsomeness—to eulogise the
“ energy and efficiency ” of the police.
* * * *
Clerical ideas of delicacy and tact often
run on queer lines. Present writer lately heard
an Anglican sky-pilot discourse on—what do you
think ?—the laugh of Sarai, wife of Abraham !
The erudite reader will remember that aforesaid
lady laughed scornfully when the angel told her
husband that she, a woman of advanced age,
should bear a child. And this sacerdotal ass dis-
coursed for 30 minutes on this subject to a mixed
audience.
* * • *
“ A.E.C.” suggests a plan to prevent rail-
way seat-jumping:—
Any passenger should, on application, receive with his
ticket a label bearing name of station to which he is
destined, and to which the seat is consequently “ re-
served.” For these labels, attachments should be fitted
beneath the rack to a number corresponding with the
number of persons the carriage will properly accommo-
date, each compartment being capable of receiving one
label only, which it should be impossible for anyone but a
railway-official with a provided key to remove. Each
label should receive a distinctive mark corresponding
with a similar mark on the passenger’s ticket—say dat e
of issue, with a number. The label to become invalid
when the station named thereon is reached or passed, and
only then replaceable by a fresh one. Of course, a new
by-law would be needed entitling passengers to duly-
placarded seats throughout their journeys.

• * * •
Norfolk Island affairs are ripening for an-
other Parliamentary blast. The best thing for
the island and N.S.W. will be to thresh the whole
subject out from start to finish. The annexation
of the island by N.S.W. was secretly effected by
ex-Gov. Hampden, with some vague knowledge on
the part of Premier Reid. Neither N.S. W. people
nor Parliament knew of the transaction till it was
completed. It is to be feared that evils and the
suspicion of evil will continue to grow until the
island’s affairs are administered in open day.
Wouldn’t Maoriland take over the troublesome
and wholly unprofitable little island ? M.L. is
more accustomed to dealing with islands and such
like kittle cattle.
* * * *
“S.S.”: Be woman’s appreciation of the
hairy man and “ Puyyaka’s ” opinions in a recent
Bulletin. If admiration for the hairy male is
merely an indication of female reversion to the
monkey type there must be an extraordinary
number of Homopitheeal reverts in Melbourne,
where the kilted Scotch regiment is sprinkling
the town with undraped legs. I have noticed the
agitation caused amongst knots of females when
the legs appear, and it is always the woolly calf
that attracts the eye of the sex and holds it
longest. By the way, there is no attempt on the
part of the average damsel to hide her keen in-
terest in those stubbly Scotch shin-bones. Her
stare is direct and deliberate, and the fluttering
bosom finds relief in little gasps and giggles which
are very evidently far from expressing its true
emotion.
“English as she is wrote.”
Here is a fac simile of the address
on a letter posted in Peshawur
(Indo-Afghan border) and recently
delivered at Lismore, N.S. W., Aus-
tralia, to the person for whom it
was intended:
Zizarat Khan,
Sin ni Dist.,
Laz Mor,
Astriluja Oontrey.
The writer evidently intends “Syd-
ney District, Lismore, Australia
Country.” In view of the quaint-
ness of the address (to say nothing
of the incorrect, not even pho-
netic spelling) the letter’s safe
delivery on this side speaks volumes
for the administration of matters
postal both here and abroad.

Douglas paper launches out in this cordial
fashion : —“Record-breaking is Cairns’s strong
point. This time it’s babies. Three single young
ladies of that go-ahead town have lately increased

cheered the other in his patriotic sorrow by re-
marking, “Bear up, comrade ! Poland is not
yet wholly destroyed. Our wives are bearing
children— and our maidens do the same 1 ”

AMBROSIAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The queen of ever}' flower that blows,
She stood beside my garden-gate—
Just where the lusty red, red rose
Sheds attared sweets o’er his red mate.
She asked me for “one scentless bloom”
A bee-bell blue or snowdrop white—
While she exhaled such rare perfume
As held me mazy with delight.
I gave her all a man may give ;
I gave what I could not control;
I gave my life while I shall live ;
I gave her my unruly soul!
And she took up the gift I bore,
She bound it fast—to set me free—
And now my all, for evermore,
Rests in her gentle custody.
Parramatta.
LESLIE OARBS..

s IN N.S.W.

ARE TWO-THIRDS OF THE ELECTORS TO COERCE
ONE-THIRD?
(Certainly Yes, if we are to have majority-rule.
It is a sad thing, but owing to shortsighted
action of the Creator there is nothing else for it
unless things are to be settled with a club every
time. There is absolutely no alternative : either
the two-thirds must go right under and be coerced
by the one-third, or they must be ruthless and
resolute and coerce the miserable one-third. And
somehow it seems “ un-English ” not to go under
with dignity.)

“ Gumleaf ” to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin.— On June 24 you published a par. by
“ X,” purporting' to be a correction of one of mine re
Barrister John Burslem Gregory, now shut up in KeW
Lunatic Asylum under somewhat peculiar circumstances.
It is always disastrous to the truth to be a friend or an
acquaintance of one or other of the parties between
whom one is called upon to judge. “X,” it would seem,
is a friend of Mrs. Gregory, or of someone who is a friend
of that lady, and this has led him to put more inaccuracies
into every square inch of his epistle than I have ever
noted in any newspaper “ correction.” I will number off
his mis-statements. (1) “X” says : “It (my par) said
that Barrister Gregory has for years been shut up in the
asylum, which is not a fair way of stating that whereas
the confinement started only three years ago, the patient
has been more than once released on probation at his
wife’s request.” Now, I have the authority of the Master
in Lunacy (Mr. Prout Webb), for saying that since
Gregory was shut up in the asylum at his wife’s request,
in Aug., ’96, he has never been released on probation. (2)
“X”says:—“He made at least one serious attempt on
the life of Mrs. Gregory.” Well, I read Mrs. Gregory’s
affidavit, in reply to Gregory’s application for release, in
which it can hardly be doubted that she brought forward
the strongest charges she could against him ; and no
mention is made of any attempt on her life. The furthest
she goes in that direction is a bare assertion, unsupported
by medical evidence, that she had suffered “ great men-
tal terror.” (3) “X ” says : “He (Gregory) insanely sus-
pected his wife of trying to poison him.” Gregory swears
he never believed anything of the kind, and never told
anyone that he did. All he says he complained of was
preservatives in the meat and bad flour. (4) “X ” says :
“ Judge Holroyd heard the same argument (as was heard
by Judge Williams) when Gregory came before him on a
previous writ of habeas, and disposed of the application
in the same quiet way.” At this stage, will it surprise
you to learn that Gregory has only been once before the
court on a writ of habeas corpus, and that was when
Judge Williams dealt with him ! Further, Judge Holroyd
never had Gregory before him on a writ of habeas, and,
instead of disposing of Gregory “ in the same quiet way,”
Judge Holroyd granted the habeas by which Gregory was,
after many attempts, able to get before the court, on an
affidavit prepared by himself, without any assistance, in
his ward at the Asylum. (5) “X ” says :—“ The Judge
did not need any medical evidence to convince him of
Gregory’s lunacy. The poor fellow gave it away in his
argument.” I venture to say that only one who desired
to injure Gregory would have made such a statement.
From first to last—l was there all the time—Gregory’s
arguments were lucid and forcible. When Judge
Williams announced that he could not go behind
the medical evidence, Gregory immediately replied—
“ Does your Honor really mean to say that I have to
spend the rest of my life in the asylum?” (6) “X ” says:
—“ In a general sort of way, Gregory thinks everybody
wants to murder him.” Gregory was faced with this
statement in court, and said he never thought, nor did
he then think, any such thing. These are a few of the
principal inaccuracies which “ X ” has been guilty of in a
short paragraph. There is a host of minor ones, but,
then, wasn’t it Oliver Wendell Holmes who said, “ Good
feeling (especially for women) helps society to make liars
of us all. Not absolute liars, but such careless handlers
of the truth that its sharp corners get terribly rounded.”
By the way, Gregory intends to appeal against Judge
Williams’s decision. I may add that lam a mere out-
sider, absolutely disinterested, and personally acquainted
neither with Mr. or Mrs. Gregory.— Gumleaf.

On an ordinary Sunday paper the literary
staff finishes its work before midnight on
Saturday, and the compositor generally does like-
wise. It is the Monday paper, with the report of
the dreary shepherd’s sermon in it, which blows
the Sabbath into small fragments. To get that
out the literary staff and the compositors must
start to buzz about 7 p.m. on Sunday instead
of going to enjoy the balm of Parson Dryhash’s
discourse, and one great reason why the com-
positor seldom gets to heaven is the way his Sab-
bath is wrecked getting up a paper for holy
snufflers to read on Monday morning. The Sun-
day paper breaks a corner off the Sabbath, but tha
Monday one knocks that holy occasion endways.

A N.S.W. food item :
“ A.G.”: The carcases of condemned tuberculous
cattle in N.S.W. dairying districts are mostly “ fed to the
pigs ; ” so that the disease gets there all the same with
pork-eaters. Once actually heard a farmer ask the
Health-Inspector “if there was anything to prevent a
man giving his diseased cattle to his pigs ?” Was answered
that though, unfortunately, there was no law at present
to prevent him, it was an “ objectionable practice.”
Same Inspector told me that he was careful never to eat
pig in any shape. The law evidently wants amending.

cemetry.
P.S. No ladies cats or dogs kept.

* * * *
The fact that the 13th June (on which date
the long-missing s.s. Perthshire was picked up by
the Talune) is the Feast of St. Anthony, induced
some good, credulous survival from the middle
ages to set forth in a Sydney daily (with an inci-
dental sigh over “those who are unhappily out-
side the true fold ”) that when, the previous day,
he visited a Sydney convent to bring away his
daughter, he found that the nuns had been burn-
ing a lamp in front of the statue of aforesaid St.
A., for the safety of the Perthshire. And, as said
St. A. is credited with being the celestial finder of
lost things, this amiable enthusiast actually ap-
pears to suppose that these circumstances have
some connection with each other. And the world
is supposed to be moving !

• * * *
The Sydney Mission to Seamen, now run-
ning for 18 years and cackling loudly round town,
raises locally only about one-third of its income.
Two-thirds are, and have always been, sent out
from a collection-plate in London.

“C.F.”: .Reinterpreting “cables.” Naval
officer of my acquaintance, who came out to China
leaving his wife at ’Ome in an interesting condi-
tion, had previously arranged a code by means of
which she should inform him of the result.
“ Tweedledum ” to mean son and doing well ;
“tweedledee,” daughter ditto. The message ar-
rived as “dumdee,” and I diagnosed twins. Cor-
rect !

Loyal outburst in Hunter-street, Sydney—
Lady Darley wishes attention called to the fact that a
pair of stockings worn by Her Majesty the Queen, and
made especially for her, will be on view this afternoon at
the bazaar to be held in St. James’s Parish Hall, in aid of
the sisters of the church. The stockings were sent out by
one of the Queen’s dressers to a friend in Australia, and
later on fell into the hands of Mr. Pope (of Farmer and
Co.), who has kindly lent them to Lady Darley.
Reminds slightly of what Albert Smith says he
saw at a Parisian bazaar once. A group of gesti-
culative Frenchmen admiringly regardful of a to-
be-raffled-for pair of ! But then, the owner
was the most beautiful and shapely Flossie of her
day, whereas Her Good Gracious is most virtuous
—and stout. Nevertheless, a contributor, intoxi-
cated with loyalty, is moved to song :

* * • •
“ Limber ” remarks to The Bulletin that
the gratuitous use, by private persons, of N.S.W.
Permanent Artillery horses calls for Parliamen-
tary inquiry, notwithstanding that Major-Gen.
Ereneh is credited with defending the practice.
[The other side of the case is that the battery
horses seldom get enough work, and by loaning
them out they are regularly exercised, and are
thus in good condition for work when required.]
In some N.S.W. country public
schools, including those of certain big
towns, there is a growing practice of
abolishing the hour-and-half mid day
recess. The youngsters are kept at
their tasks (without recreation or
food) until 2.30 and then dismissed
for the day. Rain, dust, wind, or
threats of any of them serve as a
pretext for a practice which is already
having bad results, and is vigorously
assailed by local medical men.

Two letters (Exhibits 1 and 2)
in a certain (squared) Australian di-
vorce-suit of not long ago. Exhibit 1:
Dearest.—Oh, to live that brief half-hour
once more ! To feel your lips warm-pressed
to mine, to know you love me ! Come
back, come back, and let us live again that
swift sweet madness which possessed our
souls. You are mine, and I defy all the
world to keep you from me. As soon try
to quell the flames of a mighty fire by one
weak human breath, than kill this sore
longing of my heart. It is wrong. The
supremest of wickednesses ! But what are
right and wrong when one is tied by the
mere civil rites of the world to a marble
doll whose very touch is repulsion, and an-
other, warm, passionate and loving, is
offering her love only that she might fill your life with
overflowing happiness. I want you ; I need you ;my
whole being cries out for you. Come 1
Exhibit 2:
The wife found your letter in my pocket 1 Damn you !

» * • •
“Celt”: The most Caledonian part of
N.S.W. is on the Lower Clarence. They have
there every brand of Scot, from the rollicking
chiel after Burns’ heart—one eye on the lassies and
the other on the whusky—to the dour, rigid Coven-
anter saint and the canny mon with whom “bang
goes saxpence ! ” There are 40-year residents who
still affect braid Scawtch in their households. The
Highland folk have their own kirk, where the
Gospel is preached to them in Gaelic undefiled,
and pipers are as plentiful as pumpkins, no social
gathering being considered complete without
plenty of skirling and skipping. Holman, M.P.,
had the bad luck to strike the annual gathering of
the clans on 24th May for his anti-Bill address,
and between Federation and whisky things got
badly mixed.
* * * *
“ Holdar M.” : The flower-dodge again.
T’other morning, a little bare-footed girl called
round, with a pathetic appeal for flowers, to deck
(much sobbing) the coffin of her just-deceased
sister. The tearful request was bountifully
granted ; but, later in the day, I came upon the
same mourner, at a city corner, exposing those
identical vegetables for sale.
Estimated that 837 persons are employed
in the furniture trade of Sydney, 431 of whom are
Chinese and 406 Europeans. The late Ninian
Melville, who used to class coffin-making with
“ furniture,” lived and died in the faith that an
Act obliging Chinese to stamp their goods would
drive the yellow man out of the trade. But no-
where has such an Act had the result anticipated.
• * * *
Melb. factory-inspector Ord suggests the
imposition of a JBSO license-fee on Chinese furni-
ture-makers, with a limit to the number.
• * * «
The Novel-party and Poem-party ideas
have spread even to the bakblox—out into those
parts where the “darnce” is marred by candle-
grease, and the dingo is aggravated by the music
and goes howling across the plain, and where the
local dude has his scalp-lock anointed with suet.
Somebody in a far-back township had read of the
idea, and it was decided to try the entertainment
—everybody to represent a book or a poem the best
way possible. When it came to the push, only
one man could remember any bit of literature to
represent—the rest gave it up, and came as they
were. The exception arrived carrying a swag, a
large carpet-bag, a small trunk, a billy, and a
good-sized rock, and he represented the “White
Man’s Burden.” Nobody guessed it. Nobody
even dreamt that he represented anything. The
crowd told him he was a new kind of and
threw the things out.

“Y.A.”: Be tight-lacing (8., 18/2/’99).
Girl-missionary once expostulated with aged
Chinaman about binding his daughter’s feet.
Quoth he: “My makee tie-um foot, no can run
away ; welly good. You makee tie-um belly ;no
can chow-chow ! (eat) —welly bad ! ”
Let us sing “God swathe the Queen,”
As we fasten both our een,
In no gaze of idle mocking,
On this pledge of England’s straightness,
On this emblem of her Greatness,
On our Queen’s much-travelled stocking !
Let us chant “ God dress the Queen,”
As we think what might have been,
(At our heart are fearful knockings !)
Had V.R. been more than human,
And forgetting she was woman,
Never worn a pair of stockings !
B.E.

The obligingness of Govt. House is becoming a
bye-word. Lord Beauchamp has accepted the
post of Editor, and Lady Mary that of Editress,
to the x ress Bazaar Newspaper, and both are
taking great interest in the Fair. Ladv Mary
presides at all the meetings, and His Ex has
promised his autograph to one fair stall-holder
who will deal chiefly m signed photographs.
Altogether, it looks as if the Fair will be a highly
picturesque structure. Mr. Backhouse was show-
mg at last meeting a model of the different stalls
and it seems that everyone will sell in an olde
Englyshe habitation, with an open front like a
butcher s shop.
Lord Beauchamp went into the backblocks last
week, and saw the raw bushie in all his glory.
Ihe tour was meant to be a peaceable holiday
jaunt. But, lo ! at every railway-station His Ex.
fell into the arms of Mayors and Mayoresses burst-
uwf out of new kid gloves ; and was showered with
addresses that commenced tremulously, “ Y’hex-
cellentcy and suet, we ’aving come ere to bid yous
welcome, and continued in a corresponding
strain of wild beauty not unmixed with pathos
(Dan. O Connor, Epistle xlii.), until the picked
child with the longest curls and best frock stepped
forward from behind a bouquet.

The bush went to great trouble in the way of
welcomes to His Ex. It gave balls for him,
erected arches, burnt “ kirrosene ” with lavishness,
and followed him through Moree with a procession
three-quarters of a mile long. H.E. was rather
weary when he went up there, and a long course
of country banquets, and the wild, unadulterated
stare of the populations, played havoc with his
jaded nerves at times. He was obliged altogether
to forego one ball. Ah, how simple that looks on
Eiper ! But the tragedy of the Real defies words.
very bush-girl in the district had the hope of
dancing in the same Lancers. For days before-
band the sweet bush hair was curling. And then
all the brilliancy and turkeys and wonderful home-
made sweets and Sydney-draper dresses, dwindled
a '™y into one little sad, quadrille-sized party,
which hid its aching heart under assumed smiles
and only kept up out of a sense of duty to the
turkeys. The rest of the Moree ball stayed at
home and thought his Ex.’s committee over in
solitude.
But still there might have been tears if Lord
Beauchamp had been there. Long ago the late
Frince Eddie opened a country ball in South Aus-
tralia with a Miss Jones. And Miss Jones lost
the programme on which the name of England’s
possible future king was written. Mr. Jones
offered enormous rewards for that programme.
Fifty pounds ; a hundred pounds ! He even ran
into four figures. But the programme was never
found. And Muss Jones never, never married.
She seemed to have lost her hold on life.
. Speaking of Royalty, the Queen of England
isn t like the Queen of Spain. Sydney has had
our Queen’s stockings to look at for several days.
Fine, black silk—that have actually been on. At
the Church Bazaar, where they were exhibited, a
lady was heard telling her husband “The Queen’s
stockings will go through a wedding-ring.”
With her legs in them?” he replied, incredu-
lously. Had he never seen the Queen, even in a
picture ?
You will be sorry, Myee, to hear that some
artists are hurt because I lately mentioned them
m the same line with j'oekeys, etc. I haven’t
heard how the jockeys take it yet.
And, do you know, that little friendly evening
at .Govt. House hasn’t done so very much towards
bridging over the breach between the two
societies. While the beautiful At Home was in
full swing a member of one society was smitten by
a large, dull fact. He looked round, and saw
that the whole of the V.R. party was talking to
members of the rival sect. He frowned a dark
sombrero frown, and waited till the private sec.
should have finished with one small, inoffensive
member. Then he strode up to that small mem-
ber, and whispered in his ear, “You’ll hear more
of this.” “Of what?” “Your society is mono-
polising the vice-regal attention in the most bare-
faced way.” “And to what do you attribute
that? ’ said the small man, neatly. But it isn’t
that sort of neatness that heals breaches.
It was after I wrote to you last week about Amy
Castles’ one frock that she appeared in another.
At her big, bursting matinee concert she wore a
little purple cloth dress, with a white satin sailor-
collar, and no hat. Everyone who had seen her
before observed how thin and white she looked.
It seems that her nervousness, never seen by
the audience, attacks her in the green-room,
and causes fits of sickness. All her good
fortune notwithstanding, it makes one’s heart
ache to see that little white, wan face staring
steadily into the thousands of faces before her.
Also, her throat is troubling her. A Sydney
throat-specialist has warned her that she runs
great danger in continuing to sing according to her
present system of voice-production. So her offer
to sing in aid of a Sydney Conservatorium of
Music is not likely to come to anything. She was
continuously sick all that last week inSydney. At
the matinee, big, kind Madame Wiegand had to
take her out on to the Town Hall verandah, and
keep her in the open air between songs.
Did you see any of the correspondence over that
Conservatorium ? Dan O’Connor flourished like a
blackthorn. “The rare and fragile casket which
enshrines the glory of highly-sensitive female
genius is so costly, so perishably precious, that it
demands from the chivalry of man eve generous
and kindly protection—every shield, sympa-
thy, every comfort, every encouragement.” Just
remember that, if ever there’s an Irish Meta-
phrastic evening. And Dr. M‘Carthy recipro-
cated feelingly, and, with his admiration of Dan
and Amy, worked in the fact that his own little
Maud would give concerts in Sydney in a few
months. (She returns before the end of the year,
after some brilliant American touring.)
Re encouragement, remember our own “Charlie”
Huenerbein, that delicate, sympathetic accom-
panist and favorite of 20 years. And be sure to
go to his benefit concert. It comes off at the
Y.M.C.A. Hall this Thursday, July 13th, and
deserves solid patronage from Sydney people.
The Boys’ Brigade ball has been fixed for Tues-
day, July 18, Paddington Town Hall. Mrs. Rey-
nolds and Mrs. Playfair are the secs.; Mrs. J.
Dean, treasurer.
“Novel teas” are getting up to variations.
There was one at the Salon last week that called
itself a Metaphrastic evening, but it wasn’t really
dangerous. It only intensified things by depicting
quotations from Shakespeare instead of novels.
One bold Metaphrast wore a little mirror on her
chest. She was “ I met a fool.” Unguessed! I
don’t think I shall go to any of this variety. No
one knoweth how she will be met there. And into
the parties that are not Metaphrastic now enter
things not generally invited to social entertain-
ments—worms, flies, pills, clumps of hair, that
adorn a woman and point a tale with ferocious ■
realism.
Mrs. Caro and her pretty daughters had a merry
tea and novel-afternoon last Monday, at “Mel-
rose.” Fifty feminine guessers worked hard for
hours, but so ’cute is your practised novelist-
knower that only seventeen were guessed. Ethel
Turner carried a wooden soldier in a toy cradle.
Miss Dolly Hordern showed a boy on an iceberg,
shrieking “Oh!” Think them out for yourself.
The names of the novels that flew round! At
last, Providence has found a way of wiping the
dust off the books that nobody reads. Or, should
I say, of avenging itself on the books that can’t
show reason for their existence ?
“ Why are not the University women in the van
of every movement ?” is often asked. The reason
is that they are women first, and University
women second. About a year ago they had their
chance. One of their number, who was away in
America, sent them word of a “novel-tea” idea,
and urged them to try it. One only of her fellows
in Australia tried to get the suggestion carried
out, but met with no success. No one would see
anything striking or interesting in the proposed
plan. Now that it has become a society craze, the
University women are getting as much “ worked-
up as anyone else, but the honor and glory that
belong to pioneers are lost to them for ever.
The successor to the novel-party is the song-
party, where a sober-minded man enters a room
with a doll hanging down his back (“The Girl I
Left Behind Me ”), or carries a basin of sugar in
either hand (“Sweet and Low”). But the very
latest N.Y. fad is the “supper-evening.” The
entertainment here consists in cooking the supper
for the party. Each young man has a dish and a
young lady assigned to him. The girls are pro-
vided with aprons, told where to find the in-
gredients' in the different pantries, and all repair
to the kitchen. The men have to make the dishes,
being instructed by their partners. A few couples
are told off to set the table, and, when all is ready
they eat the supper—and that is the party. It is
said by those who “have been there ” to be quite
the most fascinating form of entertainment ever
invented. Perhaps !
Sydney people are so fond of “A Trip to China-
town ” that Saturday’s big, affectionate first night
was only to be expected. Harry Conor, in his old
part of Welland Strong, sang “The Bowery ” as
tellingly as ever. And Carrie Moore showed that
she has learnt a good deal, and acquired a good
deal more, since Xmas panto. She got all the
flowers on Saturday, and played poor little Sadie
Macdonald’s part of Flirt in a bright, smart, but
perhaps slightly business-like way. I mean Carrie
gives you the impression that Carrie Moore is
working for all she knows. When that impression
fades, or is veiled behind a little extra spontaneity
there stands out a first-rate little artist, and a
rapidly rising one, too. She looked a fetching
Flirt in a little grey-over-pink frock, with grey
shoes and stockings, and white cap and apron.
Miss Gillette sported the prettiest frock on the
stage. This tall, dark ’Murkan dresses well
consistently, and wears her clothes with a
dashing air and superior pose of head.
The Chinatown frock showed a polonaise of a
soft blue silk, slashed up all round in
huge tabs over filmy skirt of white tucked chiffon.
Miss Aliens Crater has her voice, after a nasty
throat operation. Both she and Miss Louise
Hepner have suffered from bad throats in Sydney.
As Miss Crater is one of those very light-haired
blondes who can wear black to perfection, she sets
off her frock of black lace with transparent yoke
and sleeves of lightly-tucked chiffon worn with a
plumy black hat. In some plays it is kind to veil
the stage as much as possible,' but it isn’t neces-
sary in “ Chinatown.” So badly was the curtain
worked on Saturday that the gods got nothing but
aggravating glimpses of feet, with an occasional
ankle thrown in, in spite of entreaties to “yup
with the ryag.”
I see that Walter Bentley, who will shortly
play Silver King for the Firm, has taken to
literature. He has been shedding on Sydney
gently-tinted reminiscences of “my friend, his
Grace of Beaufort, dear old Charlie Harcourt,
Irving, Sothern, and all my othah dear friends.”
A little less Grace and gush, and Mr. Bentley
would be quite a fair writer, a much-weeded im-
provement upon the usual forsaken-garden style
of actors driving quill - pens through terrible
tangles of flowers and vegetable-matter,
Theatre-coats are all the rage in London. These,
to be worn during the play—are cut to three-
quarter lengths, chiefly in broche satin. The
sleeves are plain, without the slightest pleat at
the shoulders, the back is a sacque shape, and the
straight fronts are cut to show a full fall of lace.
Theatre-capes are running wild. They reach be-
yond the knees; and a point at the back, shawl
fashion, with rounded fronts, is the toniest model.
Inside the cape hundreds of little frills of val. lace
cover the lining, from the top of the high collar to
the cloak’s edge. Brocade, with deep lace flounces,
or covered half-way with guipure under bands of
fur, is considerably swagger.
Outspoken Dr. Zillman, of Sydney Unitarian
Church “The first king of Israel fell through

So at last the striding intellects of this city have
been favored with a new lending library. For a
long time the danger of taking books from the
Macquarie-street building has been repressing
people in search of Carlyle, Scott, and the other
books that go out so regularly from lending
libraries. With Carlyle and Scott, borrowers
took home all sorts of horrors from the old room,
which reeked poisonously in summer, and had
quite a noticeably evil effect on the health of its
officials. The new rooms are three storeys up
in the Victoria Markets. A few hundred clean
works of fiction have been added, and when the
George-street tram starts the library will be con-
veniently located.
. From Mrs. G. E. Wilson I have received an
interesting little prospectus of the newly-formed
Child-Study Association of Australia. The object
of this association is to help parents and teachers
to a more careful and scientific study of children.
Says the circular: “Parents wonder how it is
that their young people are delicate, anaemic, or
under-sized. Teachers complain of the difficulty
of teaching them, and the lack of attention and
concentration in their pupils. The only way is to
have every child that enters a school properly
tested and measured so that means may be taken
from the first to counteract weaknesses and bad
tendencies. By this it is hoped that much of the
crime and insanity that lies as a possibility in the
future can be avoided and eliminated.” A colossal
hope 1 How to fulfil it is being explained in the
first instance at a series of lectures.
In Sydney is to be found one woman on whom a
title has had no ill effects whatever. A while
ago she was receiving guests in large numbers—
mostly people who said “ Haw ! ’’—and when the
affair was well on, and she had shaken 500 hands
or so, she was seen looking round over her
shoulder. “Wherever can it be?” a friend
heard her murmuring. “Dear me, I wonder
where it’s got to?” She whispered into her
friend’s ear: “I couldn’t keep them on ; so tired
with standing so much,” and plunged out with
one foot in a wild semi-circle. “Both gone”
she whispered pathetically.” The friend took
a long walk round the long silk train. At
the far end, three yards away from the owner,
sticking out from the train’s last flounces, she
found them. Poke them in with your parasol,”
whispered the titled one. They were pushed in»
till her foot touched them. Then she kicked them
well under her, and stood over them smilingly for
the rest of the afternoon. (I nearly forgot to say
they were her boots.)
Have you read Mrs. Potter’s weird soul-
unveihng to Raymond Blatherskite in London
Daily News ? She even out-Potters Potter. “I
make-up according to the method of Romney,
Vandyke, Reynolds, Gainsborough. ... I touch
my mouth and nostrils with vermilion, and so give
a gem-like quality to my face. . . . And the hair
—it is wonderful what you can do with a swish of
hair to pull yourself into a period.” A discovery
already made by Porphyra’s lover, who pulled
Porphyra to a full-stop with one long yellow string
of hair “three times her little throat around.”
. -^l rs - Potter gets on to the old question of dress-
ing to suit emotions. “I would play all emotional
scenes in white ; a serious scene, or a tragic, or a
sinister, in black ; whilst red, of course, is defiant,
reckless, wicked.” But how are our new colors to
be represented? In the Potter’s own words—
Costumes should not only harmonise with the
occupation of the wearer, but they should be the
expression of the century and the age they repre-
sent ” This age is representing itself in other
shades. Red has turned deep-pink-magenta, and
taken on the incipient virtue of all good women
who confess to magenta dresses. To be either
defiant, reckless, wicked in the red of the day is
impossible. And black? Black serious ? Why,
black is the great gateway that leads to all that is
wonderful and gleaming and glittering in this
world. Steel, and silver sequins, and jet paillettes,
and crusted nets, have lifted black up into the
foremost place of fashion’s giddiest raiment. There
is nothing under Heaven so young and snappy and
enviable as a modern black frock.
What to-day wants is a new system of staga-
xrocking that will be for ever uniting the old, old
stage emotions to the shifting colors of the hour.
What a keen, sweet sense of propriety when the
wicked rich woman in white with a blue sash stabs
her poor, good young lady seamstress rival in
Cuban-red cashmere that would have lasted her all
the summer had she only lived ! Or when Sir
Henry s dear, sweet little bride, in green and blue
shepherd s-check shirt, red tie, and pastel blue
cloth skirt, has wicked lies told her of her hus-
band s perfidy by Mrs. Olde Catte, in soft, pale,
clinging, un-smoked grey, destitute of trimmings,
and a white grebe muff and boa.

On Wednesday (sth July) Lady Rrassey,
gowned in purple, opened the Victoria Hospital
(run by women for women) with a golden key.
Ihe ceremony was witnessed, amongst others, by
medicine-folk galore, male and female. The yard,
where most of the company snuffled and sneezed
through the preliminary speechifying, had been
a a i ln a I rhemuafcid 0 7 state of puddle and slush.
At last the doors opened, and the damp-footed
brigade swarmed into the hospital, a place so dainty
as to rob the future of half its terrors. Snow-
white linen, cream-white iron bedsteads, such a
pet of an operating-room, rounded germ-baffling
corners, sterilised-water taps, and white-enamel
surgical table. Beholders for the moment entered
into the feelings of Rhoda Broughton’s hypochon-
driacal heroine, who nightly prayed that ‘she
might be counted worthy of operation.’
Nurses at the new hospital are pretty girls,
their snowy caps and aprons showing up against a
background of green-cotton frock. Among all
women-workers,, ministering angels probably take
the cake for beauty. A girl who knows she’s nice
to look at naturally chooses a sphere where ail her
fellow-laborers don’t wear pel tieoats. Extra-nice
belles m want of pin-money adopt a nurse’s
vocation cause one meets such nice men.’
