A Word in Confidence.
It is a pleasant duty to give honor, where justly
due, but in this age we reserve the right to exer-
cise our discretion as to what is worthy of praise.
We must not, however, in our caution fail to
discriminate. Must not condemn the Navy be-
cause one officer blunders ; must not ridicule or
underestimate the value of our entire fire depart-
ment because one fireman forgets his discipline;
must not mock at religion because one member
goes wrong ; must not sneer at the entire medical
profession because one physician makes a mistake,
nor decry all proprietary medicines because some
lack merit.
While it is the duty of everyone to avoid the
ardent pretender, one should give credit when
merited. You may rely with confidence upon the
following statement from a prominent Australian :
Dr. Robert Shaw, L.D.S., M. 8., D.A., Rock-
hampton, writes: “I have much pleasure in
stating that I prescribed Warner’s Safe Cure very
frequently whilst acting as medical officer on the
Cairns-Herberton Railway Contract, and obtained
more satisfactory results from it than any formulae
of my own prescribing.—ROßEßT SHAW,
L.D.S., M. 8., D.A.”

bubbles. T. J. Byrnes hasn’t been dead a year,
and young Queenslanders already ask “ Who was
he?” Parkes is a piece of legendary lore, and

Some people wondered why orator Deakin
gave away the chance of making a splashy and
showy speech in his best colored manner, in
moving his motion favoring Scripture-lessons in
Vic. State schools. The reason is just this:
Deakin’s chance of moving the resolution came at
6 p.m. He had to dine with the President of the
Council —who was giving a swagger official ban-
quet —at 6.30, and he had to dress for the dinner.
It was a choice between an oration and cold soup
with no oysters. With the odor of turtle under
his nose, Alfred could not rise to the ocoasion, so
he only spoke for a quarter of an hour.

near Gospel. But the hero of the yarn is not the

predecessor, the irascible Fitzgerald, once a noted
Tory editor, who got promoted for his many jour-
nalistic misdeeds. The present Auditor-General
is, however, following in the footsteps of the
departed Fitz. Warburton, though Auditor-
General, is said to own an Opposition newspaper,
and to slate the Government therein in a way
quite surprising for a dispassionate publio servant.
But this may be only calumny.

“ Scribo The late Archibald Michie will
always be associated in my mind with a case of,
say, Smith v. Brown. M. was for plaintiff, at
some such a place as Gladstone’s Nathalia, but
the scandal was a lot beyond Dancing. Brown
was the local doctor, and the Smiths and Browns
who had been very thick, suddenly broke off the
intimacy. At a gentleman’s party some one
asked Brown the reason. “Oh,” says he “it’s a
case of Potiphar’s wife.” This buzzed and reached
Bishop Perry’s ears, and Rev. Smith proceeded
against Brown, civilly, for alleged assault on Mrs.
Smith, a woman so handsome that there was quite
a sensation when she lifted her veil in court.
Verdict for defendant, despite a terrific speech by
Michie. “Gentlemen this viper—willing to
wound, and yet afraid to strike ! ” —Brown sitting
opposite him. Michie said afterwards, though,
that Smith was a fool.
King, the notorious negro convict of Pen-
tridge prison, is now quite mad, says a recently-
discharged prisoner, and when brought into the
open after his last stretch of solitary, he was no
longer black but absolutely green, like a beer-
bottle. Since his conviction in ’92 King has done
over 500 days “solitary!” and in one year he
spent no less than 136 days on bread and water !! !
Like Weechureh, who ended his attempts to
“obtain justice” on the gallows of Melb. gaol,
King suffers from being a bush-lawyer, and all his
attacks upon warders and fellow-prisoners are
based upon the idea of “having his case made
public.” King is a madman, and when he kills a
warder that warder’s blood will be upon the head
of the brutal and senseless System. Why, even
the Westralian penal commission recommends the
abolition of “ solitary ” and punitive low diet.
On the morrow of N.S.W. Federation-
vote, a small Bendigo co-operative co. ran a little
social as an ad. for itself. There was no sign of
the banquet which had been announced, but the
chairman proposed “Success to the Federal Move-
ment,” coupled with the name of Dr. Quick, and
suggested that, while the local Father of Federa-
tion was speaking, the ladies should go out and
get the coffee ready. Said F. of F., who had been
previously bursting with jubilation at the N.S.W.
referendum-result, was completely staggered by
the chairman’s remarks, and sarcastically observed,
to commence with, that, in view of the important
refreshment-suggestion, he would be very brief.
The cold and scanty succeeding observations of a
profoundly-disgusted Doctor were made to the
accompaniment of the clinking cups and saucers
in the adjoining room.
Old James Prendergast, who lately retired
from the Maoriland Chief-Justiceship after sitting
in a high {dace for 24 years, was never a bril-
liant lawyer, but he had the merit of being a
plain veteran who loathed needless “frill.” When
he filled the occasional billet of Acting-Governor
he knocked so much of the cocked-hat dignity out
of the business that people said he was unfit for
the position. With more force and grit he would
have made a good administrator for a place like
Papua, for he would have been quite content to
live on Irish stew, and go about in any clothes
that were handy, and to sleep on a stretcher
with the statutes of the country in a gin-
case under the bed. Prendergast was
Acting-Governor before Hercules Robin- /
son took charge, and his reception of
Hercules the Great was a grief unto the
snobocracy of Wellington. The Gov.
arrived in a worn-out battle-ship, and
was greeted by the Mayor and stomach
—that is to say, corporation, the princi-
pal sin-shifters, the consuls, the brass /
band, the National Anthem, the police,
military, vegetarians, stray dogs, lost
cats, the corporation dust-cart, the steam-
roller, the volunteers, Friendly Societies,
Rechabites, pickpockets, larrikins, and
general chaos. And when this great out-
pouring streamed up to Government House
to deposit His Ex. at his own door it found
old-man Prendergast waiting there to swear
in Robinson and hand over the authority.
There was a kitchen table on the verandah.
On it were a Bible and Prendergast’s rather
faded top - hat, and nothing more. Prender-
gast, in that aged black suit which always had a
greeny look when the sun fell on it, stood behind
the table, with his long hair blowing in the wind.
When Hercules arrived the Acting - Governor
shook one of his hands and shoved the Bible into
the other, told him to kiss the book, and then
seized the tall hat and departed on foot. It was
the plainest and most sudden swearing-in that
the province ever saw.
John Harvey, who lately died at Salis-
bury, S.A., is almost, if not quite, the only
“ gentleman of color ” who has had a pp.ee in an
Australian Parliament. His negro 1. iood pme
from his grandfather ; but all the coloi fwa ''
the skin. Mr. Harvey, who during 60 years
a splendid reputation in the province as ong of its’
most useful men, was a co-worker with Torrens,
whose Lands Titles Act is now nearly world-
famous. Harvey was probably Torrens’ earliest

education, John Harvey did remarkab’e things.
In the early sixties, when Deniehy
edited the R.C. weekly at Melbourne, the pre-
sent writer and he used to meet daily at the
Rising Sun, an old-fashioned pub. in Little
Bourke-street. Deniehy hated Melbourne. Said
it was started on greed and would ever remain
a glutton. “ A bloated thing, sir, oozing yel-
low fat.” And, of course, Sydney was all the
other. “ Kissed by the gods, how can she help
her beauty !” he would rave. “Nothing can
make her ugly—but gold,” he went on. “ Gold !
what a curse it is—the true devil. How it
spoils everything, and man first and most. And
the time is coming (this brandy is good, eh?)
when all art, and literature and every
effort of mind, will bear the hideous brand,
and nothing will be of value of heart or /
intellect that has not its market-price. It
has been so in the past, and will be so
again. And then, reaction ? Why, of
course. The stomach, long time patient,
rejects at last. But, meanwhile, what ruin
to high thoughts, pure ideas, beautiful
imagination, and daring energies has
been wrought by the market-price ! ” And
so he would talk—flying from one thought
to another, yet somehow keeping the chain
linked coherently. Of one thing he was assured
—that in the future art-life of Australia, Syd-
ney would take the initiative, and maintain
the lead. “If we should ever have art
and literature of our own, my boy, they will
be born and will flourish in the cradle built by
Nature for beauty and its expression.” Then
he would call for another brandy and “ score it
up.”
Obit., John Campbell Dibbs. The three
brothers ex-Premier George, T.A., and the de-
ceased, made one of the oldest and most promi-
nent family groups in Sydney. They were born
in Sydney, lived their lives there, and in their
several walks maintained high personal reputa*
tions. J.C.D. was less widely known than the
others, but where known commanded as high
esteem.
The late George Gordon had one of his
early speeches translated into the Australian ver-
nacular soon after his arrival in Melb. In those
days, the genial artist never swore, and one day,
when a stage-hand started whistling and nearly
sent him mad, he turned to the “ splodger ” «ith
—“ Go down, do, and tell that man to stop that
noise.” The white-clad assistant clambered down-
stairs, made towards the offending siffieur, and
there rang up to the scenic artist the words, “ Mr.
Gordon says to stop that blanky row, you blanky
.” The artist nearly fainted at the time, but
the effect of the free translation was so wonderful
that he began to consider the advisableness of
learning the new language.

The father of Dean Cowper, of Sydney,
whose 90th birthday was celebrated on Monday
week, left England for Sydney in 1808, with the
rank of Assistant-Chaplain. The post was a
Govt, one at the time, and the salary attached to
it was £240. Some interesting correspondence is
extant, showing how income-tax even at that date
mdft the neaceful squirm. That Rev. Cowper
obtaita, Fan advance of £l2O to provide equip-
ment, etc., but the money did not go as far as ex-
pected, and he had to apply for additional indul-
gence ; the main point he insisted upon was that
he was charged £l2 as income-tax on the £l2O (a
half-year’s salary), and that civilised necessaries
for his foreign home were all dearer than he had
estimated. He obtained an additional advance,
with income-tax again deducted, and arrived in
Sydney in time to take over the historic St.
Phillip’s. He has been dead over 40 years.
WIT—FROM THE PIT.
“A man so weak that he could hardly walk
arrived at Spencer-street station, Melb., from the
country , and, having some difficulty in discovering
the whereabouts of his luggage, asked one of the
porters where he might find it. The porter, after
surveying the man's appearance, replied: ‘You
had better try the morgue! ’” — ARGUS.
A Mr. and Mrs. Adams were immigrants
to S. Australia in ’52 in a ship with nearly 800
others. The old lady died the other day, leaving
eight children, 54 grandchildren, and (37 great-
grandchildren—l29 altogether. Nearly a sixth of
the big boatload, and all well inside 50 years.
The peroration of Gov. Brassey’s address
to Melb. Chamber of Commerce, on the Hempire,
appears in full in the Times and other London
papers. Uncle Tom paid for the cabling out of
his own pocket, being evidently proud of that
gilded little unoriginal bit about the beat of Eng-
land’s morning drum rolling round the world.
The cable, however, at press-rates, cost him close
on ,£3O. But he loses no chance of keeping him-
self before the ’Ome public, and £3O for a space on
the cable-page of every principal daily in England
■ —for the cable went through Reuter —was worth
the money.
During N.S.W. Federal campaign one
Clarence-river sugar-grower was in a quandary.
He was a good Catholic and loved the Cardinal,
but he also worshipped the local member —Mac-
farlane—with a devotion inspired by much in-
formation about the land laws and promises in re
a road. The Cardinal was for the Bill and
Macfarlane was against it, and the latter argued
that Federation would kill the sugar-industry.
The wavering elector thus explained his dilemma
to sympathetic friends, “You see, the Kiardinal
and Macfarlane are agin aich other, an’ Oi dunno
which to go wid. Maybe the Kiardinal doesn’t un-
therstand the sugar industhry as well as Mac-
farlane does, but then, you see, Macfarlane, bein’
a Scotchman, can’t have the grace o’ God like the
Kiardinal, although he is a number,” On polling-
day he was still undecided as to which of his guides
he would follow, and perhaps he voted yes-no.

Aboriginalities.
Pathetic wail from a poor way back (N.S.
Wales) editor in his issue of July 1:
POVERTY-STRICKEN.
This issue has been brought out under extreme diffi-
culty. In the first place, we had no paper to print it on ;
in the next, we had no money to get any with. In this
extremity, a kind friend in town came to our assistance
and wired for a supply—and so we came out, rather late,
perhaps, but still alive. For the past fortnight, we and
our family have been living principally on bread and
water. And as the baker now threatens to stop our
oredit, unless he sees the color of our coin, we are in a
pretty fix. Unfortunately, we can’t show it to him. It’s
m our subscribers’ pockets. We are not ashamed
to proclaim our poverty. We think, rather, that
the shame should lie with our subscribers who
fail to pay, and thus reduce us to this condition.
We have several business letters to write ; but for the
last fortnight we have not received sufficient to pay for
the necessar3’ stamps. This is no exaggeration, but, alas,
’tis stubborn fact. It is a hard battle—a battle that
could be made so easy if our subscribers would only pay.
Each debt is so little, too, and yet, in the aggregate they
come to over £6O. Think of it 1 £5O in our books, and
not enough cash in our pockets to buy a loaf of bread or
a two-penny stamp 1 But we will struggle on, and trust
to your better feelings to help us yet. May God prompt
you to understand that we cannot live and work on air
and water.
* * * *
The following has just; been received by
His Excellency the Herr Governor of the Aus-
tralian Colony, Melbourne, from Feldstrasse,
Hamburg:—
“ To free the land which your Excellency rules from
the rabbit plague is the wish of the present writer. I
offer to submit to your Excellency an unfailing remedy,
which you can cause to he subjected to proof, and I rely
upon your Excellency’s honor to see that I receive my
share of the published reward. The value of our remedy
has received practical proof in South America, in a cer-
tain part of which there was a plague of rats. In ten
days all the rats in the district were dead, Let a strong
rabbit be oaught in a trap in such a manner that he is
not injured. Then let a pendant portion of Spanish
pepper be placed in his posteriors. The posteriors must
be sewn together in order that the animal may retain
the pepper, whereby great caution must be exercised
so that the animal may not scratch and bite people.
Then let the animal run loose in the prescribed district.
In a few weeks’ time no living rabbit will be found in the
district. The animal will be seized with a kind of mad
fury, and, by biting, will communicate the same madness
to other animals, and so on. ' Your Excellency may rely
upon it that this is no jest on my part, but the strict
truth.”
A Tasmanian squatter’s two harum-
scarum sons used occasionally to visit Launces-
ton for a spree, and on the last night of their
stay they would order an elaborate dinner and
finish up by smashing all the breakables on the
table —of course to be charged for in the bill.
On one occasion, however, they had omitted
the “smash” act, and, seeing the landlord
making chalk-marks on the wall they asked
him what they meant. “Oh,”said Boniface,
“ you haven’t broken anything and I was
merely chalking it up.” “Frank, my boy,”
said one brother, “we must keep up the 1
family reputation ! ” —and the usual perform-
ance followed. On another occasion,
the then Governor of Tasmania hav-
ing offended the father, the sons
resolved to get even with him. Ac-
cordingly His Ex. was invited to
stay a few days at the house, and,
suspecting nothing, accepted. For
the usual weekly meet of the
hounds he was provided with a
most unmanageable “ puller,” and
as soon as the hounds were off and
His Ex.’s mount was giving him a
handful to hold, the two youths
ranged up, one on each side of him,
and started bumping him to and fro like
a shuttlecock, till at last they sent him
clean over. The Governor went back
to the house, packed-up, and left. He
always afterwards referred to the twain
as the “two blood-thirsty young can-
nibals from the Midlands.” And now
both are steady-going old stager*—
one being J.P. and ex-M.L.A.

“Snell”: My friend from Swift’s Creek
tells me of meeting a small but interesting party
on the track to Bruthen. The party consisted of
a tired-looking man, a woeful and weary woman,
and an emaoiated horse. The man was towing
the horse, and the unhappy woman was carrying
the belongings of the three, a decent donkey-load.
The usual wayside compliments were exchanged,
and the solitary individual felt free to inquire:
“Good Lord! why don’t you make the horse
hump that swag o’ truck?” The man lifted
a dull eye, and languidly replied : “Oh, tha’s all
right, mate. It’s his turn next.”

A Gippsland grazier, who has been through
the mill in Western N.S.W., had a pet crow
running about his house for a long time. The
bird was caught with a broken wing when young;
the setting of the wing was an experiment that
interested the man, and its success resulted in the
evil bird becoming a domestic pet. One after-
noon, the grazier went out on to the verandah,
where his baby was sleeping in a perambulator,
and found the crow perched on the child’s fore-
head, with a peculiarly crow-like air of gluttony
about it, picking softly at the lid of the young-
ster’s eye, just as he had seen its obscene kind
pecking at an exhausted sheep, to induce the
animal to open the eye that the ghastly scooping-

“ Hugh ” : The township storekeeper had
run out of tobacco, and we were miserable. So
when a swaggie came along the line, and asked me
for a smoke, I curtly told him that not one of orr
gang had had a smoke that day. “Oh! is that
it?’’said swaggie. “Well, you seem a decent
feller, an’ blowed I’ll see yer stuck fer a smoke. ”
Down came the swag, and out of a dirty little
bag he produced 20 or 30 small “ junks ” of
tobacco of different brands and qualities. “D’ye
like it dark or light?” he asked. “Dark, eh?
Well, ’ere’s a bit o’ Derby. Yer see, I asks every
man I meets for a bit ov terbacker, so’s I won’t
never run short.”
* * * *
When it was known in England that the
Maori chief, Patuone, had saved some British
sailors from being killed and eaten, King William
IV. sent out to him as a present, per warship
Buffalo, a suit of armour. The gift was formally
handed over to Patuone at Waiheke in Hauraki
Gulf, and when the case was opened there was
found with the armour a suit of green baize to be
worn underneath. Patuone was big, and the
armor small, but he endured the fitting on with
gravity, and then stalked solemnly to and fro in
all the Maori’s unconsciousness of the ludicrous.
Then he tried to squat down Maori fashion, but
the iron joints pinched him so that he arose with
marvellous rapidity. “ What are these things
for, anyhow?” he asked, and was told that the
suit was to protect him against his enemies. “My
enemies !” he said. “If my enemies caught me
in those things I should be dead in no time ! ”
Then he took the armor off, threw the pieces in a
heap, and said: “ Tell your king I am grateful
for this,” and he walked off with the green baize
suit.

Some light thrown on a verj^siiff 1 rJ,®’ -
ing yarn :
Dear Bulletin.— Re your comment on my bull-frog and
postage-stamp yarn (B. 20/5/’99). In order to convince
you that a postage-stamp will stick on a frog I would post
you one but that I fear froggy would not take kindly to
the postmarking process. Also as I don’t know to what
extent the Bulletin staff may have been filling-’em-up-
again, the sudden advent of the reptile might have
disastrous consequences ; so I must just ask you to take
my word that a frog’s skin —except when coming out of
water—is wot damp, but is as sticky as glue and makes a
postage-stamp stick like a porous plaster. In the case of
my frog he had to traverse three miles of basaltic country
in which a mosquito couldn’t have got a drink. Which
is, doubtless, why he lost no time in getting back.

“ J.A.H.” : Re jew-fish nests (B. 17/6/’99).
Have watched these fish in Mehi River, Moree,
for hours swimming round this circle, and
then going in centre and remaining perfectly still
for fully quarter of an hour. Any other fish ap-
proaching would start them off again, but they
never seemed to be far away from the nests.
* • * *
An Albury-district man had a curious ex-
perience lately. Cut his finger badly and was
riding to doctor to get it dressed when his horse
fell, broke its neck, and lay on rider, who was
found by a belated traveller, insensible, with con-
cussed brain. Had he not been so discovered he
would have been frozen to death, as that night
was the coldest the district had experienced for
years.

The late Judge Redmond
Barry was once presiding at the
trial of an aboriginal at Portland
(Vic.), for the murder of a gin.
His associate at the time had
been “keeping it up ” the night
before, and when the court
opened was hardly fit to begin
proceedings. So his Honor,
with his well-known punctilious
manner and strictly - correct
speech, took the presentment
and began : “ Prisoner at the
bar ! The Crown presents you,
under the name of Billy, which
is the only name by which you
are known, and charges you that
you did, on the 13th day of
March last, unlawfully kill and
slay one aboriginal woman,
known as Mary ; how say you,
guilty or ? ” Then the
prisoner stopped the graceful
flow suddenly and broke his
Honor all up by yelling out:
“Oh ! stop-uin all that blanky
yabber I Me kill -um plenty
gin ! ”

“ A Shields ” to The Bulletin from
.Rochester (Vio.): —
You have got hold of the prevailing, but erroneous,
notion re the naming of Bendigo (Vic.). It was this way :
Myers, a squatter, who died lately at St. Kilda, and after
whom Myers’ Flats were named, started, in ’49, to take up
land on Picaninny Creek. He began at the Big Hill and
went 20 miles along the creek. The station went five
miles S.E. of the present city, and about three miles
N.E. towards Ravenswood, and the Bendigo of to-day
was entirely within the run. Myers was called away
during the marking-out process, leaving things to his
overseer, and when inspecting the boundary marks sub-
sequently he asked the overseer, “ How did you manage
about the creek? ” (which is remarkably crooked). “ Oh,
from bend to bend I go ! ” was the reply, with which
Myers was so taken that he called the run “ Bendigo.”
The diggings were then known as the Loddon goldfields.
This version doesn’t sound very convincing

“J.W.M.”: An impressive group stood
round the booking-office for the Great Unknown.
The contents of the bundle about to be lowered
had died of a glorious burst. Occasion
—the opening of the new pub. Ma-
loney read the burial - service from
the most appropriate part of the
Mining-regulations, and there wasn’t
a dry eye in the crowd. “ Fill up ! ”
said Maloney by way of Amen, and
the dry dust was shovelled in. “Now,
boys, we’ll go an’ wet the first stiff-

“ 0.P.” : After many years, I
have returned to the bush to be sur-
prised. I had expected them all to be ex-
tinct long ago, but they are here still—the
emu and the kangaroo, the dingo, and the
bandy “ chap ” who rides a well-made horse
and saddle, and peels at the nose all the
year round. Also the gaunt, stumpy-fin-
gered carrier of the characterless face and
characterful character (an outrage on all
physiognomy), with the strong joke, and
short waistcoat that arches at the back.
All these and many of the other old asso-
ciations are here, to recall the time I
used to shyly approach the man with the
braces and dingo’s-tail beard, and take his
horses to water for the sake of the ride.
After all, the world is mostly a treadmill.

“Pelican”: Butcher birds, grey, and
black-and-white I take to be merely different
varieties of same species. Have kept both kinds
for years from fledgelings, and have never known
the grey turn black-and-white. The young of the
black-and-white show those colors decidedly be-
fore 12 months old. Further, the eggs of the
grey variety are always brown ; those of the black-
and-white, three parts green or blue with brown
on large end. I write now of Darling Downs
(Q.) district. Apropos, can anyone explain why
the eggs of the ordinary magpie (know all about
the kurrawong and other varieties, so am not
making mistake) should be of different colors (at
least three) in different nests ?—l, streaky red ; 2,
streaky blue or green ; 3, grey, with brown spots
at blunt end. Birds laying these different eggs
do not show the slightest external difference.

Westralia is trying the experiment of
letting cats loose to prey on the rabbits, and 300
were recently bought in Adelaide for the purpose.
The same plan was once tried in S.A., but the
animals fraternised and kittens and bunnies
frisked about together.
* * * *
Harry Stockdale writes, re recent an-
nouncement that a Mr. Cox had taken up 340 sq.
miles of country west of Charlotte Springs, Cen-
tralia, which he intended stocking with goats :
Novel as this notion may appear, there is a pronounced
business side to the “ goat,” especially as regards use of
areas that might otherwise be classed as waste lands. In
warm climates the animal breeds twice a year, 2 to 4
kids at a birth ; and, as it will thrive on vegetable sub-
stances actually poisonous to cattle and sheep, areas in
Australia quite useless for latter animals are well suited
to goats. Not easy to understand, though, why Mr. Oox
should have gone so far afield ; and, as skins from cold
districts (like our Monaro and Tumut) are considered
more valuable, the Centralian locality referred to must
have some special advantages—probably the twice-yearly
breeding—to compensate for climatic and distance draw-
backs.

One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of calcium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, the recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
of crystalline carbide of calcium. Following up
this success, the English Acetylene Company have
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney —who own the Willson patents for
Australasia—are getting ready to give the Aus-
tralian pirates a surprise. This co. have now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36 Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*

Re the abandoned prosecution of Bishop
Tugwell for criminal libel in connection with his
statements about the West African liquor-traffic,
“ A.D.H.” writes :
I was in the Niger Coast Protectorate in ’9B, and will
just give you an idea of what the white man in those
parts does drink. If he hasn’t got fever, “ jumps,”
“softly softly,” or other of the many curses of the
“ white man’s grave,” he gets up about 5, and, after a
cup of coffee, bit of toast and a sardine or two, he works
till between 10 and 11, when the cocktail is in requisition ;
and while waiting for breakfast there will, perhaps, be a
round of drinks for each member of a mess of perhaps
half-a-dozen. At breakfast, beer is drunk, and after a
smoke there is a general turn-in for a sleep in pyjamas.
About 2 p.m., work is resumed till 4 or 6, and before the 7
o’clock dinnerthereareabundant cocktails, gin and bitters,
&c. Dinner (fish, goat, tinned soups and vegetables) is
washed down with plenty of beer, claret, port and sherry,
finishing off with Benedictine, Chartreuse, or peach and
cherry brandy. Then, after coffee, come poker, nap and
billiards, enlivened with much whisky and soda. Asa
matter of fact, Bishop Tugwell, in attributing 75 per cent,
of the local white mortality to hard drinking, is probably
rather under the mark than over it.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Ed. Bulletin.— Re butcher-bird. “H. J.
McC. ” (B. 24/6/’99) is in error when he says the
name arises from the bird’s “ murderous propensi-
ties. Give b.b. a frog, and you’ll soon understand
whence the name. In a jiffy he’ll have all the
joints dangling from bars of cage, which will look
like miniature butcher's shop. He will break
meat up, and hang pieces round in the same way.
The b.b., in fact, is the most interesting bird we
have. Have never been without one. —Yarrum.

“ Alex. M.” : Re origin of term “ butcher
bird (“ 11. J. Mac.,” 8., 24/6/’99), the designation
was more precisely due to an extraordinary habit
of the British b.-b. (Lanius eoccubitor) which
“ hangs up ” portions of its prey till wanted.
Have seen a thorn-bush stuck all over with
“joints ” of small birds, frogs, mice, &c., the effect
being ludicrously butcher’s-shoppy.
* * * *
“Perth”: Re aboriginal mourning cus-
toms, the Wide Bay (Q.) gins wear in their hair
little plumes of emu feathers bound to a wooden
skewer, and called “ poowong.” They don’t seem
to value them much, however. Have got a score
of ’em for “ gibit bacca.”

“G.S.” : Apparently not generally known
that tin-kettling dates back to pagan times, when
bells were rung and other noises made in front of
a house before the newly-married pair entered, in
order to drive away evil spirits. In Australia,
t.k. properly means disapproval of the match, as
in the case of a May-and-December union. We
went one better than tin-kettling once in our little
Q. town, on the occasion of an old man marrying
a young girl promised to a more youthful, but less
wealthy, suitor. Latter with some friends spilt, on
the nuptial night, a few buckets of blood around
the house and then drove in a cow. Effect upon
“ moo ” of smell of bovine blood was that in 10
minutes the old man’s garden was full of a bellow-
ing mob of cattle from anywhere within hearing.
The concert lasted till daylight, and saved a lot of
tin-hammering.

“ D.G.S. ” : Re “ Hun’s ” query (8.,
24/6/’99). There are several species of sea-horse,
but I presume the one meant is the small, dark-
colored sort ( Hippocampus Novce - Hollandice).
Often caught in prawn-nets in Port Jackson, and
may also be found at low tide attached, by its
prehensile tail, to the fronds of Laminaria and
other large sea-weeds.

“ Yar ” : Never saw marsupial scalps ten-
dered in payment for grog until I came to Illa-
warra, where the practice is getting common.
Recently saw two dairymen proffer four hare-
scalps as payment for drink. Explained that
“dorgshad got at ’em and eaten a ear off,” but
were promptly fired out. Further down coast
have seen black shags’ heads tendered —and
accepted.

“ F.A.R.” : Came across a lot of
blacks once on a lower Richmond
station camped in scrub, and dis-
mounted to see what they were up
to. Told me they were going to
“ make” two young men, and I stopped
to see the performance. The old men
with torches of tea-tree bark first
singed all hair off the neophytes, who
were then forcibly held down on their
backs, and a roll of blankets put un-
der each so as to expand the chest.
Then the oldest man of all knelt down,
and, holding a sharp piece of flint be-
tween finger and thumb so as not to go
too deep, cut into the subject’s chests
exactly as a butcher “ scores ” a leg
of pork. The wounds were then
rubbed with ashes, and the youths held
over a slow fire with blankets round
them so as to keep as much smoke as
possible about the cuts. No food was
to be given for three days afterwards.
The “ridging” of the cicatrices is due
to the use of the ashes.

bell Town (Tas.), the annual show was
always followed by a dinner, at which
the local magnates used to get glori-
ously “full”; and once, while the
feast was in progress, the diners heard
a noise familiar to them—that of a
horse being ridden up the stairs. Said
one guest, turning to his neighbor, a
big sheep-breeder: “ I’ll bet ,£lO,
R , it's one of your sons that’s
doing it.” Taken—and won; it was
neither of the sons, whose wily parent
had seen them to bed, dead-drunk,

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
Stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

If Love Were All!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If Love were all, I should not hesitate ;
But I should lay at thy beloved feet
My crown of life, secure to find my fate
And all that makes my own self incomplete,
If Love were all.
If Love were all, how simple it would be
To sit apart from storm, and strife, and stir,
In some old garden, rare with rosemary,
And redolent of quaintest lavender—
If Love were all.
But Life has many voices, dear, and we
Hear, dearest, Duty’s clarion, and pass
Out from our fairyland of fantasy,
Out from the old-world loneliness — Alas!
Love is not all!
DORA WILCOX.

The Commercial World (London) writes re-
garding the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society
“ We always confidently anticipate a good year from this
vigorously-conducted Mutual Society. The circumstance,
therefore, that the figures for 1897 show no falling away
from the steady and substantial progress which is inse-
parably connected with the Society’s past career occasions
us no surprise. As a matter of fact, the Society earns its
success by deserving it, and, that much being conceded,
the best possible has been said for the continuance of the
large measure of publio support which it has hitherto
fallen to its fortunate lot to receive.” Pleasant reading.*

Copies of Yols. 11., 111. and IY. of Horwitz’s
Victorian Statutes have just arrived at thjS office from
the publishers, Messrs. Sands & M'Dougal, Melbourne.
And they are such valuable books that The Bulletin is
heartily sorry that it has not been able to find any trace
of the arrival of the First Vol. They represent an
enormous amount of dry reading, brain sweat, and
general hard work on the part of the compiler, Mr. Louis
Horwitz, barrister and solicitor, of Melbourne. The three
vols. cover the Statutes from Auction Sales to Livery and
Agistment. In the Third Vol. the various Evidence Acts
are collected, and the splendid Factories’ Act, which
provides for the minimum wage, is collated, to clear
away any little intricacies owing to the bad old habit of
the lawyers of making every instrument a wilderness of
language and repetitions. Also, other democratic enact-
ments such as the Provident Societies and Trades
Union Acts, and laws dealing with matters relating to
the health of the people, and Insolvency Acts (relating to
the freedom of the people), and the new rules appertain-
ing thereto. The Fourth Vol. deals with the Instruments
Acts, and acts relating to the sale and exchange of land
for local purposes are, for the first time, published with
the principal enactment. Also an analysis of the Official
Hand-book to the Land Act 1898. The work was pro-
duced entirely in Victoria, being printed in Melbourne
and Hamilton, and is a useful and valued additi»_ to the
office library.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The dread of Jones may probably account for
Dr. Grace's absence in the second and third Tests. It
needs young- and well-strung nerves to face the lightning
wheatfielder ; and when it is remembered that, early in
the season, Jonah nearly shifted one of the doctor’s ears,
it is not surprising the latter prefers pavilion to playing-
area. It will be interesting to see if he turns up for the
Gloucester match.
• • • »
Melb. Age has overcome its squeamishness of
three years ago, and now, with the Argus, hangs out its
cricket-returns—carefully marked “ copyright on Sun-
day morning, whereat the unco-guid driving up Collins-
street to church, shake their heads sadly after they have
casually “ piped off” the state of the poll.
* * * *
At Northern cricket-match fast bowler goes on
with the captain behind the sticks. First ball hits wicket-
keeper on lingers and goes to boundary, scoring 4 byes.
Second ball hits wicket-keeper on jaw ; also 4 byes.
Third ball wicket-keeper receives on his chest, whereon
he takes off pads and goes out to leg. Bowler looks at
him and sweetly inquires “ if he is going to have a bloom-
ing backstop.” “Yes, Mr. Jones, yes, certainly—if
there’s another fool in the team.”
* * * *
I’arpot and Harry Rose are the sole survivors of
the aboriginal cricket-team which Wills took over from
Victoria to play Sydney in 1867. The team played a
three-days’ match with the Albert Club, and was badly
beaten. Tarpot is now on a station near Alburv, and
Rose is in Hamilton (Vic.) district. Both were referred
to in print recently as being members of the aboriginal
team which visited England in ’6B, but neither Tail ot
nor Rose went to England.
* * * *
Little wonder red-headed Fitzsimmons went
down so badly in the world’s-cbampionship fight. An
American paper published an interview (beforehand) with
Jeffries, sen. Latter, a preacher in California, said the
Lord would be with his son throughout the battle.
* • » *
May nob be generally known that dethroned
pug. Fitzsimmons got divorced in America from his Aus-
tralian wife, one Julian being “ co.” Later, Bob hitched
on to Julian’s sister, and Julian took Mrs. Fitz. No. 1
unto his bosom 1 Julian has been the M.L. pug.’s man-
ager for years, and still held that position at latest dates.
This peculiar domestic complication travelled with vaude-
ville shows throughout the States during two years
without a split. Everyone concerned was apparently
satisfied, but the ex. and present Mrs. F.’s gazed upou
the valiant, freckled-faced, bald-pated, auburn-haired
Robert’s “turn” from opposite boxes. Their feelings,
until they got used to it, would have been worth an inch
or two of even Bulletin space.
* * • •
The downfall of no Australian athlete was ever
regretted less than that of ex-world’s champion pug.,
Fitzsimmons, and reasons are not far to seek. First
neglect of his aged parents, as exposed to the world by
Tiuiaru (M.L.) charity-board; second—discarding, in
America, of his Australian wife, who had stuck to him
through days of want and toil ; third—turning renegade
to his country and becoming a naturalised Yankee (for
which, let it be said, the Yanks condemned him more
than any others) ; and, fourth —drawing the color-line as
an excuse for not meeting his whilom tutor and certain
master, Peter Jackson. “ I’m an American citizen now,’*
quoth he, “aud I cannot meet a negro.” Cowardly
subterfuge 1
* * * »
“Kid” M‘Coy, the Yankee pug., who claims
to have fought over 1000 battles, is son of a Hoosier store-
keeper so pious that he refused to keep tobacco in stock.
Mao., jun., got out at an early age; was waiter, barber,
billiard-marker in turn, and finally took to the ring.
The presence of Martin (“Mum”) Quirk in
Sydney turns on the tap of reminiscence. Twenty-five
years back few could use their mauleys more cleverly
than “Muni.” A couple of his brothers were shining
lights among the R.C. clergy of the day. He toured
N.S.W. West for a good while with the giant “Stone-
wall ” Jackson, giving exhibitions. (Martin says Jackson
is now following the profitable and congenial occupation
of killing wild pigs.) Writer remembers “Stonewall”
coming to Sydney in the early 70’s with great blare and
flourish, to fight Jem Mace. Jackson was tried out at
L. Foley’s rooms in Bathurst or William Street (forget
which), his vis-d-vis being a big fellow, a heralded world-
beater from out back. The go lasted exactly 3min., when
“ Stonewall,” amidst some fierce work, stopped, and,
with much dramatic effect, addressed the crowd of ring-
siders : “Gentlemen, I’ve broke me arm and lost me
chance of winning the championship of the world ! ” A
few minutes later Foley took-on Brewer and settled him
inside 12min. Thus did two much-boomed prospective
subjugators of the world’s pugs bite dust in a quarter of
an hour. Close on 40 years since Jackson—then known
as the “ Parramatta Butcher” —was licked by “Yellow
Jimmy ” in a slashing go at Rooty Hill (N.S. W.).
» • * *
A Sydney boxing-olub intimates, per notice
posted in the office, that “ Gentlemen desirous of settling
disputes without recourse to law, and in the only manly
fashion, may do so at this club by giving the management
three hours’ notice, when gloves and all necessary atten-
tion will be provided.” To be hoped that this new insti-
tution will meet with the appreciation it richly merits,
for, though the manner of settling smacks of the primeval,
the cost amounts to no more than the effort, and the
result is more convincing than the best decision the Privy
Council ever delivered.
* * » *
Erstwhile world’s-champion “ feather” boxer
Billy Murphy is coming home to Australia again. This
onoe-marvellous combination of whipcord and wire,
though probably about a third-rater now, would easily go
through all his division here at present—unless, perhaps,
Melb. Jack M’Gowan might prove a lion in the M.L.
tailor-pug’s path.
• * » •
Chief Justice Madden confided to a Melb. inter-
viewer recently that he learned to use his hands first
from Harry Sallars, the darkey who ran a Melb. tobacco
shop in the sixties, and who afterwards hawked fish round
Melb. A few years ago Sallars was living in Carlton and,
during a temporary absence, the rumor went out that
Harry had “ thrown seven.” When he returned and
heard the news, he prepared a huge placard “ The dead is
riz,’’ and fixed it in his window. Harry Sallars, who
fought old Tom Curran to a standstill, was at latest in
the Benevolent Asylum at North Melb. What an end
ior a champion pug and the teacher of an Excellency 1
* * * *
Concerning the prevailing crookedness of “ box-
ing " encounters. Reg. Pell (son, by the way, of the late
Prof. Pell), who referees most of the big fixtures in the
West, does what he can to keep things straight by putting
the door-takings in his pocket before the “star” item
comes off, with the announcement that it all goes back to
the people if he is not satisfied. He followed this course
at the recent meeting for the Australian heavy-weight
championship.
* • * *
Glenloth’s Melbourne Cup should have about
knocked out the supposition that opals are unlucky.
Glenloth’s rider wore an opal pin in the race, and the
winner of first prize in “Tatt.’s” sweep was resident on
an opal-field. But neither Glenloth nor Ronda, who was
second to him, ever won a race afterwards —in fact, Ronda
has never won a race at all. Ronda, by the way, is to be
put into work again.
• * * •
Artful M.L. bookies, denied admission to race-
enclosures, now disguise elaborately, and befool the
guardians of the sacred precincts. Their ruse generally
suoceeds, but when whispering questions excite suspioion
they pass out quickly.
* * * *
The Australian starting-barrier is to get a
chance at last, it having been decided, at the instance of
Earl Durham, to use it in races for two-year-olds. And
none too soon. After Australian Survivor’s run in the
City and Suburban, he was fancied in Kempton Park
Jubilee Stakes, for which he started at 14 to 1, New-
haven’s price being 6to 1. For three-quarters of an hour
the horses were messed about at the post, and, when a
straggling start was made, Survivor got all the worst of
it, whilst Newhaven’s chance was nil after dancing about
for 46 minutes with 9st. lOlbs. on his baok. The crack
jockeys naturally object to the barrier, as part of their
skill consists in beating the starter, and the barrier gives
all riders an equal chance. And again—although perish
the thought—if an unfair starter should ever be dropped
across, the barrier gives him less show of favoritism. It
is an open secret that an old-time Australian wielder of
the red fla 2 was > un< *er the rose, an extensive owner, and
used to put some of the biggest commissions on the mar-
ket, but of course he never gave his horses any advantage

T T Hrassey curse again. A Vic. racer, named
Lor d Brassey, which distinguished itself on the flat by
running absolutely last every time it started, was put
over hurdles with no better success. At a recent meeting
at Moonee Valley the noble prad started in a steeplechase,
an d after painfully crawling over a couple of jumps,
about a furlong behind the field, stopped and would try
no more. The jockey, with more pluck than sense, kept
charging at the fence till the others had finished and
weighed in. He would probably have been trying vet,
but the clerk of the course galloped over and ordered
him in.

A recently-returned Australian jockey gives it
as his opinion that Tod Sloan is “ no horseman,” and that
the reason of the Yankee’s success is that he gets, in
oommon with the other fashionable jockeys, the pick of
the mounts. Not bad, considering that Sloan first came
into prominence by winning on horses that other good
jockeys had failed on.
* * * •
The owner of a Melb. cordial-factory reoently
started a racing string, and christened his horses Soda-
water, Lemonade, and Sarsaparilla. Like all soft stuff,
they had no “ go,” and the string is now disbanded.
* * * *
Isn’t it time that Pro Bono Publico and Co.
began to rise in the influential organs and tell the public
that the average cycle-meeting is a degrading spectacle ?
This is what happened when the democracy tired of foot-
ball. And things are ripe for it in re wheel-racing. The
cycle lion is sicker to-day than his football brother was
when the asses began to grow arrogant. Yet the recent
Melb. run of calamities didn’t evoke one moral reflection
from Pro Bono.

“ D.M.”: This clip from Ballina Pilot of long
ago throws an interesting sidelight on the nature of
the old-time Big Scrub sports-meetings :—“ One of the
most amusing items of the Tvvowharves Queen’s Birthday
sports was climbing the pig for a greasy pole, which was
won, after a desperate struggle, by Old Archie’s Scotia.
A blanket would have covered the lot at the finish.
Something went wrong with Silver Arrow’s centreboard.”
• * * *
It isn’t safe to ask ex-champion runner Tom
Malone if he knows Mickey Muldoon, the jumper. At
one time Tom used to breed some very fine “ colonial ”
game-birds, and for fear of thieves had his yard sur-
rounded with a high fence. One night Tom heard a
commotion amongst the birds, and, creeping out of the
back door in pyjamas, spotted a man with a bag full
of his pets. “ Dhrop them !” said Malone ; “ and it’s no
use thrying to get away, for I’m Tom Malone the runner.”
“ Then bedad,” cried the thief, shying the bag over the
fence, “I’m Micky Muldoon the jumper,” at the same
time scaling the fence like a cat. By the time Tom
got over, the bag and man were a street ahead in a spring-
cart which had been waiting in the back lane.
• • * •
Gippsland ranges, once so abundant in game,
are being rapidly stripped thereof by the indiscriminate
slaughter which the Vic. game-laws seem powerless to
prevent. One man acknowledged recently that he had
for long been shooting 12 or 14 koalas a day 1 The lyre
bird, which, like the bear, is “ protected’* all the year
round, is also being ruthlessly shot at sight, and rumor
goes that even some of the game-inspectors amuse them-
selves with the very thing they are supposed to prevent I

POLITICAL
POINTS
If Dibbs and co. go to “ the foot of the
throne” with their anti-Federal petition, they
will leave upon the toe of the throne.
The Federal party in Queensland is acting
very unwisely in allowing one or two prominent
sugar-planters, men exceedingly unpopular in
their own districts, to go round orating on Feder-
ation. Every time they speak on the subject
their advocacy breeds a score of enemies to
Australian unity.
Auckland (M.L.) is generally of opinion
that the Govt, should float a loan to complete the
Auckland-Wellington railway. That is just the
trouble in this great loan-land. Every place
thinks a loan should be floated to build its par-
ticular railway and after that the country should
swear off borrowing.
On Household Suffrage S.A. Leg. Council
is believed to he exactly divided into ten for and
ten against. The remaining four members are on
the fence, and one of them remarked recently that
Tories and Liberals have been so friendly to him
lately that he believes he is the most popular
man in the country.
A Mr. Thomas, understood to be a member
of N.S.W. Parliament, and who shared in John
Haynes’ anti-Eederal campaign, is going to Wes-
tralia on Vosper’s invitation, “for the purpose of
explaining the democratic position with regard to
the Commonwealth Pill.” Mr. Thomas was given
his seat by thg miners of Alma (Broken Hill), who
in ’9B voted heavily against the Bill. In ’99, after
a long campaign by Mr. Thomas, during which
only one address was given in his electorate in
favor of the Bill, the vote was nearly 3 to 1 for
the Bill. Mr. Thomas, therefore, goes to the
West with the blessings of Federalists as a friend
in disguise.
The Turner Government shoves the big
boulder uphill once more. Victoria’s Parliament
met the other day, and the Bills to abolish
plural voting, to enfranchise women, to regu-
late usury, and all the other democratic
measures which the House of Landlords
throws out so regularly, were formally intro-
duced once more—likewise the Old Age Pen-
sions Bill. If they are thrown out this time,
Turner ought to get out of his rut and take
a referendum, on the Assembly’s sole authority,
regarding the principal ones on the list. The re-
sult would at least enable him to state distinctly
that the Leg. Council is a liar when it says that
the country hasn’t demanded these reforms, for
the country would demand them with emphasis.
And Kingston has shown that an Assembly can
consult its own constituents by referendum on its
own authority if it feels so inclined.
Alfred Deakin’s demand for the resump-
tion of religious teaching in Victorian State
schools bumped against a very hard rock. Dr.
Maloney proposed that the heads of all the
different denominations should be asked to come
together, and tear each other’s hair in a brotherly
spirit, and decide unanimously what religion
should be taught inState schools, and then
that the Government should accept
their decision right off. And the Govt. ,
decided that it was a very good idea,
and asked Deakin how it would suit
him.
Premier Forrest, of W.A., evidently
knows about as little of the Federation Bill as the
average fat man on the roof of the omnibus. He
lately discovered, to his great surprise, that under
the Bill which he helped to frame, Westralia can’t
construct a trans-continental line through S.A.
without the latter State’s permission.
Ex - Mayor Tucker, who pushed King
O’Malley out of S.A. Assembly, has lost his seat
because of his injudicious offer to divide his <£2oo
a year Parliamentary pay among the charities of
the electorate. Once more, therefore, he and
O’Malley will enter upon a Titanic struggle in the
electorate very appropriately named Encounter
Bay.
Queensland Government begins to realise
that if Brisbane is ever to be a great port it will
be necessary to divert the river, and out off that
awkward horror of navigation, Kangaroo Point.
Yet even at this late hour it would probably be
cheaper to make a new capital on the coast down
by Southport, under the shelter of Stradbroke
Island. -
The Australian Customs Union, now near-
ing under Federation, was petitioned for by
N.S.W. Leg. Council in 1845. A Bill embodying
the object of the petition was introduced by Far]
Grey in the British Parliament in 1846, but
lapsed. It has thus taken over 50 years for Aus-
tralia to legislatively obtain what Australia as a
whole always desired.
Henry Copeland, who asserts his determi-
nation to reintrodiice a Sunday hotel-opening Bill
into N.S.W. Parliament, will be well advised if
he hunts his requirements to one demand —the
opening of such pubs, as like for two hours on
Sundays. A straight-out vote on that will, there
is good ground for saying, give a majority in favor.
If, however, Copeland complicate the issue, as has
been his wont, his motion will be shelved or lost.
“If Sydney has not a better claim than
Melbourne (for the Federal capital), what better
claim could any of our inland districts advance ? ”
yells Sydney Telegraph. None at all. Neither
Melbourne nor Sydney nor any other place has
any claim to the capital. It should be planted
where it seems best for all Australia, but the idea
of any town or city or district having any sem-
blance, of a claim, apart from the interests of Aus-
tralia in general, is absurd.
London Daily Chronicle has a special
correspondent in Australia who has been sending
articles on the Federal movement to his editor.
In one of these articles the correspondent reported
a visit to Sydney to obtain opinions from leading
men in “the cock-pit of the Federal fight.” He
came, saw, and interviewed two leading states-
men, and seriously sent home both their opinions,
which were seriously published. One was Ed-
mund Barton, and the other was—Affleck !!!!
True bill.
An anomaly in S.A. politics is the fact
that the Tory crowd opposing Household Suffrage
in S.A. Leg. Council is led by John Gordon, who
was a member of the Kingston Govt, until June.
’96, and would probably have been sponsor for the
measure had not the hospital dispute brought
about his resignation from the Ministry. Gordon
is a strong Ministerialist, but as regards Suffrage-
reform announces his willingness to stump S.A.
against it should Kingston’s threat to dissolve be
acted upon.
That great bogus anti-Federal petition
which the Sydney Parochialists presented to Par-
liament some time ago contained 40,000 signa-
tures, and the Parish Pump party s wore they were
all those of genuine electors. Yet when the
CRITICISM, OR PLAIN STOUSH.
PERSISTENT POET (who has been interviewing the
editor) : “ An' ye call this criticism! ”
referendum came the party, in the region where
that petition was got up, couldn’t muster 35,000
votes. Petitions are all much alike. The auto-
graphs largely represent the Flossie, and the small
boy, and the person suffering from drink, and the
passing dog ; also the man who humps the peti-
tion round puts down two or three dozen names
at a time when business ia slack.
“ W. Cat ” writes :
G. T. Simpson, one of the best speakers in Westralian
Assembly, is a N.S. Welshman by birth. He has just
gone through the court in quickest time on record,
having been declared bankrupt, publicly examined,
wound up, and granted a discharge all within six
months. His certif. has been endorsed to the effect that
his insolvency was due to misfortune, so he can take his
seat on the assembling of Parliament this month as
though nothing had happened. Simpson has long been
in wretched health, but is on the mend, and will prove a
tower of strength to the Opposition, which will be rather
stronger this session than the Forrest crowd likes.
Holmes, member for a Fremantle constituency, has openly
renounced his Govt, support, and will sit with the
straight-out Opposition. Other rodents are taking their
tails under their forelegs preparatory to jumping over-
board. Coolgardie water-scheme will swamp the Forrest
Govt, for a certainty. The gigantic scheme is already a
laughing-stock, no foundation having yet been found for
the weir with which it, is proposed to dam the water in
the Darling ranges. Rocks ahead, and Forrest knows it.
When Premier Reid was on his Federal
tour in the N.S.W. sugar districts he agreed at
the Tweed to meet a deputation about State-mills
if his reply would be kept strictly secret. Said
reply is reported to “ have given complete satis-
faction” to the dep. ; and curiosity is rife in
adjoining districts as to what the
Premier promised ’em.
TRIUMPH.
SMALL BOY: “ Hit Billy, comean' bring your
sister — ’ere's the 'ead teacher as sick as a dorg.”
G. H. Reid held N.S.W. Treasury port-
folio from 30th August, 1894, till June 30, 1899 —
four years, 10 months; the longest continuous
official grasp in N.S.W. “responsible ” history.
James Watson (4 years, 1 month) comes next.
“Pukaki” writes of the bad old days of
Provincialism in Maoriland :
In Canterbury, M.L., the provincial Govt., having
ukased that no person could take up a block of land of
less than 20 acres, proceeded to cut up a large portion of
the province into 21-acre sections alternated with blocks
of 19£, so that the holders of the former had the free
use of the latter, the 19-acre blocks not being legally
saleable. In Hawke’s Bay, where stock bad to be carried
in proportion to area held, when the inspector was ex-
pected at squatter A’s place, all the stock from the
neighboring stations would be mustered in A’s yard, and
when the inspector had arrived and duly noted the num-
ber, he would be asked inside for a “ Presbyterian.”
Generally he would remain for several Presbyterians, and,
meanwhile, the collected stock would be passed on to
squatter B’s homestead —and so on. The Provincial
Council of Hawke’s Bay once bought a big block of land
from the Maoris, and immediately sold it at an absurdly
low figure to 12 gentlemen, known locally as “ the
Twelve Apostles.” And by a curious coincidence the
Twelve Apostles were the Provincial Council.
Brisbane Twaddlegbaph has an idea about
the recent N.S.W. referendum :
Reports in Sydney newspapers make it clear that the
victorious voting in some of the suburbs of Sydney
against the Convention Bill was due to good organisation.
Equally good work, elsewhere, must have produced at
least as heavy a-vote against the Bill as there now is re-
ported to have been for it. The reason is on the face of
the facts. There were about 120,000 persons to whom
voting-papers were sent, who took no trouble about it.
Either they thought that the Government could not be
serious, would not go on with Federation—a very reason-
able supposition—or they supposed the opposition against
it good enough to obtain a great victory.
The idea that it might be the other way about—
that the 120,000 were Federalists and didn’t vote
because they felt quite sure that Federation was
safe, and that it wasn’t worth while to kill the
already-dead parochialist over'again—is quite be-
yond the range of the Geebung’s understanding.
Items from N.S.W. Federal referendum
poll: The largest vote “ for ” —King Division,
3622 ; largest “against ” —the same division, 2922 ;
the greatest majority “for” —The Murray,
2044; the greatest majority “against”—Nor-
thumberland, 916 ; the greatest number of in-
formal votes —The Murray, 30; the least
number of informals—Newcastle West and
?• Newtown (St. Peter’s Division), 1 each.
T. M. Slattery wants the Commonwealth
Houses to be called Parliament and the State
Houses Legislatures, as is the casein Canada, and
rates the Federal Bill drafters for “gross inac-
curacy,” because they failed to notice the point.
But there is no reason why Australia should go
out of its way to be like Canada. Common-
wealth Parliament and State Parliament
stand boldly contrasted in the public mind,
and that is the main thing. The constitu-
tional difference between a Legislature
and a Parliament does not apply to the
State Parliaments of Australia, for these
will still be “sovereign” in many respects.
Premier Turner’s proposal to re-enact the
Income-tax on a reduced scale —when Victoria has
still between two and three millions of deficit to
pay off, and its Old-Age Pensions to provide for,
and wants money in all directions for public
works—is a lamentable backsliding. Of course,
the situation is a difficult one. The Income-tax
Bill was merely a temporary measure, and expires
this year. The House of 48 Rich Landlords
would probably throw out the new Bill altogether
if it provided for a continuance of the Income-tax
on the present scale. It may be a question of
taking a reduced tax or none at all. But at least
it was worth while making a fight over the matter.
Jagged truth from Orange Leader :
Dr. MacLaurin is already talking about the opinions of'
“ the large minority of electors being respected' by the
Parliament.” This is all very well, but isn’t it nearly
time the majority had a show?
The new F ederal movement appears to be
fast gaining strength in Maoriland. If that
province—the third in point of population— oame
into the Commonwealth it would lend
a tremendous democratic impetus to
Federal legislation; it would prao-
tieally compel the Federal Go-
vernment to establish an Old
Age Pension system all over
the Commonwealth (the
Federal Govt, would have
to take over the M.L.
Old Age Pension busi-
ness, and it could
hardly do that without
making the system uni-
form throughout Fede-
ral territory); and it
would bring a group of
the ablest democratic
_ statesmen in the world
into the Australasian Par-
liament. Maoriland is a cold
country where the people are
really alive, and its influence would do
a great deal to counteract the mental
lassitude of the North.
The Fates are unkind to Rockhampton.
She is, at present, fighting an utterly hopeless
battle against Federation while the Bananaland
Govt, is pushing on the connection between the
Central line and Gladstone, which is to deprive
the city of sin, sweat and sorrow of its daily bread.
Sweating, according to S.A. Factories
Inspector, was never so rife in Adelaide as now.
S.A. has no minimum-wage law, and the local
Factories Act, which Premier Kingston intends
to amend, has not been a success. S.A. workers
cry out that the cheap alien hawkers, who swarm
over the country, are the main factors in reducing
wages to starvation point.
How would the Transvaal Outlanders take
it if the Paul Kruger Government offered to re-
organise its political system on a purely British
model? Kruger, with his big belltopper and the
huge grey door-mat under his chin, to be made-
hereditary monarch so that no Outlander need
hope to have a hand in the election of the Head of
the State. The First Raad, which is purely Boer,
to be made a House of hereditary Boer aristo-
cracy, with the same power of blocking innova-
tions as the British House of Lords. The Out-
lander to have a vote for the other House after
five years residence and naturalisation ; to pay the
same taxes in the meantime as anybody else;,
taxation to be adjusted so as to fall mainly on
wealth—the Outlanders have most of the wealth ;
and there to be no guarantee that one one-man
constituency won’t contain ten times as many
voters as another one-man constituency—in other
words, that the vote of the Outlander in the big
city won’t be just one-tenth the value of that of
the Boer in the small towns.
Cabled from London that the Agents-
General advise a reduction in the compensation to
be paid to the Vine-Lowles syndicate. According
to law, the three provinces (Queensland, N.S.W.,
and Yic.) which contribute to the cost of govern-
ing British Papua have to be consulted re all im-
portant acts of government. The Vine-Lowlea
syndicate engineered a huge land-steal and trad-
ing-monopoly concession without reference to
N.S. W. or Victoria —a concession which was care-
fully kept dark lest these provinces would kick,
and which was wholly illegal. The swindle was de-
feated at the last moment at great expense in
cablegrams, whereupon the defeated boodlers ask
Queensland, N.S. W., and Victoria to pay part of
the expenses they incurred in trying to defraud
Queensland, N.S.W., and Victoria out of their
governing rights in this matter. And the Agents-
General, instead of kicking these impudent and
rapacious frauds right into the North Sea, are
actually haggling about the amount to be paid
them.
Politically the people of Maoriland lead
the world. And the most democratic people on
earth are so impressed with the beauties of the
Federal Constitution, with its glittering Braddon
Spot, that a meeting at Auckland lately assem-
bled itself together and resolved :
First resolution : “ That, in the opinion of this meeting,
the Federal Constitution of Australia is the most nobte
monument of Government that human wisdom and
statesmanship have yet evolved ; just and equitable to
the confederate States, and carefully conserving the
rights and liberties of each, in all matters purely local
and this meeting expresses its entire approval of the-
same.”
Second resolution : “That, in the opinion of this meet-
ing, the time has arrived when the attention of the-
people and Parliament of Maoriland should be earnestly
directed to this great question. That Maoriland has-
everything to gain politically, commercially, and socially
by casting in her lot and becoming one with the federated’
States, thus consummating an Australasian Federation ;
that to delay is fraught with imminent peril, there being
great danger of a Confederated Parliament, with Maori-
land left out, imposing restrictions which may delay or
defeat further efforts to unite with them."
The M.L. people know at least a little aboutr
democratic institutions, and this opinion ought,
to count for something.

HATTE’S WONDERFUL FAIR.
“ /DOMING EVENTS,” says the poet, “ cast their shadows before.” What, you may ask, has that
much-used quotation to do with Hatte’s 17th Great Fair —now on? Wait a minute, and vou
will see the application. Probably you know Hatte’s at Newtown ; but possibly you don’t. The
most famous people and the most wonderful things are not known to everybody. When Napoleon
was at the height of his glory, when his victorious armies were marching everywhere, a peasant was
found only a few miles from Paris who had never heard of the man. who set up or knocked down kings
at his pleasure. So we will assume you have never yet heard of Hatte, though, in that case, you
would be almost as ignorant as the French peasant. However, we have something to say about this
same Hatte ; and when we have finished you will be a deal the wiser, and regret you did not acquire
the knowledge long ago. You will regret it because such information might have saved you scores of
pounds.
But, to come back to “ events ” and “ shadows.” Hatte’s establishments occupy a long stretch of
the main thoroughfare in the heart of busy Newtown. Had you passed along that thoroughfare
during the past fortnight, you would have seen drayloads upon drayloads emptying themselves on the
pavement till the piled-up cases cast long “shadows before.” They indicated a great “coming event ”
an event which has now come —his 17th Great Fair. Thus you will see how well the quotation
applies.
And it is a great Fair—not a miserable twopenny-halfpenny thing so often denominated a
“fair.” Hatte has held many fairs, but this “takes the cake,” as they say. There are special
reasons why the present fair is better and more attractive than even its predecessors, why you will get
even bigger bargains than before. Do you want to know why? We will tell you. At the close of
the late season in England—which, you no doubt know, precedes ours—Hatte’s home buyer
cleared out a deal of the surplus stocks of the home manufacturers, of course on very favorable terms.
It was hoped that these shipments might arrive in time for the commencement of the Autumn and
Winter Season here ; but they came just a little too late. Now all these goods Hatte has determined
to put into his Great Winter Fair. They include the newest, freshest, and most fashionable obtain-
able—everything either lady or gentleman can want in the way of clothing—and they are all of
superior quality. Thus you see why the present Fair is specially worthy of your notice and patronage.
Everything will be sold at Fair prices ; and there will be big bargains in every department.
Ladies should not fail to inspect the large display of elegant Parisian millinery. The charming
bonnets and gems of hats cannot be surpassed anywhere. Mrs. Hatte brought them direct from Paris
during her recent visit; and they are such as are worn by the leaders of fashion in the gay French
capital. They are all marvellously cheap.
The collection of dress materials is one of the largest and most carefully selected ever offered to
public notice. The designs are beautiful and fashionable and very durable, and will make very pretty
dresses —just the thing for winter wear. It will be seen that the prices are fabulously cheap.
In the gentlemen’s departments, Hatte has this time excelled himself. Such an assortment of
winter clothing was hardly ever before got together. Fancy a fashionable, well-made, stylish Chester-
field overcoat from 12s. lid. to 255. For 17s. lid. you may secure a garment fit for a prince. Macin-
toshes are also going at bargain prices—from 12s. lid. to 355. These are some of the best goods
obtainable, and are warranted to keep out the wet. Then, again, the ready-made clothing—a single
garment or a complete suit —is equally notable. For a pound, or even under, you may appear attired
as a perfect gentleman.
There are also big lines of mercery and underclothing, too numerous for specification in a news-
paper article; but a casual glance will show you what remarkable value is offered you during the Fair.
The boot and shoe department deserves particular notice during the currency of the Fair. Hatte
keeps one of the largest and most varied stocks in the colony. They come direct from the manufac-
turers, being bought on the spot by Hatte’s home buyer; and include all the best makes, both Conti-
nental and American. There are some special lines suitable for hard country wear ; as, for instance,
the new military cossack. This is a handsome boot, of great durability, made of the very best
leather ; yet it is sold at the low price of 7s. 6d. The American boots more particularly are noted for
their excellence of material, ease and elegance. All going at Fair prices.
No finer display of blankets is to be seen anywhere than at Hatte’s Fair. You may get a pair of
these useful articles for 2s. lid. ; coloured from ss. 6d. ; and very serviceable, warm bush rugs from
2s. 6d. The ordinary price of these goods would be just double. There are miles of flannels and
flannelettes —the latter from 2s. lid. a dozen yards. Remember all these goods are the newest and
best.
Hatte’s ironmongery department has now become as famous as the others. Here you will find
all kinds of agricultural implements, saddlery and harness at prices, during the Fair, fifty per cent,
below those of the city. It is impossible to detail the items in this comprehensive department; but
you will find in it everything you can possibly need.
Residents in the country, who find it inconvenient to visit the town, can share the benefits of
Hatte’s fair. They have only to write for a copy of the Fair book and patterns and they will be
posted free. You can thus make a selection of what you require, and the arrangements for trans-
mitting goods by post, rail and steamer are now so sure and simple that your orders will be as satis-
factorily executed as though you paid a personal visit. Hatte pays the freight on parcels of £2 and
over by rail or boat.
Gentlemen would do well to note the advantages of sending their tailoring orders to Hatte. His
two guinea suits to measure (including extra pair of trousers) are the marvel of the day, having
effected a complete revolution in tailoring. There’s a splendid assortment of the best and newest
materials to choose from ; the fit is perfect, and the workmanship of the best. Write for patterns f]
and self-measurement forms, and the garments will be despatched in a few days. These suits give
every satisfaction, and hundreds of letters have been received from country customers, expressing
the utmost appreciation at the extraordinary value given. In fact you could not get a better suit for
double the money at any other establishment.

CvclT(so-called) thrown together in a plough, chair, or stove factory ? We think not.
Cyclists are now becoming educated on Bicycle Values, and decline to pay their
good gold for the products of a repair-shop.
We are the only Direct Branch of an exclusive Bicycle Factory represented in
Australia; consequently, “ RED BIRD ” riders get better value for their money, as
they save the intermediate profit.

await your inspection.
