AGENTS EVERYWHERE.
Mr. Gerard Miller explains the origin of Ihe Cake Walk
as datin" back to the bad old days when the South per-
petrated the horrors of Legree. The slaves only amuse-
ment was to deck themselves in the cast-off fiuei> of-their
tyrants, and imitate on grassy lawns m the moonlight
the minuets of the aristocratic ballrooms. And at Sidney
Palace the Cake-Walkers are arrayed to represe t that
remote period. It is an easy matter, watching their
graceful P movements, to imagine that time has iolled
backwards and left one among the gardens of the Old
Dominion. .
Impecunious musical talent can’t be easily boomed in
Holland, seemingly. A young Dutchman, with a passion
for the piano and no means of raising a benefit fund, was
advised to put his hard case before Queen Wilhelnnna.
The practical damsel responded with an otter of six
months’ tuition gratis from a leading professor, the prp-
fessor to report on the young man’s alleged genius at the
end of that time. If the report is favorable, the pupil
will be commanded to thump away and complete his
musical education at the Queen’s expense.
Titus Salt” : In all my life I only met one juggler
who could “lay over” Cinquevalli. This man used to
throw a blank cheque, a pen, and a bottle of ink in the
air,and the cheque would come down filled in and signed.
It was a perfectly good signature, too, and the bank paid
the money without any question. He came to grief in
Carolina (U.S.)—this was about ’59, in the old slavery
days. Just by way of giving a finish to his performance,
he threw a nigger-slave up in the air, and he came down
free, and the crowd lynched the juggler on suspicion of
being an abolitionist. His name was something like
Cinquevalli, too ; I think he called himself Stinkynioun-
tain or something like that. He was a very remark .ble
man with pale black hair, and used to have 18 hard-boiled
eggs for breakfast.
Old “Dad” Williams, who died at Collingwood the
other day, aged near 80, was a great song-and-dance
“ artist ”’in the early days of Victoria. He was a sailor
in youth. Settled in Melbourne in the forties, and per-
formed at John Thomas Smith’s Queen’s Theatre in
Queen-street, now a furniture-maker’s. Smith kept the
pub next door, on the corner of Little Bourke-street,
and called it “ St. John’s Tavern.” He had arrived at
Melb. as a 0. of E. scripture-reader, and gave his pub. an
ecclesiastical title. Williams was one of the first in
Australia to “do” Chinese parts. The old man danced
and sang in private up to the last. Another old Melb.
mummer is Charles Walsh Pugh, who lately became land-
lord of a pub. in Smith-street, Fitzroy. He played and
sang in the old theatre in Lonsdale-street, now Carton’s
livery stables, opposite Melbourne Hospital. Pugh, poor
old fellow, is now blind.
Curly-headed musician Scherek has a big memory. As
a boy, he was a prodigy pianist in Europe ; even now he
is a better artist than many of the stars who are brought
out for people here to marvel at. His memory enables
him to conduct without the aid of a score. But in doing
this he runs the risk of criticism of the kind that assailed
Wagner when he first went to England. Wagner, know-
ing Beethoven’s symphonies by heart, at first conducted
them from memory. But some of the critics found fault
with him, and said he would do the business better if he
conducted from score. At the next concert Wagner con-
ducted with a mighty big score on his desk, and he
turned over the pages solemnly as the music proceeded.
The critics said it was all right then. But Wagner let his
friends into the secret that he conducted from a score of
Rossini’s “ Barber of Seville,” placed upside-down on his
desk.
Few people outside the theatrical profession knew that
George Gordon was at the top of the tree as a scenic
artist in London when Garner persuaded him to come out
to Australia. He came for the sake of the change chiefly,
and the change suited him so well that he made his home
here. London has no great charm for scene-painters.
When Walter Spong returned to the Big Smoke he wrote
to George Gordon in terms of .disappointment and dis-
gust at the sunlessness of the city.
It is a long worm that has no turning. Rougemont has
rounded on one of his critics, and offers to bet the Daily
Chronicle a large sum of filthy lucre that he will ride
a turtle in any aquarium, and guide it by jabbing his toe
into its eye. But what aquarium proprietor is going to
risk the eye of his expensive turtle against the great ad-
venturer’s toe-nail ?
The old dodge of having the Mexican gold and
Abyssinian diamonds stolen wore out. Then the divorce
was worked till it grew hollow and transparent, and now
Alma Stanley has struck out on a new track by periodi-
cally catching cold and dying in various parts. It was
Alma’s second or third death that was recently reported.
Charles Arnold was to have sailed from London on 6th
June for Cape Town, British Central Africa (which sounds
like a new locality for a dramatic co. to go to), Australia,
and Maoriland. L. J. Lohr will manage for him in Aus-
tralia. Arnold is bringing out three plays : “ Captain
Fritz,’’ “Paul o’ the Alps,” and “What happened to
Jones.” “ Hans the Boatman ” and “My Sweetheart”
are both on the printed circular, but they are scored out,
so apparently the good old play with the big dog and the
“kiddies” has been dropped from Arnold’s repertoire.
The Bulletin weeps.
Said that the graceful chansonette sung by a recent
much-boomed debutante at her first concert, was written
in her honor by a foreigner here resident, and that not the
song, but the singer, was encored—a distinction too
subtly painful to suggest itself to the mind of any ordi-
dinary man of crotchets and quavers. But —“ You must
sing my song again,” said this one to the singer as both
stood in the wings listening to the applause. The damsel
smiled mysteriously, went out again, and sang “ Home
Sweet Home.” And when that composer heard he rent
asunder the MS. of his gem and danced a fandango on the
fragments.
Soult, the “dead bird” for the G.N. Hurdle
race last Saturday, went out at 6 to 4 and made only a
poor show considering the starting price. He had the
best of claims to win “ on paper," so Tub Bulletin fancied
Iln,atter 1 l n , atter of forn) - In remarking that “ next to
the dandy black gee-gee. Mystery, Valdis and Nilus may
be worth talking about, just for the sake of argument "
this mcredulous newspaper saved its prophetio bacon.
Valdis and Mystery were bracketed as second favorites
afc *? wi-?? ch . an ul th S? fin « s hed first and second. The
Mark Whitty stable adherents proved to be the knowing
ones this year. Ourtin took the mount on Valdis for
choice, but felt pretty confident that Mystery would get
there or thereabouts. The winning stable backed both
and had a calmly happy time as the two leaders came
down the straight with the rest of the field fairly out of it.
Soult, perhaps, was not at his best on Saturday,
for, granting that he held a bad, boxed-in position most
of the journey, there was no visible reason why he should
not have gone to the front half-a-mile from home if he
had been capable of the effort. The favorite didn’t look
bright enough to please everybody in the “birdcage,"
and it is hard to believe that he ran true to the “form’”
of a fortnight previous, when he gave Borderer 41b. and
beat him handsomely over the two-mile course at Caul-
field. On Saturday he met the same Borderer on 181 b.
better terms, and finished lengths behind him, whereas
Mr. Miller’s horse, in getting third place for the big
event, showed that 0 to 4 was a fair price about Soult’s
chance—on paper. So much for “ certainties."
* * • •
The satisfactory dearth of casualties—only two
falls in the three races over the sticks —suggests that
cross-country jockeys may congratulate themselves on
the present mediocrity of jumping horses in general.
The Flemington course was certainly no heavier last
Saturday than when Redleap (with list. 101 b. up) scored
his second G.N. Hurdles victory in 9sec. faster time.
There were no first-raters to force the pace, and conse-
quently nothing came to grief in the 3-mile raoe. Slow-
running makes safe jumping.
• * • •
Backers of coincidences will naturally go for the
double V, and take Vigil in the G.N. Steeplechase.
The son of Lord Wilton has shown himself to be in form ;
he beat Troy and Hayseed a few days ago, and may do it
again over the longer course, nevertheless he will not
carry the present writer’s two-and-sixpence. The Steeple-
chase is a very’ open-looking handicap. Floater, Domino,
Ditto, Hayseed, Pirate, Doondiah, Mistletoe, Troy, Vigil,
Ouyen, and even Comboyne, appear to have chances.
The weight will not stop Domino, if he can go the three
miles over heavy ground, but this is a doubtful point.
The writer prefers the chance of Pirate to beat Hayseed,
Floater and Domino.
* * • •
Coincidence at Warwick Farm (N.S.W.) races
last Saturday : The first race was won by Lady Lygon,
and the next by Oast Iron, a son of imported Beauchamp.
* * * »
The nearest approach to the renowned “ Malua
rush ” seen in Sydney for many days was shown by the
big-framed black Sunrise horse Eastern King, in snaring
the principal handicap at Warwick Farm on Saturday.
At 100yds. from home the favorite, Mayo’s Carberra, and
Bellwood were in front and going strong with Eastern
King apparently lost in the crush ; but a little further on
a dark shadow flitted down the centre of the course and
when the judge was reached the lengthy descendant of
Sunrise was found with his head poked out in front of
the field. Since Wallace, Australia hasn’t produced
many nags that can down E.K. at the finish of a slow-run.
race.
* * * *
Saturday's hurdle race fit Warwick Farm was
a stirring event. Four started : Sammy Brass, a large
shapeless hunk of beef on four shaky legs, an embittered
grey .mare rejoicing in the name of Lady Lygon, Satan, a
real good cut of a ’bus-horse, and an indescribable quad-
ruped called Escape. Lady Lygon was a slightly-better
favorite than Satan, and, climbing the fences and jump-
ing them sideways and backwards and any other way
that came handy, she got home first in the break-neck:
time for two miles of 4min. Bsec. Satan was second, and.
Sammy Brass, who when galloping his hardest goes up
and down in the same place, arrived late and was third.
Escape got round some time before the last train left.
The prize was £5O, or thereabouts, which would have
been an extravagant figure to give for the whole of the
starters. There is big money waiting for anyone who.
will quietly start a strong jumping stable in Sydney.
* * m *
The column wheeze in Sydney D.T. last Friday
re the attitude of the A.J.O. towards registered book-
makers running “ shops,” much amused turf circles, and
is generally put down as having been written in the in-
terests of one man. Only two or three of the leather-
lunged are in the “ shop ” business now—the others'
having given up when warned to do so months ago.
* * * *
Cross-country jockey Rheece, who died at Syd-
ney St. Vincent’s Hospital last week, was as game a man
as ever rode a horse. Injured on June 3, and suffering
from fracture of the base of the skull and laceration of
the brain, he remained unconscious for over a month.
The tenacity with which he clung to life defied medical:
explanation, but it was characteristic. For no other
rider was the ambulance waggon so frequently called into
request. Many a gruelling tussle has Rheece had with,
the scythe-bearer, but none so prolonged as the last.
“ Poor Billy ” was carried-in on a stretcher so regularly
that his very escape from accident was food for com-
ment. Not to see him in bandages and splints or using a
sling was rare.

should be good results from the crossing of the St. Simon,
stock with Musket mares.

The Australian XI. up to Saturday last had
played 17 matches—won 9, lost 1, and drawn 4, and had
scored 6710 runs as against the Britishers’ 6420. Noble
heads the batting-average with 46 per innings ; Hill is
next with 42, Worrall 41, and Gregory, Darling, Trumper
and Trumble all over 30. Noble had made most runs
(953), and Hill held highest individual score (160). Laver
had scored most “blobs,” viz., 6; Howell was second
with 4, Noble, Gregory, Trumper and Jonah 3 each, and
Hill 1 only. In bowling, the consistent Trumble is an
easy first, with the fine average of 13 6 for 70 wickets,
Laver next with 17, Noble and Howell.lB, and Worrall 69.
Hill and Noble have three times soored centuries, and'
Worrall, Darling and Gregory twice, whilst Laver holds
the distinction of having pjayed in a match without
getting a strike in either innings, owing to one being
closed and the Other unfinished. To date, the XL has a-
finer record than any of its predecessors.
* .•
With Iredale,'Worrall and Hill temporarily
disabled, it is lucky for the Australian team that 14 men.
were taken. Iredale is credited with having been the-
source of opposition to the inclusion of the last man on
the ground that two extra were ample ; it was the irony
of fate that he should be the first to be unable to take the
field.

• * * •
Jim Phillips’ report on the match with Oxford
University goes to show that the Australians might have
fared badly at times but, for the terrible deliveries of
“ Jonah.” The fast bowler was holiday-making when the
team tackled the Oxonians, and Phillips writes :—“ For
the University Champain and Collins gave an elegant
exhibition of batting. Try as Trumble and Howell would
they could not effect a separation. . . . Darling was
sorely tried, his bowling, for the first time on the tour,
being fairly collared. He made frequent changes, but it
was not until 190 were up, with only two wickets down,
that Champain was well caught at cover-point for 120,
made without a real chance.” At Cambridge, where
Jones bowled, but failed to come off (one wicket for 124
runs) in the first innings, two of the ’Varsity batsmen
scored 248 runs between them. Again, at Yorkshire,
where Jones was not seriously on the job, little Brown
knocked the bowling all over the place.
Shrewsbury seems to have excused himself rather
feelingly from playing in the third test on the ground
that he didn’t “ feel equal to the strain.” The strain of
wanting to beat ’em would be pretty severe in his case,
for he cherishes an old resentment against Australia.
The last team that Shaw and Shrewsbury brought to this
country was neglected in favor of another English Eleven
captained by W. W. Read, under the patronage of Melb.
Cricket Club. The pro. crowd returned ’Ome vowing
eternal unfriendship for everything Australian. They
were not even given a chance to try ©occlusions against
Read’s opposition lot, though the public would have
rolled up freely to see a match between the two English
■elevens after they had, in turn, defeated the chosen
“ Our Boys,” who were about to try their luck in Hing-
land. What a difference in relative “form ” since then !
a * » *
Mention of Yorkshireman Bates as one of the
few bowlers who have done the “ hat-trick” in an Anglo-
Australian contest is another reminder of the present
lack of “all-round” talent among the English pros.
Bates and his contemporaries, Ulyett, Barnes, and Bar-
low, were valuable batsmen as well as bowlers in their
day. With the exception of Hayward, who never bowls
brilliantly even at his best, these isn’t now a pro. worth
talking about in comparison with Trumble and Noble. In
the three test-matches up to date, 60 per cent, of the
Australian wickets fell to Hearne, Rhodes, and Young,
who played six innings among them for a grand total of
17 runs.
* * * *
Weather-conditions are singularly against the
present Australian team in England in the matter of
records. With its splendid batting-battery, it should at
least make one sensational score. The 1893 team put up
843 against the combined ’Varsities at Portsmouth, which
is the second-highest record in first-class cricket. Strange,
too, that Murdoch’s record-innings for an Australian
eleven in England—2B6 against Sussex, in May, ’B2 has
never been topped since. Against the weak county
bowling it should be easy for one of the present team
during August—the real English batsman’s month.
Darling does not seem to have yet struck the
proper pair of batsmen to start the Australian batting.
In every match, so far, the first wicket has fallen cheaply.
A few years ago, when Lyons and Bannerman were the
opening pair, you could pearly always depend on 100 at
least for first‘wicket. Darling’s opening seems to be
always two hitters —one of whom generally fails to hit.
He might try Blackham’g plan of a hitter and a stone-
waller ; according to English criticism he has enough of
the latter to choose from.
* • * •
Clem. Hill has suffered from a nasal trouble for
the last three years, and Adelaide doctors told him he
would have to undergo an operation. But he ignored
their advice to have the affection—a small polypus—re-
moved before leaving last April.

Football match N.S.W. v. Q., at Brisbane, last
Saturday, was the 39th, of which .N.S.W. notches 24 wins
and Q. 12 ; three were drawn. Visitors in latest meeting
scored 11 points to 5, and the game was about the best
’seen in Brisbane for years. The N.S.W. men, though by
no means the crack 15 (keenness of interest in club-com-
petitions precluded a better selection), out-played oppo-
sition in almost every department. The Queenslanders re-
cently “ laid over ” Britishers by 11 points to 3; then what
might almost he termed a N.S.W. scratch lot comes along
■and vanquishes the Bananalanders. Ergo, it is just as
well our friends from “down under” are not going to
visit M.L. .

Dear Bulletin.— Condole with N.S.W. and
■Queensland on the shocking moral and physical depravity
of their footballers as made manifest in Bulletin “ sport-
ing notes” of July 1, wherein appears note after note
proving the local players to be sneaks, wasters, weak-

angelic Englishmen whom they beat. Queensland has
since beaten the immaculate Britishers, probably by a,
further display of sneaking, cowardliness, ruffianism, and
skite, although Rev. Captain Mullineux generousy
blames the umpires. Don’t deal much in football myself,
but The Bulletin has convinced nie that local parsons
should follow Mr. Mullineux’s noble example, and set, upas
missionaries to the Rugby footballers of Australia. Of
course, a few of them would be eaten, but the parson is
notoriously heedless of the scoffer. —Snell.

* * * *
The Victoria Club (Melb.) billiard tournament
this year seems likely to end in blood and hair. A certain
member —playing under the name of “ Prosper”—who,
somehow or other, had not, to put it mildly, shown his
best form in practice, received a handicap of 160, others
being as many behind scratch. As usual, there was much
betting, and £l4OO was taken about Prosper at odds of
100 to 1 for the final. But, in his first heat, he showed
such form that betting: was paralysed, and odds of 3 to 1
on him for the final were laid. He continued gaily on his
way, and, at time of writing, is quoted at 10 to 1 on.
There are six left in the battle for the £250 prize, and J.
Cohen, who was drawn against the cert., refused to play,
leaving him the bye. Then the crash came. Bookmakers,
who laid him at 100 to 1, say they won’t pay. They have
appealed to the committee to disqualify Prosper, but the
committee admits that it is powerless, and the affair
will probably end in law. Coleman, one of the best
players in the club, met Prosper in a heat, and caught
him in two breaks, but the “ mug” then took a hand, and
beat the scratch-man pointless.
* * * *
The second engagement between Captain’s Flat
(N.S.W.) miner, sturdy Dan O’Connell, and Jack Conlon,
at Golden Gate A.C. '(Sydney,), on Saturday night last,
drew a packed house. Dan, hardly as fit as he might
have been, looked twice the man whom Conlon so off-
handedly defeated some weeks before, and shaped twenty
times as well, for though he stopped dozens of hard jabs,
swings, hooks, and uppercuts (which left palpable traces
of their effect), and was spreadeagled on (probably) 30
occasions, Dan came up full of sand and had the danger
signal flying all the time. Round after round did Conlon
jump nimbly away from savage lunges and sweeps that
would have sent him out of the ring had contact been
made, and such an amount of vim were they carrying
that the deliverer of them frequently went sprawling into
the ropes or slithering on all-fours along the floor.
O’Connell had the crowd with him to a man through his
plucky display ; even “ Ironbark ” Burge, who had backed
the other fellow, was beside himself with excitement,
urging the gritty Dan on. The twelfth 3inin. provided
something to be remembered. O’Connell landed a fear-
ful punch on the back of Conlon's neck, sending him
prone as if felled by a club. The crowd rose upon its feet
and howled again. Conlon staggered up like a drunken,
man. O’Connell lost his head and missed with several
mad rushes. Meanwhile Conlon pulled himself together,
reached the point hard, and down went Daniel. The tables
were turned; O’Connell weakened as the battle pro-
gressed, Police stopped it in round 18, and the verdict
went in favor of Conlon.
• • * *
“Old-Timer”: Witnessed Conlon - O’Connell
fight in Sydney, Saturday night. Saw O’Connell brimful!
of fighting-instinct and pluck, but adopting crudest of
methods—stand up before lusty young Conlon, inches
taller and longer in reach, and take a terrible gruelling
without winking during an hour of very hot work. Crowd
cheered Dan, and jeered Jack for not going in and
mixing. Why should he? Mixing meant great risk to.
him. He was fighting for his backers’ money and the
purse. O Connell is game to the core, and so is Conlon.
First-named pug’s exceptionally short reach handicaps
him much, but, lor’, how cunningly he smothered up t
Referee gave the fight to Conlon when police interfered
in 18th round, but how can stakes or purse be paid over
on that decision—the conditions being best of 20 rounds,
and O'Connell was prepared to keep at it?
* * * *
The battle between Herb M‘Kell (N.S.W.) and!
Dutchman Jim Holloway at Johannesburg (S.A.), June 3,
involved the South African championship and drew a.
£l5OO gate. Barring the eleventh round, wherein M’Kell
tried to bite Holloway’s back and later knocked him
down whilst the referee was interfering and the Dutch-
man had his hands by his sides, 22 very tame rounds were
witnessed. M’Kell kept running away the whole time
and would not fight—the crowd hooting and groaning at
him from beginning to end. Match had to be declared a
draw as conditions provided for that decision if both men
were on their feet at the end of the 25th round. Ih s
occurrence is said to have given the new boom a knock
from which it will not recover. Johannesburg papers
assert that M’Kell would have won had he been game to
fight, but he wasn’t. This pug’s career bristles with
similar happenings; such is his reputation in Australia
that no one would give a purse for him to fight anybody.

We are requested to inform Bulletin readers
that the management of the Sydney Safe Deposit (in Ash-
street, back of Paling’s, Sydney) have decided, with a
view to popularising the use of the safes, to offer a special
discount of 25 per cent, to persons paying rent for two
years in advance. At present the annual rents in advance
are from two to six guineas, and the new system will
have the effect of reducing the rents to from 1J to six
guineas. It has also been decided to lease safes at from
15s. per quarter and 255. per half year, a further innova-
tion.*

The Amy Castles outburst in Sydney has
excited discussion upon Soul and Expression in
Art. If Soul is Truth, and Art the expression of
Truth, then the more soul the artist is blessed
with the more does he make Truth apparent and
vivid. It must, therefore, be perfectly obvious to
the Sex when we say the beautiful Truths of soul-
delighting female contour are best expressed by
that boonful work of Art the P.D. Corset.*

Deniehy, daughter of that distinguished man of
letters, the late Daniel Henry Deniehy. A sister,

has been dead over 30 years.

The advisers of young Newman, the inven-
tor of the new noiseless and smokeless electric-
gun, must have vast confidence in the weapon’s
possibilities, if, as alleged, Newman has refused
<£75,000 from the Admiralty for his invention.
The little model gun, weighing no more than a
light rifle, has shown great effect at five miles’
range. Newman, an apprentice at Whithead’s
torpedo-factory, is only 20 years old.

When Bourke (Afghan) Carrying Co. be-
gan to flourish its notorious agreement and crack
the whip over recalcitrant drivers at Broken Hill,
there was a prompt rally among local Labor-men,
and a searching cross-eximination was promised
if Abdul Wade, the general manager, got into the
box. And Abdul Wade promptly climbed down—
told a local interviewer that he wasn’t going to
prosecute any men for anything they had done,
and had, moreover, put up the drivers’ wages all
round to <£3 per month as a minimum. Also, he
explained that it was the prospect cf the passing
of the Alien Immigration Restriction Bill which
led him to post off an agent to the Punjab (he
says that most of the drivers are not true Afghans)
and fix a consignment for a long term. Hence
the six-years’agreement, with the right of “ sack ”
on the co.’s part at any time.
Re the alleged mysterious robbery of £9OO
from Perth (W.A.) G.P.O. It will be remembered
that the Commission of Inquiry recommended
that Mr. Stevens, who had charge of the monthly
pay, should refund the amount at the rate of £lOO
a year. When Stevens went to lunch, he and his
assistant put the money in a black bag, which was
deposited in a cupboard, and the office-door closed
with a Yale lock. When they came back the
money had disappeared, and neither the police nor
the commission was able to say how the trick had
been done. Stevens, acknowledgedly an old and
trusted officer, has received final notice to refund
the loss on the monthly time-payment system. As
a victim of circumstances, Mr. Stevens is to be
commiserated, but a head department of Govern-
ment which is in the habit of leaving <£9oo with
only a Yale lock between it and the hungry
world is evidently a peculiarity peculiar to Groper-
land.

The Nordach consumption-treatment, re-
cently started at Yass (N.S. W.) new sanatorium,
consists mainly in abundant and generoi s dieting,
with plenty of fresh air. The climate of Yass
(190 miles from Sydney) is ideal for the pur-
pose, as the place, besides being 1600 ft. above
sea-level, is upon the western side of the dividing-
range, and is thus sheltered from the pulmoni-
cally-injurious moist sea-breezes. The building
was erected as a country d welling by Hamilton
Hume, the explorer, and is surrounded by garden
and orchard grounds of no less than 186 acres’
extent. The whole place, though every attention
has been given to sanitation and disinfection,
gives more the impression of a private country
home than of hospital or sanatorium. It is under
the management of Mr. H. A. Unwin —himself
an exemplification of the curative effects of the
local atmosphere.

Jb® ‘ Alfred ’ has a great reputation for suc-
cessfully treating typhoid. Lots of lads of 15 and
upwards go there m summer suffering from this
complaint. Whether released by death or re-
covery, these susceptible sufferers to a boy go
their ways deeply in love with ‘night nurse.’
“Delirious cases are always anxious to get
home. A lady driving past the ‘Alfred’ was
accosted one day by an escaped patient with
nothing on but a shirt. He begged and entreated
her to take him to his wife and children. She
didn’t like the responsibility but hadn’t the heart
to refuse, so just remained passive. He jumped
in, took the reins, drove to his address—and
finally recovered.

a visit to the Brasseys by the time this Bulletin
is published, and several dinners and dances have
been arranged to allow of the Very Nicest meeting

generally shown round the place. The Brasseys
will probably return to Sydney with Lady Mary,
and go on thence to Brisbane.
“ One of the attaches on an Australian Gover-
nor’s staff seems too chirpy for his surroundings.
At a recent function he skipped up to the host
and said, ‘Do you want to get rid of H.E. yet?
Say when you’ve had enough, and we’ll let him off
the chain. We woke him up to bring him here,
and the sooner he gets his ticket-of-leave the better
for this child and eo.’ The outraged entertainer
recoiled, inwardly recited what he could remember
of ‘ Gorsave, ’ and at last broke the awkward pause
with a solemn reproof to the scoffer.
“The grey-gowned, white-gloved Mayoress got
through with her second reception on July 6th.
Some of the newest tailor-suits which waited upon
her at the Town Hall trailed all round and had
scalloped Goat-skirts, to the knees at the back,
but shorter in front.

“The winner of a recent technical - college
cookery competition sensibly chose a set of sauce-
pans as her prize. The committee was at first
about to approve of the selection, until one shocked
member pointed out that the Governor was to
make the presentation, and ‘we couldn’t ask his
Excellency to handle saucepans, you know.’ ‘Of
course not! ’ chorused the committee, and the
frize-winner was awarded Milton’s ‘ Paradise
iost,’ as it had a pretty cover and would look
Well in the Governor’s hands.
“A woman hereabouts has an antipathy to cabs.
Once upon a time, as Mine Host’s buxom daughter,
she was engaged to the late Lockie M‘Bean, the
Deniliquin millionaire. The furniture was bought
and the cage all ready for Lockie’s bonnie bird,
when, arriving unexpectedly at her mother’s house,
he saw the bride-elect, who was slightly indis-
posed, driving off in a cab. ‘Was that -?’ he
asked. ‘lt was. ’ ‘ Did she take a cab ? ’ ‘ She
did.’ ‘Well, good-bye, Mrs. , to you and
your daughter. No man could stand such extra-
vagance ! ’ And so ended Lockie’s young dream.
“According to authorities, the head gardeners
at Kew (Eng,) belong to the flower-loving sex,
while their subordinate diggers of earth and
drawers of water are mere men. Those girl-
grafters at our own horticultural reserve
listen to lectures and potter at light jobs.
When there’s perspiration to be shed, the de-
moiselles relapse into the rdle of ‘weak women.’
That’s where the male students come in. By-and-
bye, when the fair horticulturists win their
diplomas and set up ‘on their own,’ they’ll be
eaten out of house and home by able-bodied he-
apprentices, indispensable for the manual labor.
“The other day, a card, inscribed ‘The Hon.
Miss Jobson,’ was sent in to a lady at South
Yarra. Thinking Gov. Brassey had sent one of
his friends on a morning visit, she tripped to the
drawing-room, paused suddenly on its threshold,
and exclaimed, ‘ Low—Kate ! ’ It was old Jobson’s
eldest and most ambitious. Jobson is M.L.C.
“Nowadays if Mary Jane, the servant ga,l,
expresses her unpolished opinion of things in
general when out with her young man she runs a
serious risk of being mistaken for a member of the
highah circles. A voice from the drapery trade
whispers that the daughter of a gilded landowner
on being shown some gowns which were more
expensive than she wanted them, said, . Oh
thanks! She-wasn’t-taking-any. ’ Then, again, the
daughter of an official personage went shopping
with a large, untidy damsel from Sydney, and the
astounded ‘young persons’ behind the counter
didn’t know whether to treat the visitors’ Billings-
gate as simple sportiveness or polite conversation.
Anyway they thought it best to give a respectful
hearing to such remarks as ‘Oh my ! we-can-get-
them-anywhere-up-the - street -at - ninepence. Oh,
lor ! five-shillings ! take-me-home ! ’ And that
awful customer, it seemed, was taken home in a
carriage behind a pair of quite well-bred horses.
“How the tailors and haberdashers do lay
themselves out to cater for the foibles of the men !
Last summer the Melb. craze was to have the
riband round the gay youth’s straw hat decorated
with what purported to be a yacht-club badge j
this winter the nautical notion is still to the fore,
the straw-decker being exchanged for a yachtsman s
blue cloth cap, whereby the bold counter-jumper
and the buccaneering barber’s-assistant fascinate
the giddy bar-lady.

Flossiest trippers on the block. Cheeks be-rouged,

that you can hear it coming.
“There was a fair audience at Scherek’s sixth
concert (Saturday, July Bth). ‘Athalie’ and
Handel’s ‘Concerto,’ both turned off in tanieish
style, were followed by Gounod’s ‘ Berceuse.’ The
last-mentioned was sung by Minnie Waugh, whose #
smart lilac-silk blouse opened over chemisette and
under-sleeves of ivory satin, while her becoming
hat and brocade skirt were black. ‘ Danse
Macabre,’like all the less heavy numbers treated
by Scherek’s orchestra, was served-up to perfec-
tion. After cock-crow, when the clicketty-
clacketty dancers had scattered, the last faint
rattling of their bones as they huddled back into
their graves was quite pathetic. And Bertie
Zelman piled on the pity by his wailful rendering
of Death’s lamentation re the vanished revel.
Fair-haired Una Bourne, who rattled a pair of
bones during this item, hopes to carry on her
education in Europe some day, but will not ‘go
a-begging.’ Meanwhile she will learn and practise
here, accept musical engagements, save her earn-
ings, and watch for some lucky breeze to waft her
into the haven where musicians fain would be.
“Miss Hansen-Knarhoi, the Bendigo violinist
who lately electrified the Austral Salon, is en-
gaged to play at Mrs. Doubleday’s concert at
Melb. Town Hall on 29th inst. She is of Danish
extraction, and her real name is Knarhoi ; but
finding that people could not pronounce it, her
father, some years ago, dropped it, and took the
better-known Danish name of Hansen, which she
now retains in conjunction with her patronymic.
She is an unaffected young girl, tall and good-
looking, with the most passionate love of her art.
A travelled lady who heard her play lately said
that, with the exception of Sarasate, no one had
made her feel as Miss Knarhoi did. Her double-
stopping and technique are marvellous, and she
has not had a lesson for the last seven years. Yet
she is little over twenty.
“ Three ‘ high-class ’ little love-songs have been
forwarded to Allan’s by Mr. Albert Mallinson
(Madame Steinhauer’s hubby) late of Melbourne.
Composed and published in Germany they have
the words in both German and English. ‘The
Plaint of the Leaves ’ is graceful in a slow and
mournful strain, the ‘ Slumber Song ’ —difficult to
interpret—is an artistic thing with a fetching
accompaniment, and ‘O, Thank me not,’ can
almost be called lively. As compositions the
three Mallinson efforts are good as far as they go,
but they seem rather unfinished. Probably they
are a little too good to live —in a robust sense.
“ In one of Melbourne’s fashionable tea-rooms
the wages earned by one of the kitchen-maids is
about a shilling a day for a day of 12 hours. She
is going hard all the time, and receives a penny an
hour for ber pains. The girls who wait earn the
munificent wage of nine shillings a week for all-
day service, five or seven for two-thirds of the
day. And as these girls are ladies, tips are out of the
question, so they agree to look spruce and smiling,
to run about all day with trays, to display sym-
pathy with the needs of each customer, to be
affable, but not free; dignified, but not haughty—
in fact, to cultivate an amiable tea-room manner
which must be as far removed from the seductive-
ness of a barmaid as the comedy of Peggy Pryde
is from the tragedy of Sara Bernhardt—for about
twopence-farthing an hour.
“ And, again, about tea-rooms. They have
washed clean cup and platter, but it is as well not
to inquire what lies behind. In one room (unlet
at present, I fancy) there was much to tempt eye
and appetite; but I went behind the scenes on one
occasion, and from out of drains, from lavatories,
from everywhere arose odors unspeakable. Germs,
probably of all sorts, gathered and gloated over
masses of butter, the cans of milk, the jars of
cream.
“ The place to me was intolerable, and I asked
one of the girls if she did not suffer from it. ‘ Oh,
yes ; we all get sore throats now and again, and
at first I thought I could never stand it, but I am
so used to it now I don’t notice it. Some of the
girls get sick, but I am very strong. ’ And as she
spoke a kitchen-maid set down a can of milk
beside the drain, remarking— 1 It’s best to leave it
in a draught.’
Y ours affectionately,

Two Hebraic gentlemen came in with two
tinted ladies ; likewise a fire-eating major arrived.
They all gave their orders at once : “ Stewed
oysters ’’ “ Raw oysters ” “ Curry.” Waiter :
“ Beg pardon ’ ” Major : “Curry, d’ye eear me—
hurry!” Gents: “Two stews.” Ladies: “Two
raws.” Waiter (rushing up to slide) : “Two
stews for two Jews ! Two raws for two ——’s,
and one ourry in a blanky hurry ! ”
The Queensland anti-Federalists are telling
the local workers the same lie that did so much
service in N.S.W.—that under Federation their
wages will be “ reduced to the low standard of
N.S.W. and the other provinces.” In N.S.W.
the wail was that Cornstalk wages would be
reduced to the low standard of Queensland, Vic-
toria and other provinces. In W.A. the cry is
that wages will be reduced under Federation to
the low standard of N.S.W., Queensland, Vic-
toria and other provinces. The anti-Federal
theory is that in every province the wages are
higher than in any other province, so Federation
will reduce vyages in every province to the abject
level of all the other provinces.
The Prince of Wales is credited with hav-
ing said—“ After my mother, the Baroness Bur-
dett-Coutts is the most remarkable woman in
England,” which was a very natural remark for
Albert Edward to make under his royal circum-
stances. The Queen is remarkable for having
held an imposing billet for 60 years, and the
Baroness is remarkable for having been a very rich
woman all that time. They did their duty up to
a certain point, and the duty didn’t put any strain
on their constitutions. They are chiefly famous
for having lived in exceptional comfort, whilst
the “ masses,” for whom they have both expressed
much sympathy, were dying off at a comparatively
early age. In an intellectual sense, neither the
Queen nor the Baroness is great, and, as for physi-
cal endurance, any octogenarian female in a work-
house can produce a grander record.
A genuine-looking yarn, incidental to
the marriage of Lord Rosebery’s daughter, ap-
peared in M.A.P. The tale runs that a London
“district-visitor,” going her rounds among the
slums the day after the gorgeous ceremony, dis-
covered a carman’s wife preparing to pack up her
best bonnet and her one other apron and clear out
of the ’appy ’ome. “ I went to the wedding yes-
terday,” snorted the carman’s missis, “ becos I
was once in Lord Rosebery’s service—before I had
the misfortune to marry. When I saw the Dukes
and Duchesses riding by, specially the Duchess of
Cleveland, I made up my mind that I couldn’t put
up with Bloggs no longer. He’s so big, and such a
hearty eater, and so common, and he fills the whole
place, and I’m going.” Similar yarns might be
told, with more or less truth, of a hundred work-
worn women after every public pageant. A horny-
handed toiler who marries a “gentleman’s servant ”
is simply asking her to despise him as soon as the
sordidness of her new life has got fairly on her
nerves. She has lived in a world where the first
duty of man is to look always clean, and the
thought that Bloggs can’t help having blue and
broken finger-nails gives her no solace when she
sees him cutting a thick slice off the loaf. “ The
Manxman ” was very much the story of Mr. and
Mrs. Bloggs, with a well-educated bachelor thrown
in.

“Young Bill” Roberts, who died the
ciher day, aged 54 years, was a Sydney solicitor
6f long standing. He was so named to distinguish
him from his father, “ old Bill ” Roberts. “ Old
Bill,” erstwhile the king of the criminal side of the
police-courts, who conducted the defence of mur-
derer Bertrand, is a Sydney native, and was
admitted a solicitor in Nov., 1845, having served
articles with the historic “Bob” Nicholls, with
whom also James Martin learned law. Old
solicitor as Mr. Roberts is, there is one older—
Mr. Andrew Hardie McCulloch, who dates from
Feb., 1844. Two other ancients are John Dawson
and ffm. Russell, both dating 19th Nov., 1846.

A Bit of the Truth.
The most pertinent and startling question ever
asked is this :—“ What is truth?”
A Roman Governor propounded it once, under
circumstances which greatly worried and perplexed
his official mind. And he was not the first man
or the last —not by myriads. So far as I know
there has been no satisfactory answer. Some
people (radicals and come-outers of various sorts)
fancy that in this, the tail end of a rather braggart
and conceited century, they have flushed a fair-
sized covey of truths by firing speculative shot-
guns into every wayside bush and bog.
But have they done it ? No, gentle reader, no.
They have put up crows and sparrows, the same
crows that picked the bones of the cave-dwellers,
and the same sparrows that sold two for a farthing
in the time of Pilate. There were plenty of fools
of old, and there are plenty now. The ancient
doctors, indeed, prescribed some horrible stuffs as
medicine—they used electuaries of viper’s flesh and
recommended pomegranate seeds for toothache
because those seeds resemble human teeth. Very
shallow and silly, to be sure, this sounds to us.
But if you wanted to find things that come very
near matching them in modern practice, I could
show you where to look. On my table I have a
list of about 300 new “ remedies ” introduced to a
suffering world within the past twelvemonth.
“ Must be some good ones among them,” do you
say? Possibly ! Time will tell. Meanwhile let
us stick to whatever we are sure of. “We learn
how to cure diseases,” said Celsus, “by experi-
ence, not by reasoning.”
“ Some of the greatest truths in medicine,” said
a learned Scotch doctor, “ came by the humblest
means ; not by synthesis or venesection, but by
the observations of peasants and the experiments
of motherly women.”
Concerning a medicine discovered by one such
woman, thousands of stories have been told and
letters written. Here is an example
“ For many years I have suffered from indiges-
tion and weakness. I seemed to have no energy
for anything. I had a poor appetite, and what
little food I ate caused me violent pains in the
chest and between the shoulders. Frequently I
had attacks of giddiness, and when I stooped I
suffered from an unnatural rush of blood to the
head. The pain which I was called upon to bear
was often very severe ; it affected all parts of my
body, and at night I got little proper and refresh-
ing sleep on account of it.
“As time went on and the complaint grew
fixed upon me, I came to be exceedingly weak,
and now and again was obliged to take to my bed.
I lost flesh and became quite thin, living, as I
did, only on milk, beef tea, and other kinds of
liquid food. It will be understood, of course, that
I had medical care, besides attending the South
London Dispensary. Yet I received no benefit
from what was done for me.
“It happened that in January, 1889, a friend
—Mr. Pullen—told me he had suffered in a similar
manner, and been cured by a remedy called Mother
Seigel's Syrup. Acting on his suggestion, I got a
bottle, and, after having taken it, I found great
relief. Presently my appetite returned, and food
no longer distressed me. Convinced that Mother
Seigel’s Syrup was adapted to my ailment, I con-
tinued the use of it until it was no longer needed.
My health and strength were re-established, and I
have since been well. This medicine has done
what no others had been able to do. My husband,
who suffered from biliousness, used it with the same
result. You have my free consent to publish this
brief statement, if you desire to do so.”—(Signed)
Mrs. Julia Massey, 133 Lorrimore-road, Kenning-
ton, London, S.E., January 20th, 1898.
There is no royal road to. the discovery of truth
or knowledge. Anybody may find it anywhere.
It is not always he who seeks that finds. Valuable
discoveries are usually made by what, for lack of
a better word, we call accident. The medicine
that cures is the medicine we want, no matter
whether it is as old as the earth or was picked up
yesterday in the fields by a child. That Mother
Seigel’s Syrup cures is proved by a cloud of wit-
nesses. It is a bit of the truth. Therefore it will
not die out, and nothing can take its place.

A Debilitated Nervous
System.
RESTORED TO ITS NATURAL VIGOR,
THE CASE OF
Mrs. William Hanchett.
[From the Windsor , N.S. W., “Australian.")
(by our special reporter.)
“No one knows how I’ve suffered,”
said Mrs. Hanchett, of Macquarie Street,
Windsor, New South Wales, to a reporter
from the Windsor and Richmond Gazette,
who waited upon her with a view to obtain-
ing an account of her ailment and her
recovery.
‘ From wl.-a t did you suffer, Mrs. Han-
chett ? ”
“ From
BSoodlessness ant? Nervous-
ness;
also from awful nervous headaches. First,
my appetite began to go, till I didn’t care
if I never saw food. Then the headaches
came on, generally twice a day, and lasting
an hour or more at a time. Whilst they
lasted the pain can only be described as
agonising. My head was fairly racked with
it. The pain was of a piercing, shooting
character, and flew backwards and forwards
all round my head like shaip
Knives in f*sy Brain;
As the days went on I wasted away. I did
not seem to have sufficient blood to keep
me going, and, although I tried all sorts of
nourishing foods, they did me no good. My
face became pale and waxy, and my lips
were as white as my face. The whole of
the day
I Felt Languid and Tired.
My household duties, which I formerly did
in the course of an hour or so, now proved
too much for me. W T hy, if I only tried to
wash up the tea things I should have to
give it up and sit down. My nights were
always restless, and it’s nothing but the
plain truth when I tell you I didn’t get ten
minutes’ sleep from the time I w ent to bed
till I got up in the morning. It wasn’t long
before my
Nervous System Broke Down
under the strain that was put on it through
the want of sleep. I was so nervous that I
positively daren’t go to bed at all without a
light burning. If I dropped a knife I
screamed out loud. Little things like this
gave such a shock to my nerves. I was
frightened to go out at nights, even with
someone with me. This may riot seem re-
markable to you, but when I tell you that
before I was ill I could go anywhere across
the bush in the blackest night without the
least hesitation you will understand what
a change my
illness Wrought in Me."
“Your nervous system must have been
very weak.”
“It was, indeed. My nerves were all un-
strung. If anyone spoke to me unex-
pectedly I trembled like a leaf. I wouldn’t
answer the door unless I was absolutely
obliged. I didn’t care to see strangers at
all, but felt I should like to be away from
everyone.”
“ Did you keep up your spirits under all
these afflictions ?”
“No; I don’t believe anyone coull. I
became very low spirited. Things which
formerly gave me pleasure did not now
interest me. When neighbours or friends
came in to have a chat to try and liven me
up a bit, I should merely say yes and no ;
and, to tell you the truth, although I appre-
ciated their goodwill and kindness, I was
always
Pleased to See Them Go."
"Did you take any medicine for your
complaint ? ”
“ Oh, yes ! I took many different things
till I really got tired of taking physic.
Some of the medicines and pills I took were
advertised to cure complaints such as mine
in two or three weeks.”
“Did these so-called remedies do what
they pretended ? ”
“ They did nothing at all, except the
pills, and these gave muoh pain to swallow,
and I could feel them *
Sticking at the Bottom
of my throat for hours afterwards. I he*
lieve I should have been just as well off if T
had saved the money I spent on these
things and swallowed bread nills and
water.”
“ Did a doctor see you ? ”
“ Yes ; and prescribed for me, but unfor*
tunately his medicine proved of no value in
my case. I was really in a very precarious
state, and, what was worst of all, the future
held out to me no hope of getting better.
One must get better or worse. One doesn’t
remain stationary in sickness, and I recog-
nised that day by day I was growing a little
worse.”
“ But you look alright now. How do you
account for the change ? ”
“My husband brought me home a bottle
of Clements Tonic. He said, ‘ you must try
this,’ and, as he insisted, I took a few doses,
but I must confess with very little hope of
any good.”
“ Did it have any good effect ?”
“ Yes ; my nerves felt slightly calmer in
tone, so I continued taking Clements Tonic,
and I was very pleased to find that it
enabled me to sleep better at night. I now
felt
Sure of My Ground,
and I never missed a dose. The condition
of my nerves improved every day, and along
with this I lost that despondent, hopeless
view of life, and began to look forward to
health and strength again. Nor was I dis-
appointed. I steadily improved, my head-
aches went, and once more I could take a
cheerful interest in my daily work and
surroundings. Clements Tonic
Plucked Up My Spirits
wonderfully. It braced me up all over, and
I soon began to eat well and put on flesh.
That strange waxy appearance of the skin
changed to a healthy hue, and by a steady
use of Clements Tonic I gradually attained
my present
State of Health and Strength.
I am not a bit nervous, and I can go any-
where and do anything without the least
feeling of fear. If you like to see my hus-
band he can endorse what I’ve said, and
he can also tell you what good Clements
Tonic did for him.”
“ What ailed him ? ”
“ He got ill through exposure, and he got
so thin that a four suit fitted him when he
once wore a six, and that was too small; so
you can guess how
He Fell Away.
His liver was affected, for he got as yellow
as a guinea. Clements Tonic put him right,
and he’s working to-day.”
“ May I publish all this in our paper? ”
“ Certainly.”

I, AmcbHanchett, of Macquarie Street, Windsor, in

make this solemn declaration, conscientiously be-

COUNTRY TRIAL ORDER RESPECTFULLY SOLICITED.
On the Track.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SWAOOIE, on his way to God-knows-where, strikes
some hard cases among squatters. One afternoon
he pulled up at Dustangibba and hailed the boss.
« Any show of a job, sir ? ” (The “ mulga wires ”
had posted him as to civilities.) But Woollen-
skins surprised him.
u Are you a big eater “Well, I dunno,
stammered Swaggie ; “ don’t see as it signifies,
anyway.” “ Don’t it, by jingo ! D’yer expect
me to be stuffin’ gluttons when I can get good
workers with small appetites.” “ Well, you’ll not
find many smaller eaters ’an me, anyhow,” said
Swaggie. He was a small man. “ Good. Do
you take sugar in your tea?” “Why—yes!”
“ Oh, then I don’t want you ! Too d much
sugar used in this kitchen already.”
* • * •
Next day Swaggie called at Muckwood and
asked for beef. “ I’ll see directly,” the manager
said, and went inside. An hour later he came
out and entered the men’s hut close by. Swaggie
was waiting. He’d come a long way with empty
bags, and was tired and hungry. He tightened
his belt to keep his trousers up. The manager
was yarning. He yarned till dark. One of the
men then told him Swaggie was still waiting.
“I know. I’m just trying his patience,” was
the reply.
“By Jove!” said the man afterwards, “he’ll
get a maulin’ for his tricks yet, an’ if anybody
interferes, I’ll wooden him / ” He was sacked that
week. The inevitable crawler informed. . . .
“ Patience-trying ” is a common diversion, prac-
tised in the hope that the cadger will go away.
In one case, a Queensland woman said to the girl,
among other things, “ Tell the loafin’ cow to
wait! ” And the.“ cow ” waited accordingly, till
the handyman and the cowboy had got their
dinners and filled their pipes. Then, to a second
message, the girl brought reply, “She can’t come
out—she’s got her baby to mind ; but the boss will
be home to-morrow night.” The “cow” waited
some more. The next place was too far off. The
“ missis ” started on the piano—to amuse the dear
baby. Then she played at kangaroos, and the 1.0.
grew desperate. “ Tell your mistress,” he said,
“ that I want to buy some meat.”
That fetched her. She came out singing “ After
the Ball,” entered the meat-house, and uncovered
the “ swagman’s cask ” of neck-pieces. “How
much do you want ? ” “ How much a pound is
it?” “Thrippence.” “Gimme one pound.”
She laughed. “ A pound ain’t much.” “ I know
it ain’t,” he retorted—“for thrippence!” She
cut off a hunk of gristle and weighed it. Being
a quarter over the pound, she hacked a slice off.
Payment, two 2d. stamps. “I haven’t a penny,”
she said. “ Will you take the other piece of meat
for the change?” “Good Lord! woman, I couldn’t
think of it,” gasped swaggie ; “ such liberality on
your part would break this God-forsaken station 1
Good-day, ma’am ! ”
Old “Daddy Pintpot,” of the Dawson, had an
awful hatred of ’Tilda’s men. One stuck him up .
for flour once, and gave him a surprise. Daddy
was dipping the flour out of a bin with a pannikin,
a spoonful at a time, when the swaggie interrupted.
“ ’Ere, you dunno how to dip up flour. Lemme
showyer;” and, grabbing the pannikin, he had
dipped about 301 b. into his bag in half-a-minute.
“There,” he said, “ that’s the way to dip up
flour I ” And, slinging the bag across his shoulder,
he was gone before “Daddy ” had got his breath
back.
There’s a genuine pleasure in taking-down a
skinflint. The Hard-Case is the man for that.
They met one morning, and the latter asked for
rations. Said Skinflint: “ There’s an axe and a
heap of wood over there. Apply one to the other,
and apply to me after.” Hard-Case went
ahead till all the wood was “smalled.” Then he
sought Skinflint. “ Got your bags ? ” said S. He
was pleased. “Nevermind the bags jes’ now,”
said Hard-Case; “pay me my wages fust.”
“Your wages ! ” “ Jes’ so 1 I s’pose you know
something o’ the Masters and Servants Act?”
“ But, man, I didn’t put you on! ” “ You
ordered me to cut wood, an’ I cut wood—which
entitles me to a week’s wages. Your sort’s been
gettin’ at my sort too long with yer bloomin’
woodheaps. Pay me a pound now, or keep me
the week.” Skinflint paid ; and for years after
the application of the axe to the wood-heap was a
byword on that station.
Another Hard-Case, who professed to be a born
rainmaker, was refused supplies at a Q. station.
He knelt down in front of the house, took his hat
off, clasped his hands, and prayed that the cream
of damnation would fall to that squatter and all
belonging to him; and that for ages no rain might
fall on that accursed run. As he was scorching
the last blade of grass, and singeing the only
remaining shade-tree, where the one surviving
moke was seeking shelter, a shower of rations
dropped about him, and the drought was broken
up.
Grass-eating as a means of softening the callous
hearted was a common dodge, now played out.
Last case I know of was on Lower Lachlan. A
gaunt-looking tramp, being refused, dropped on to
his hands and knees, and began to browse like a
cow. “ Here, you mustn’t eat that! ” said the
squatter. “ That’s imported grass, and I can’t
import grass for the likes of you ! ”
E.S.S.
According to Cecil Rhodes, “ there should
be only one language in the world—English ! ”
A distinguished German philologist has said that
“if there ever shall be a universal tongue, that
tongue will be English ; ” but, all the same, a
little modesty wouldn’t hurt the Colossus.
A girl of 16, who recently married in
Monaro (N.S.W.) district without her parents’
consent, is alleged to have resorted to a very old
and useful device. She painted “21” on the soles
of her shoes, and then assured the parson quite
truthfully that she was “over 21.” At least.it
was absolutely true if she wasn’t sitting down
with her feet spread out in front of her when she
said it, also provided she was a reasonably-per-
pendicular girl. There are a good many varieties
of this trick. Many years ago, in England, a life-
insurance office disputed a claim on the ground
that deceased hid greatly understated his age,
and that he dit dat 53, whereas he should, accord-
ing to the policy, have been only 43. Deceased’s
son swore that the old man was “just under 44.”
While he did his swear he had a friend sitting in
the rain on the old man’s tombstone with 44
shillings in his pocket to save his soul from per-
jury. Deceased was just under 44 shillings.
An Epicure in Emotions.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was small and slight; there was something
foreign about her bright dark eyes and scarlet
lips, a vivacity and devilry which had lifted her
from sordid beginnings to the unlimited creature-
comforts so dear to women of her type. She sat
alone at an afternoon concert, and appeared indif-
ferent to the fact that she was the cynosure of all
eyes. She was beautifully dressed, perhaps a
little overdressed, but everything she wore was
perfect of its kind, and she divided her pleasure in
the music with a sense of amusement in the know-
ledge that the furs she wore were the gift of the hus-
band of a woman near her, and felt that the woman
suspected it. It made an absurd bond between
them. The other woman wore a tailor-made
gown on a home-made figure, her gloves were
sloppy, her hair just “put up,” not arranged.
Her eyes eagerly devoured every detail of her
rival’s dress and features; she tried to persuade
herself that there was paint on the soft skin, dye
in the glossy coil of hair. But the only art was
borrowed from the masseur and the hair-brush.
Some few feet away sat a handsome youqg man
with a girl. The man had colored slightly on
meeting the dark, bright eyes, but they had
vouchsafed him not the smallest spark of recogni-
tion. If there was one thing more than another
on which Rose Reid prided herself, it was her
tact. She declared that it was infinite, that it
embraced the whole secret of life. That it began
with a keen sense of the social and moral atmo-
sphere wherever she might be, and merged into
second-sight. There were many men she knew
among the audience, and it was her relations with
them, and with their womankind through them,
that gave a zest to her afternoon’s enjoyment. It
was as caviare to the palate. She knew that
many women had already inquired of brother,
husband, or lover, “Is that Rose Reid?” or “Is
that the creature you men rave about ? ” or “ Do
you mean to tell me you call that skinny, oat-
faced thing pretty ? ” Once she lifted her hand,
apparently to settle her hair, but in reality to
show a man she was wearing the bangle he had
sent, and at that very moment the girl beside him
was whispering, “ Harry, would you like me to
get pink or yellow for my new ball-dress ? ”
Then, as presently the hall was hushed to listen
to some marvellous, daring conception of Schu-
mann’s, and the fancy, fed with delicious sound,
fled eagerly to riot in the glorious mad Paradise
which music only can create, Rose Reid leant
back, her lips parted, her bosom heaving, her spirit
away in the world that only a Bacchante with a
Soul may enter.
When the item was concluded, she came back
to everyday things with a start, and, glancing
round, saw that the tailor-made woman was sup-
pressing a yawn. .She felt amused contempt,
and ostentatiously diew her furs closer about her
throat. Then she saw that a little girl sat beside
the woman —a thin, tired child, timid and de-
pressed. She took occasion when the concert was
at an end to get close to her, and with a quick
movement grasped the little hand in hers. The
child glanced nervously at the broad back of her
mother looming in front, then up into the
stranger’s laughing eyes. She smiled back from
sympathy, nodded as Rose Reid laid a warning
finger on her lips, and, a moment later, found a
Sold coin in her hand, but the pretty lady had
isappeared. •
“ Poor George f ” murmured Rose as she stood
for a minute on the kerb outside. “What a
woman ! Poor little girl! ”
A drizzling rain was falling. The streets had the
miserable, deserted air common to Saturday after-
noons. They looked like a . series of bareback-
yards. People hurried away as fast as trams and
cabs would take them.
Rose had raised her umbrella to hail a hansom
when her attention was arrested by the face of a
man passing by. Not one of the crowd from the
concert, but a miserable, shabby man, with his
thin coat pulled up about his ears, and his hands
thrust deep in his trousers’ pockets.
“ Ned ! ”
He started.
“ Rose ! ”
“ That’s me. You’re down on your luck.”
He shrugged his shoulders, but made no answer.
There was answer sufficient in his appearance, in
his hopeless glance, his slouch.
“ Come home with me, I’ll ”
“ Home with you, be damned ”
“The same as ever. You could make me.loye
you again, I half think. I’ve never ceased to.”
He looked for a moment into the bright eyes,
and a sardonic smile crossed his face.
“Is it worth while talking like that to me/”
“It is only to you it is worth while talking so.”
“That’s pretty; your old tricks are as neat as
ever.”
“Ah!” she frowned, “you never understand.
I loved you —but I loved the others too. You
were stupid to be jealous ; you were more stupid to
find out. ' Where are you going? ”
“To the dogs—there are a fine crew on the
road. We start down the road in broadcloth,
but as we near the last turn this is the correct
uniform ” —and with a wave of the hand he signi-
fied his rags. “ You’ll follow me—and we’ll meet
again there—among the dogs ! ”
Her fingers played with the clasp of her purse,
and his eyes rested on them for a moment.
“ Do you think I’d accept any help from you?”
“As snarling as ever ! You are the only naan
who has insulted me, but you are the only man
who knew just how to kiss me. Ned,” her voice
dropped, “ wouldn’t you like to kiss me again ? ”
They had moved to some little distance from
the crowd, which had now almost dispersed.
He looked into her eyes again.
“ Are you a woman or a devil ? ”
“ Whichever you want. Come home with me.”
“ Bah! Like this, and be grateful for your
bounty. Home be damned ! ” and he flung away
down the street.
She told something of the story to another man
as they sat together over the fire that evening.
“You would have liked me to do something for
him, I knew,” she said, “for you would never
have known me had it not been for him, and ”
Her eyes completed the suggestion.

Our Friend —the Enemy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Away through the Ages he bruised us and bat-
tered,
For mighty of muscle and wary was he ;
He wielded a cudgel that shivered and shattered,
He gave us a physic and never took fee.
He taught us to stiffen our back to the blow,
He taught us to weep and he taught us to crow,
And he taught us to greet with a grin our defeat
—and to stand up again to the Fist of a Foe !
Whenever he thought we were lazy and tender,
He rolled up his sleeves and the game was begun;
He blew a big horn and he bade us surrender,
And called for a crowd to look on at the fun.
He laughed when he found we were flabby—“Ho-
Ho!”
And he winded us clean when he landed us low,
And he taught us to train, till the limb and the
brain were a match for the might in the Fist
of a Foe !
O dear is the hand of the friend that we cherish,
The girl that we love is the light of our soul;
But manhood that dallies for ever must perish
And Peace hath a drug in the deep of her bowl.
But a hater of ease and of sham and of show,
A stirrer of blood when the pulses are slow,
And a Call unto Fame, and a flash and a flame to
the tinder in Man is the Fist of a Foe.
He started the fight in the gloom of the chasm
That yawned in the bed of the infinite sea;
He followed it up from the budding of plasm
And measured his might with his mate in the
tree.
He’s guiding us whether we like it or no,
He’s moulding a race by the grace of his blow,
And he’s bruising our poll for the good of our
soul—this Bigot who’s shut in the Fist of a
Foe!
He’s blended of Preacher and Bludgeon arid
Prophet,
He driveth a spur in the spiritless drone ;
He’s salt in our blood and we hate him like
Tophet;
He harries us on from the Pit to the Throne.
He’s tethered the cravens and got them in tow
(O, little he recks of their railing, I trow);
And the trusty and true, when their season is due,
get a benefit too from the Fist of a Foe !
He’s rough and he’s tough and he laughs at re-
verses,
To drub and be drubbed is the goal of his game ;
He giveth us grit and he getteth our curses,
And leathers us lustily ever the same.
He pounded the cannibals ages ago,
Till Culture and Learning were born of their woe,
And we’re deep in his debt—for he’s pounding us
yet—this Angel of Light with the Fist of a
Foe !
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.
Tonkoko, the Peacemaker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hosking’s girl, coming out marry
him, was annexed on the passagi y »
warrior ; whereafter the coffee- planter, wander g
disconsolately eastward, fell among friends at

Celebes ’ They’re great on coffee in the nortnern
SfSi I Sold Van der Kuyter, the Reg-
dent at Manado. If I give J™ 1 » letter to Lim
DuSnan ’-saith British bureanoracv
in the Bast—“ is a bad master and a worse ad-
ministrator.” Which thing is bunkum Your
Hollander —pretending not to seek, before his o
material interest, the Malaysian s moral welfare
yet be-gospels his brown brother hardly less
assiduously than the Briton, and administers him
a great deal better ! Nor is the Dutch offici
like, the too frequent British —an official and n
more ; he is generally a man as well. Which was
why—when it was represented to the: Resident oi
Manado that he couldn't appoint an Englander o
a Government plantation —that rubicund old
potentate had answered—not without luyjelai
—that it appeared to him he could, for he had
just done it I
So Hosking went- forth unto the uttermost
parts of Celebes—into Minahassa the volcanic,
and found a paradise of industry, order, and uni-
versal Malay speech, where, 40 years had
been a hell of head-hunters and cannibals—a Babel
of conflicting dialects. All this Dutch rule had
accomplished ; so Hosking, with badly-shattereu
prejudices, settled down, and dwelt in peace with
all men, until —he ran out of pocket-handkerchiefs .
. * * *
To Hosking’s crib—a bamboo bird-cage on the
mountain slope, hedged in with ever-blooming
roses and fragrant with jessamine and oleander—
came, early one morning, the native overseer of
the plantation. A pure Minahassa, but brought-
up in Madras, Wadi spoke English pretty well
hence his present ambassadorial capacity. Hosking
read the letter, swore the swear of astonishment,
and came out in his pyjamas.
“Look here, Wadi! —do you know what this
letter’s about ? ” , _ . „ ,
Wadi showed his white teeth. “Yes!’ he
said—“ he knew.”
“ Well, who is this Mynheer De Veyva, any-
how? I never heard of the fellow before.”
“ He the Districk-Controllah, sah ! He come
yesserday on monthlee inspeck is hon. He see you
vipe vou nose wid Dutch flag, and he verree
angree, sah ! ”
“Dutch flag? Why, what the devil —stay,
though ! ” Hosking went inside, and came out,
laughing, with a handkerchief in his hand—
broadly divided into red, white, and blue. “I
never thought of it before, but this certainly, is
the Dutch tricolor, if you hold it this way ”
bringing the red uppermost —“but, hold it this
way, and it’s tlie French one? Aren’t there any
fiery Gauls on hand to resent their share of the
indignity?” ....
Wadi didn’t laugh. He liked this big, frank,
easy-going Englander, and he knew that the Heer
District-Controller De Veyva was a person to be
taken very seriously.
“ Bettah you ’pologise, Misser Hoskin ! Dat no
hurt you. Controllah, he shoot man one time
’fore—because no ’pologise ! ”
“ Devil he did ! Never mind, old chap—l can
take care of myself. But I’ll go and see this
swashbuckler, at all events ! ”
The physical persistence of the warmer-blooded
races was well exampled in De Yeyva. Descended
from a soldado of the Spanish occupation—half-a-
score generations of Low-Country blood had failed
to make him other than a Spaniard lean,
swarthy, saturnine, honorable, and self-conse-
qi" Look a deal more like a Dutchman myself ! ”
thought Hosking, as he answered, in tolerable
French, the threat man’s formal salutation. But
no further went this amicable tone of matters.
In vain did Hosking demonstrate that the insulted
handkerchief was as much the French tricolor as
the Dutch. In vain—as proof that the rnouchoir
was but one of a batch sent up from Goeontalo—
did he produce the black-white-red of Germany,
the Spanish red-upon-yellow, and the white cross
of the Danes. It was a singular thing, sneered
the Controller in his most mordant French, that,
amongst the national emblems which monsieur had
seen fit to put to such degrading purposes, there
appeared neither the Union-Jack nor the Stars-
and-Stripes ! No ! —no ! —an apology humble,
ample, and immediate must be tendered to His
Netherlandic Majesty’s Government, in the poor
person of him, the Controller ; or
“ Or what ? ” and the smile left Hosking s face
as he stiffened-up his back. He, too, had an
ancestry—with Viking in it!
“ Ou V altcrnatif ordinaire, monsieur 1
“ Oh ! hang such unmitigated rubbish !
Hosking, in Fnglish, and half to himself. I d
apologise fast enough, just to avoid this torn-
foolery, only X suppose this fellow d think he d
frightened me ! ”
The Controller understood but a word here and
.there, but one of these was “fellow.” Accepting
this at its most opprobrious value, he returned it
with “ Lia-r-r ! ” and was promptly knocked into
the corner with a left-hander that put matters
—even from the British point of view—on a strictly
war-footing.
* * * *
“ The whole thing’s too d d ridiculous ! ”
muttered Hosking, as he sprang out of his ham-
mock next morning. “ A duel in the last quarter
of the nineteenth century!—and all about a
wretched colored rag ! I won’t let any man call
Tom Hosking a liar, though ! and I’d have floored
his ‘ Royal Master,’ as he calls him, for the same
thing ! Feel devilish queer, too, this morning !
Surely can’t be in a funk without knowing it ! ’
“ Devilish queer,” in this case, meant a dull and
sickly languor —a weight at once on soul and body
—like nothing Hosking had ever felt before.
“ Were his eyes disordered, too ? ” he asked him-
self, “or why this smoky, sunset look of things at
early morning ? ” He went out. The same un-
canny glare was everywhere ; behind the weirdly-
contorted wisps of coppery cloud ; upon the pale-
green sea of coffee-bush around ; upon the black
volcanic peak above ; upon the sweltering plains
below. A huge, ungainly hornbill flapped,
screeching, to and fro. On the teak behind the
house a flock of yellow bee-birds roosted, in
frightened silence ; the bullocks at the pulping-
house stood huddled, heads together, in. a tremb-
ling group. All nature spoke, but Hosking didn’t
understand. “ A storm,” he said, and started up
the mountain-track.
* * * *
The Dutchman, first upon the ground, stood
gazing at a little rounded cloud, scarce visible
against the darkening sky. Not much, to look at,
but for a slender filament that joined it with the
summit of Tonkoko, and De Veyva—he was a
Oatholio—crossed himself and wished devoutly he
had been about some other business that day.
But then, striding through the fan-ferns, came the
Englishman.
“We have no need of seconds, sir. You have
struck me, and we cannot both leave this spot
alive. And, since swords are not procurable, you
cannot have the challenged party’s choice of
weapons. You have, I presume, no objection to
these revolvers—they are exactly alike ? ” Thus,
with elaborate politeness, the Dutchman.
“ Whatever you like !” said Hosking. “Only,
in Heaven’s name, let us get the foolish business
over ! ”
“ Heaven, sir,” returned the Dutchman gravely,
“ has but little to do with this affair ! ”
Hosking stepped eagerly up to him. “Well
then, Mynheer, if you think so, let it go no
further. I’ll apologise for the blow ! ”
De Veyva took off his hat and bowed. “ And,”
said he, “ for the insult to the flag of His Nether-
landic Majesty?”
“ Damn His Netherlandic Majesty! ” burst out
Hosking, goaded at last into full-blown Bullish-
ness. “ And. his flag, too 1 I wish I’d never seen
the beggarly rag !- or the punctilious idiot who
must have murder about a trifle ! Take your
place and blaze away ! —you’ll get no further
apology from me ! ”
Bristling now with rage, the Dutchman strutted
off. “ Restez-vous la!” he cried. “Abreast of
that arenga I will turn, count three, and fire ! ”
The tree he spoke of —the sugar-palm of Celebes
—stood solitary within five feet or so of a steeply-
sloping bank that ended, a hundred feet below, in
one of those black, unfathomable fissures wherein
the pagan Minahassas still believed their gods to
dwell. But now, as Hosking looked, the palm
bowed gently away from the bank—then swept
suddenly Over in the opposite direction, and
Hosking, with a sickening jerk from below, was
flung down upon his side. His eye fell upon Ton-
koko as he lay. The mountain’s head was
shrouded in a cloud of sable smoke, shot here and
there with snowy jets of steam and darting
tongues of ghastly, greenish flame. Then from
underneath came an awful, smothered, rushing
sound, like nothing but itself. Again the eartli-
wavepassed, the palm-tree nodded, and De Veyva,
who had fallen, with the first shock, close to the
precipice-edge, was jerked clean over it by the
second, and hung there, shrieking piteously—with
nothing of him visible but his desperately cling-
ing fingers, while faintly to the ear came up the
report of his pistol—exploding as it bounded
into the rocky abyss below. In an instant
Hosking was up !—down ! —up again !—and, rush-
ing to the arenga, curved one arm round the
slender stem, so that, stretching with the other,
he could barely grasp De Veyva’s wrist. Drag-
ging, then, for every ounce he was worth, and
helped a little by the other’s frantic digging of his
toes into the not quite perpendicular bank, the
Briton slowly hauled the Dutchman over the
edge, and crawled away with him from a spot
whence another shock might at any moment have
hove them both to perdition.
A dozen times or so, with lessening strength,
the imprisoned spirit of Tonkoko, the volcano,
strove to burst his bonds ; then, sick and sweat-
ing, the Dutchman sat up, and wept, and praj'ed,
and blessed his saints, and embraced his preserver.
Unto all which the Briton — being a Briton.!- —
said :
“ D d close shave ! Got a matoh? ’
